### Goliaths_curse_-_Luke_kemp

"Goliath's Curse: The History and Future of Societal Collapse" by Luke Kemp is a comprehensive study of human history through the lens of societal collapse. The book argues that understanding historical collapses can provide valuable insights into our current predicaments and future challenges.

Kemp defines power as having four forms: control over decision-making, resources, threats/violence, and information. He posits that these power structures are interconnected and depend on the size, skills, and health of the population under their control. 

The author introduces 'societal collapse' as a significant event in human history, characterized by the simultaneous breakdown of government, economy, and population. This concept is crucial because such collapses have greatly influenced our past and will continue to shape our future.

To illustrate this point, Kemp uses the example of Cahokia, an ancient city in present-day Illinois. At its peak around 1050-1100 CE, it housed about 10,000-15,000 people, making it one of the largest cities north of Mexico at that time. The growth was fueled by agriculture, particularly corn cultivation, which led to urbanization and architectural marvels like Monk's Mound – taller than the White House.

However, this prosperity was accompanied by darker aspects. Archaeological evidence suggests ritual sacrifices within some of the pyramids, hinting at internal strife or environmental stress that led to the city's eventual decline around 1250 CE. This serves as a historical precedent of how rapid growth and resource management issues can lead to collapse.

Throughout the book, Kemp explores various case studies of societal collapses from different eras and cultures worldwide. He examines factors like overexploitation of resources, environmental degradation, inequality, and poor governance as common precursors. By understanding these patterns, he aims to shed light on potential risks for our modern societies.

In essence, "Goliath's Curse" is not just about studying the past; it's an attempt to learn from history to navigate future challenges. It encourages readers to view societal collapse not merely as a tragedy but potentially as a warning signal that can guide us towards more sustainable and equitable paths for human development.


The text discusses the historical phenomenon of state collapse, using two contrasting examples: Cahokia, an ancient Native American city in present-day Illinois, and Rome, the capital of the Roman Empire. Both cities experienced significant decline, but their aftermaths differed dramatically.

Cahokia, a pre-Columbian society that flourished around 1050 CE, was characterized by its monumental architecture, including pyramid-like mounds and intricate burial sites. Archaeological evidence reveals evidence of human sacrifice, with fifty-three female skeletons found in one grave, likely victims of a ritualistic, elite funeral. Other discoveries include a pit containing four men with their heads and hands severed, suggesting violent deaths. The city's ruling class, presumably a priestly order, used such practices to legitimize their power and centralize the society.

Despite its grandeur, Cahokia began to crumble around 1250 CE. A farming complex that supported the city was abandoned, defensive structures were erected, and the population drastically decreased within a few decades. Within a century, the entire city and surrounding area were deserted, becoming known as "the Vacant Quarter." Unlike Rome, Cahokia left no oral traditions or stories in the collective memory of local indigenous people, as if intentionally forgotten.

Rome, on the other hand, experienced a different trajectory of collapse. After reaching its peak with a population of about 1 million around 100 CE, the Western Roman Empire began to decline due to various factors such as economic instability, political strife, and military pressures from invasions. By the 5th century CE, the city's population had dwindled to approximately 30,000. However, unlike Cahokia, Rome was never entirely abandoned. It continued as a significant urban center, eventually evolving into modern-day Rome, which now houses over 2.8 million inhabitants.

The text highlights that while all states eventually end, most do not experience societal collapse – the rapid and lasting loss of hierarchy, energy capture, dense populations, and urbanization characteristic of civilization. Cahokia's collapse was particularly severe, resulting in a complete abandonment with no trace left in local oral traditions. In contrast, Rome's decline did not lead to the total annihilation of its cultural legacy or urban structure.

The author argues that the term "civilization" is problematic when applied to societies like Cahokia and Rome due to their reliance on domination and violence rather than civility, altruism, or democracy. Instead, the concept of a "Goliath" – a dominance hierarchy characterized by top-down control, energy exploitation, and social ranking – better describes these ancient societies. This framework emphasizes the power dynamics inherent in such structures, acknowledging their fragility despite their imposing size and apparent grandeur.

In conclusion, understanding historical collapses like those of Cahokia and Rome provides valuable insights into the broader patterns of human societal development. It raises questions about the factors contributing to state stability or downfall, the nature of power structures, and the legacy left by these ancient Goliaths in shaping our modern world.


The text discusses the concept of "Goliaths" as a metaphor for dominance hierarchies that control people and energy within societies, which can be both general templates or specific sets of hierarchies often coinciding with empires. It compares this to the term 'civilization' and suggests that Goliaths are prone to self-destruction or collapse due to inherent flaws.

Historical examples of such collapses include the Late Bronze Age civilizations around 1200 BCE, which encompassed Egypt, Mycenaean Greece, Minoan Crete, and Mesopotamian Assyria/Babylon. This system unraveled over about a century, characterized by rapid decline despite likely being unforeseen at the time. 

The Roman Empire and Han Dynasty are cited as examples of later Goliaths, each controlling vast territories and populations. Their collapses were swift yet seemingly unexpected: the Han dynasty fell within five years due to a small uprising that escalated rapidly; Rome transitioned from single emperor rule to multiple kingdoms in under a century, likely due to internal strife and external pressures.

The author notes that collapses can vary greatly – some are sudden, others protracted over centuries – and their impacts differ based on location and social status. A common perception is that collapse leads to immediate, catastrophic change, but this isn't always the case. For instance, a woman living in Roman Britain might experience little immediate change despite the broader societal shift away from Roman rule.

Collapse isn't necessarily an absolute end but rather a transformation. While physical structures may decay and disappear (as symbolized by Shelley's 'Ozymandias'), elements of culture, language, and tradition often persist. The fall of Rome didn't erase Latin or the influence of Roman institutions; instead, they evolved and continued in different forms, such as through the Eastern Roman Empire and the inspiration for modern institutions like the US Senate. Similarly, Incan language Quechua survives today, and elements of Mississippian culture persisted post-Cahokia.

In essence, collapses reveal both the fragility of grand political structures and the resilience of cultures and communities. They're not uniform events but complex processes that unfold differently across space and time, their full extent only apparent in hindsight. Understanding these patterns can offer insights into how power structures might unravel, though the author cautions against simplistic interpretations or apocalyptic fears drawn from historical examples.


The text discusses the current global predicament, often referred to as a 'meta-crisis' or 'polycrisis', encompassing threats such as nuclear war, climate change, pandemics, and potentially, uncontrolled artificial intelligence (AI). These risks are presented as more rapid and dangerous than historical mass extinction events like the Great Permian Dying. The authors emphasize that while predictions of humanity's downfall are not new, our current situation requires a reliable way to assess these threats in perspective.

The essay suggests that understanding the history of collapse is crucial for navigating our future. Collapse isn't just about the demise of great powers; it also involves the destructive capabilities of such entities. It's highlighted that while competing nations and corporations are current problems, they have historical roots in human psychology and environmental changes.

The authors propose that to truly address these global risks, we must dig deeper into their origins. This excavation will trace back to the emergence of our species 300,000 years ago. The text hints at a paradoxical notion: collapse can sometimes be a blessing in disguise.

The example of Somalia is provided as evidence. After its government collapsed in 1991 following a civil war and the overthrow of dictator Mohamed Siad Barre, many welfare indicators improved. Deaths during childbirth decreased by 30%, infant mortality dropped by 24%, and extreme poverty fell by nearly 20%. Even when compared to neighboring countries with stable governments, Somalis' quality of life improved in most development indicators.

However, the authors clarify that these improvements weren't solely due to state collapse; they also resulted from local militias and governments proving more responsive and less exploitative than Barre's regime. The decline was mainly observed in education metrics because Western donors withdrew aid post-collapse.

The text concludes by suggesting that historical examples of collapse can offer valuable insights into our current predicament, highlighting the need to understand not just the symptoms but also the root causes of global risks. It implies that addressing these underlying factors could help us safely navigate the uncertain future ahead.


The text discusses the complexities and misconceptions surrounding historical collapses, particularly empires, and challenges the romanticized notions often portrayed in popular culture and literature. It argues that many past civilizations, including the Roman Empire, Incan Empire, Egyptian Kingdoms, and others, were predatory constructs characterized by autocratic rule, slavery, displacement, and exploitation of resources for imperial expansion.

The author contends that these empires were often depicted in a favorable light due to the biases of their conquerors and elites who controlled historical narratives. This bias has led to a common misconception that collapse always results in widespread chaos, violence, and societal regression. 

The text uses the fall of the Old Egyptian Kingdom around 2181-2055 BCE as an example to challenge this view. According to Ankhtifi's autobiography and the Admonitions of Ipuwer, a collection of poems written later, the collapse was marked by a loss of central authority, increased conflict, and social upheaval. However, archaeological evidence suggests that these descriptions might be exaggerated or metaphorical.

Instead of mass starvation and cannibalism, the fall of this kingdom appears to have led to a "democratization of religion," where previously exclusive rituals and symbols became more accessible to commoners. Additionally, there was evidence of increased social mobility; non-elite tombs became richer, and servants gained more freedom to speak openly. Nobles, on the other hand, experienced a decline in status and wealth.

This narrative contrasts sharply with common perceptions of collapse, which typically focus on immediate societal breakdown, violence, and a return to a "dark age." The author suggests that such views are rooted in deeply ingrained beliefs about human nature's need for hierarchy.

The text also highlights how these biased historical narratives persist due to the disproportionate representation of elites in our understanding of history, leading to what is termed as a "1% view of history." This perspective focuses on the concerns and losses of the ruling class during periods of collapse while overlooking potential benefits for broader societal groups.

In essence, this passage argues for a more nuanced understanding of historical collapses, acknowledging that while they often involve hardship and change, they can also lead to significant social redistributions of power and resources, sometimes benefiting the lower classes.


The text discusses several key points about historical collapse and its relevance to modern times:

1. **Elite Bias in Archaeological Evidence**: The majority of our understanding of past civilizations comes from monumental structures, grand burials, and written records created by the wealthy elite. This is due to the fact that these are the most durable artifacts left behind, while the majority of people (over 99% in prehistory) lived as foragers or peasant farmers, leaving fewer traces in the archaeological record.

2. **Problems with Studying Historical Collapse**: This elite bias poses challenges when studying historical collapses. For instance, when elites fall and people migrate from cities to rural areas, it's hard to distinguish between mass deaths and migrations. This makes it difficult to accurately gauge the extent of population loss or lifestyle changes during collapse events.

3. **Limited Writing Access in History**: Historically, writing was primarily an elite activity. Only the wealthy could afford writing materials, and literacy was often restricted. Thus, our historical records are skewed towards the perspectives of the ruling classes, providing a limited view of societal changes and collapses.

4. **Relevance of Historical Collapse to Modern Times**: Despite differences between past and present, the text argues that studying historical collapses remains highly relevant. This is because several factors, such as energy capture (which has increased exponentially with industrialization), population density (urban populations have surged), and societal hierarchy (states dominate global politics), are intensified versions of past conditions.

5. **Energy Consumption**: Modern society consumes vastly more energy than historical ones, both in total volume and per capita. This is due to the use of fossil fuels. 

6. **Food Production**: Despite technological advancements, our dietary staples remain primarily grains – wheat, rice, and maize – mirroring past agrarian societies.

7. **Urbanization**: The proportion of the global population living in urban areas has increased dramatically over time, reaching a tipping point in recent decades and projected to rise further by mid-century. 

8. **Societal Hierarchy and State Structure**: Modern society retains hierarchical structures similar to past civilizations, with nearly every part of habitable land under the control of political states. These states possess immense power, capable of influencing disease spread (as historical states did) and now also wielding global-scale impacts such as nuclear warfare or climate change.

9. **Human Psychology**: The text suggests that the fundamental human psychological drivers of conflict and societal collapse have not fundamentally changed over time, despite technological advancements bringing new threats (like nuclear weapons or climate disruption).

10. **Mortality of States (MOROS) Dataset**: The text introduces a hypothetical dataset called MOROS, which would include information on over 300 states across the past 5,000 years, aiming to provide a more comprehensive perspective on societal collapse and mortality beyond elite-centric narratives.


The text discusses the influence of Thomas Hobbes' philosophical ideas on our understanding of human nature and societal collapse. Hobbes, born in 1588 during a period of political turmoil in England, developed a pessimistic view of human nature due to his experiences with chaos and fear. His seminal work, "Leviathan," introduced the concept of the 'social contract,' where citizens surrender some freedoms to an all-powerful sovereign for security and protection from each other. Hobbes viewed the 'state of nature' as a chaotic, violent existence characterized by constant conflict (war of all against all) without any government or law.

Hobbes's perspective on human nature is rooted in what's known as veneer theory: the idea that civilization—our arts, laws, and social order—is a thin layer masking our inherently selfish and brutal nature. This belief has significantly shaped popular perceptions of societal collapse, often depicted as a return to this primordial chaos.

This Hobbesian narrative is not unique to him but rather echoes ancient stories found in diverse cultures and religions, including the Mahabharata and Buddhist scriptures. These tales also tell of people in a state of nature, marked by violence and discord, who willingly submit to a ruler for order and security.

Hobbes's ideas reflect broader historical and religious beliefs about human nature. The Puritan Christian worldview, for instance, held that humans were naturally sinful due to the fall of Adam and Eve, necessitating the formation of governments to maintain order and peace. Hobbes simply provided a philosophical articulation to these long-standing notions.

The text further suggests that limiting our understanding of societal collapse to isolated examples or focusing solely on Hobbes's perspective might not fully illuminate the complexities of modern, interconnected global societies. Instead, it advocates for a broader historical and cross-cultural examination of collapse, as outlined in Part Two and Three of the text, which cover various empires' rises and falls over millennia and consider potential future scenarios of global societal collapse.

In summary, Hobbes's ideas about human nature and the necessity of strong centralized authority have significantly influenced how we perceive societal collapse. His pessimistic perspective, rooted in veneer theory, posits that without governing bodies, humans would naturally descend into chaos and violence. However, this view is not novel to Hobbes and reflects broader historical, religious, and cultural narratives about human nature. The text encourages a more comprehensive, historical approach to understanding societal collapse beyond Hobbes's narrow focus.


The passage discusses the traditional view of human nature as innately violent and prone to mass panic during crises, often referred to as the "Hobbesian nightmare." This perspective, proposed by philosopher Thomas Hobbes, suggests that in the absence of strong governance, humans would revert to a state of nature characterized by conflict, selfishness, and chaos.

However, the author challenges this view, arguing that it's more informative to look at how our ancestors actually lived during the Palaeolithic era (also known as the Ice Age) rather than relying on philosophical speculations or assumptions about human nature. The Palaeolithic period, lasting from approximately 300,000 to 12,000 years ago, was a challenging time marked by extreme cold and lack of advanced agricultural practices. Despite these harsh conditions, humans survived and thrived in large part due to their social structures.

The author presents evidence suggesting that our Palaeolithic ancestors lived in small, nomadic, egalitarian bands averaging 20-40 individuals but potentially reaching up to 150-200 people. These groups were not limited to family members; they included many unrelated friends who contributed various types of knowledge crucial for survival and cultural progression. This suggests that our ancestors' social structures were far from the Hobbesian model of selfish, chaotic individuals.

Moreover, these Palaeolithic societies were not isolated but part of broader networks. While Dunbar's number proposes a limit of around 150 for human social connections based on primate brain sizes, recent studies suggest a much wider range, from 2 to over 500 people. This indicates that our capacity for social connection might be more extensive than previously thought, challenging the Hobbesian notion of innate human antisocial tendencies.

In summary, the author contends that viewing humans through a Hobbesian lens is misguided when considering our evolutionary history. Instead, evidence from the Palaeolithic period reveals societies characterized by cooperation, extensive social networks, and egalitarian structures, offering a more optimistic view of human nature under challenging circumstances. This perspective can provide insights into how humans might behave during crises or disasters, potentially refuting the stereotype of mass panic and selfishness.


The text discusses the misconception that our Palaeolithic ancestors were antisocial loners living in small, isolated groups. Instead, it presents evidence suggesting they were highly social individuals with extensive and fluid social networks. These networks spanned vast regions, facilitating trade and cultural exchange over thousands of years.

1. **Social Networks**: Hunter-gatherer societies today, such as those found in Africa, have flexible social structures where individuals frequently move between groups, amassing numerous connections—often numbering in the thousands. This mobility is not a recent phenomenon; our ancestors also had such fluid networks.

2. **Ancient Trade Networks**: Palaeolithic foragers engaged in long-distance trade, exchanging materials like obsidian and ostrich eggs over significant distances. For instance, obsidian was traded 160 km away around 200,000 years ago, while ostrich eggs were exchanged across vast regions of Africa at least 50,000 years ago. These networks were built on reciprocity and gift-giving.

3. **Cultural Homogeneity**: These trade routes fostered shared cultures and technological advancements. For example, the 'Aurignacian' culture spanned large parts of Africa around 120,000 years ago, while the 'Clovis' tradition covered much of North America 13,050-12,750 years ago.

4. **Health and Well-being**: Contrary to common belief, Palaeolithic foragers were not unhealthy or short-lived. Studies show they were taller and healthier than early farmers and have lower rates of modern diseases like obesity, diabetes, cancer, and cardiovascular issues. The Tsimané people in the Bolivian Amazon, for example, have exceptional health markers despite being a marginalized group.

5. **Work Hours**: Foragers typically work around 40 hours per week (with a range of 20-50), which includes activities like hunting, fishing, gardening, and walking through nature. This is less than the average modern worker, who might spend 55-77 hours weekly on paid labor.

6. **Mobility as Connective Tissue**: The text emphasizes that movement was integral to Palaeolithic societies—not just for finding food but also for social connection across continents. This mobility facilitated cooperation and the spread of shared culture and technology.

In summary, the passage argues against the traditional narrative of our ancestors as solitary, violent individuals. Instead, it presents a picture of highly social, mobile, and connected Palaeolithic humans who engaged in extensive trade, cultural exchange, and had robust health outcomes. The fluid nature of their societies, built on mobility and reciprocity, facilitated the spread of technology and culture across vast regions, challenging the notion of a "nasty, brutish" existence.


The text discusses the prevalence of violence among stateless forager societies, both in modern times and in the Paleolithic era. It challenges the common perception that these societies are inherently violent by presenting evidence from various sources: contemporary observations, archaeological findings, and evolutionary studies.

1. Contemporary Observations: The text mentions a wide range of homicide rates among modern forager groups. For instance, the Batek people of Malaysia have a rate of 1 per 100,000 person-years, while the Hiwi in Venezuela and Colombia have a rate of 1,018 per 100,000. These rates, however, can be misleading as they often include violence inflicted by external factors like colonizers or slave traders rather than intrinsic societal violence.

2. Archaeological Evidence: The anthropologists Jonathan Haas and Matthew Piscitelli conducted a study of almost 3,000 skeletons from over 400 pre-10,000 BCE sites. They found that interpersonal violence was rare in the Palaeolithic period, with only six sites showing any indications of violence. Of these, Jebel Sahaba in Sudan is the most significant, where 24 out of 58 skeletons showed signs of violence, but it seems to be more suggestive of occasional raids rather than full-blown warfare. Systematic studies suggest a 1.3% violent death rate in the Palaeolithic, comparable to today's global homicide and war death rates (2.2%).

3. Phylogenetic Evidence: A study surveyed 1,024 mammalian species, including our closest relatives like chimpanzees, to predict human violence levels. The results suggested that prehistoric hunter-gatherers had an interpersonal kill rate of about 2%, similar to other great apes. This indicates that humans were not significantly more violent in the Paleolithic era compared to their primate relatives or modern standards.

The text argues against the notion of humanity's inherent, war-like nature by presenting evidence from multiple disciplines. It suggests that while instances of violence exist and can be dramatic (as seen in certain modern forager groups and historical cases), the overall picture indicates that human societies have been, and likely remain, more peaceful than often assumed. The depictions of human-on-human killing in cave art are few and ambiguous, potentially representing animals rather than humans. Similarly, archaeological evidence for widespread warfare is sparse, and phylogenetic comparisons with other primates suggest human violence levels are not exceptionally high.

The text also critiques famous works like Steven Pinker's "The Better Angels of Our Nature," which propose high Palaeolithic violence rates (15-25%). It argues that such estimates lack reliable archaeological support and should be replaced with the findings from systematic, scientific studies.

In essence, the text presents a nuanced view of human history, challenging simplistic narratives about inherent violence and suggesting that our understanding of human behavior, including its violent aspects, needs to be more contextually grounded and empirically informed.


This passage argues against the Hobbesian view that human nature is inherently violent and competitive, instead proposing that our ancestors were more peaceful than commonly believed. 

The author begins by discussing genetic adaptations in humans that suggest we've evolved to withstand pain, heal faster, and remain calm under stress - traits that would be advantageous in high-violence environments. However, these changes appeared after the split from chimpanzees, our closest living relatives, implying that our ancestors were not as violent as chimps are today. 

The author further contends that instances of "chimpanzee warfare" often result from human interference rather than inherent primate aggression. They also point out that bonobos, another close relative, exhibit virtually no inter-group violence, suggesting a wide range of primate behavioral possibilities.

The author then introduces the concept of "squeamishness" - the reluctance to hurt others - as evidence of human pacifism. Historical and psychological studies suggest that soldiers in past conflicts often intentionally misaimed or refrained from using their weapons, indicating a strong inclination against killing. The development of immersive training programs by the U.S. military during the Vietnam War to overcome this squeamishness is cited as evidence that humans are not naturally predisposed towards violence.

The author challenges the idea that our prehistoric past was marked by constant, high levels of interpersonal violence. Instead, they propose that such violence was rare and personal, driven more by individual disputes than large-scale conflicts over resources or territory. Studies of modern nomadic hunter-gatherers support this view, showing that a significant proportion of fatalities were due to interpersonal violence rather than inter-group warfare.

The passage also debunks the myth of "mass panic" during disasters. Rather than descending into chaos, people tend to self-organize and cooperate in crisis situations, providing mutual aid and support. This behavior is seen across various modern crises and has evolutionary logic behind it: In the Palaeolithic era, relying on social networks during hardship was key to survival.

In conclusion, the passage argues that humans are not inherently violent but have evolved traits suggesting adaptation to a relatively peaceful environment. Our historical and psychological responses to conflict also suggest a strong inclination towards cooperation rather than violence. The author posits that while small-scale conflicts may have occurred, there's no evidence for large-scale warfare in the Palaeolithic era, challenging traditional views of human nature rooted in Hobbesian thought.


The passage discusses the historical context of egalitarianism in human societies, tracing it back to our Palaeolithic ancestors. 

1. **Ancient Guest Rites**: The concept of treating guests with kindness was prevalent across various ancient cultures. This included Greek hospitium, Roman hospitality, Norse guest rites, and Indian Upanishads' "Atithi Devo Bhava" (the guest is akin to god). These traditions were reinforced by divine narratives where hosts who showed kindness were rewarded, while those who neglected their duties faced punishment. 

2. **Mobility and Sharing**: The author suggests that these practices of hospitality might have encouraged mobility and sharing among early human societies, which could be seen as a strategy for managing disasters such as famines or natural calamities. This is contrasted with the idea of mass panic or violence often portrayed in modern disaster narratives.

3. **Egalitarian Origins**: For most of human history, before about 11,000 years ago (post-ice age), societies were egalitarian. This wasn't due to passive conditions but active choices, enforced by customs and traditions promoting sharing. Examples include arrow swapping among hunters, where the original owner decides how meat is distributed after a successful hunt. 

4. **Sharing as Insurance and Bonding**: Sharing was not just an insurance mechanism against individual failures in hunting or gathering but also a bonding method. The author notes that humans generally prefer shared activities, whether related to resources (food), social experiences (jokes), personal spaces (beds), or even life itself (lives).

5. **Political Equality**: Political power was also shared equally among members of these egalitarian societies. Decisions were often made collectively, without permanent leaders or hierarchies. Even when temporary leaders emerged due to skills like shamanism or warrior prowess, they were expected to be modest, generous, and non-authoritarian.

6. **Democracy as a Deep Human Trait**: The author argues that democracy is not a Western invention but a fundamental human trait. It isn't limited to representative government models using elected officials, as seen in modern parliamentary systems. Instead, it includes direct democracy (citizens collectively making decisions) and sortition (random selection of representatives). Hunter-gatherer societies are cited as examples of highly democratic political structures, where major decisions were made by groups of equals, unrestricted by gender or age.

In conclusion, the passage posits that human history, particularly in the Palaeolithic era, was characterized by egalitarianism - equal distribution of power and resources, collective decision-making, and a strong emphasis on sharing and hospitality. These traits are presented as deeply ingrained in our species' evolutionary makeup, challenging the common assumption that disasters lead to widespread violence or selfishness.


The text discusses the evolutionary origins of human egalitarianism and democracy, suggesting that our ancestors were characterized by a lack of lasting hierarchies or divisions of power. Instead, they employed "counter-dominance" strategies to prevent any individual from gaining too much influence. These strategies included teasing, gossiping, shaming, ridicule, expulsion, and in severe cases, death.

The evolutionary basis for this egalitarianism is attributed to several factors:

1. **Physical Changes**: Around 2 million years ago, anatomical changes in human shoulders enabled accurate throwing of projectiles (spears or rocks). This development leveled the playing field, allowing a single individual or small group to effectively counter a dominant figure, even if physically larger and stronger.

2. **Brain Size Increase**: A significant growth in brain size occurred around this time, particularly with Homo erectus, which is believed to have evolved primarily for navigating complex social environments. This enhanced cognitive ability facilitated coalition-building against potential dominators.

These factors combined led to the "first great levelling in human history," a more egalitarian society compared to other primates, where physical strength alone was not sufficient to establish dominance due to the threat of projectile weapons and collective social strategies.

The text also highlights how this egalitarianism manifested in several ways:

- **Violence as a Deterrent**: Harsh sanctions, including lethal violence, were meted out to those attempting dominance, as seen in the Ju/'hoansi example of killing a brutal hunter named /Twa.

- **Cooperative Parenting**: Early humans had to rely on collective parenting due to the vulnerable nature of human offspring. Communities that could provide better care and education for their young likely thrived, leading to an evolutionary advantage for cooperative behavior.

- **Physical Traits**: Humans lack the large canine teeth and significant size difference between sexes seen in more hierarchical primates like gorillas. Our "cooperative eyes" (visible sclera) and relatively small canines suggest a less hierarchical, more egalitarian social structure.

- **Gender Equality**: Hunter-gatherer bands were often mixed and not based on kinship, indicating a balance of power between sexes. This is proposed to be due to men and women having equal decision-making power in band movements, leading to diverse, fluid group structures rather than strictly patriarchal lineages.

Despite this egalitarian past, the text acknowledges that status competition still existed among early humans. This desire for respect, admiration, and influence likely drove some individuals to try and gain dominance, necessitating the counter-dominance strategies. The historical examples of emperors and kings maintaining large harems illustrate the enduring human propensity for status competition, even in a society otherwise characterized by relative equality.


The passage discusses the historical and evolutionary significance of status competition among humans, focusing on two primary forms: prestige and domination. 

1. **Prestige**: This form of status is earned through skills, wisdom, or expertise. It's a natural recognition by others based on one's abilities or contributions. Hunter-gatherer societies often exhibit task-based leadership, where an excellent hunter might have more influence during a hunt but would face social repercussions if they tried to exert control outside of that context. Prestige can't be inherited and is generally admired across cultures and times, from great athletes to skilled artisans.

2. **Domination**: This form of status is achieved through violence, intimidation, or control of resources. It involves imposing one's will on others, often leading to brutal consequences. Throughout history, dictators and conquerors have gained notoriety for their ability to dominate large populations. This strategy was less common in our ancestral environments due to the innate human aversion to being dominated, which likely evolved as a survival mechanism in small, egalitarian hunter-gatherer groups.

The passage also highlights that while the desire for status is universal, it's not evenly distributed. Men, especially those scoring high on psychological traits like psychopathy, narcissism, and Machiavellianism, are more likely to pursue dominance-based status. This inclination has roots in our primate ancestry, where status competition through dominance was more intense and rewarded with greater reproductive success.

The evolutionary narrative presented suggests that over millennia, counter-dominance strategies and prestige-based leadership became prevalent because they were more successful in the challenging conditions of the Paleolithic era. This balance was maintained not due to an inherent human goodness but because egalitarianism proved to be a winning strategy for survival and reproduction.

The passage concludes by noting that while status competition, particularly through domination, is less prevalent in modern humans, it hasn't been entirely eradicated. This ongoing tension between the desire for status and aversion to dominance reflects our complex evolutionary history.


The text discusses the lifestyle and historical context of modern-day hunter-gatherer societies, specifically those in Central Africa (like the Aka, Efe, and Mbuti) and the Khoisan people of the Kalahari Desert (notably the Ju/'hoansi), often referred to as "Paleolithic analogues" due to their resemblance to our ancient ancestors. These societies are characterized by egalitarianism, cooperation, and a way of life largely unchanged for tens of thousands of years.

1. **Egalitarian Societies**: Unlike the Hobbesian view often depicted in post-apocalyptic narratives, where survivors are self-reliant individuals ready to resort to violence to stay alive, these societies have historically coped with disasters and catastrophes by moving, sharing resources, and cooperating. This egalitarian way of life is considered foundational to human existence, having persisted for over 100,000 years among the Khoisan and similar lineages in Central Africa.

2. **Genetic Diversity**: The Khoisan and some Central African groups represent extremely genetically diverse and ancient lineages, with evidence suggesting they diverged from other human populations around 300,000-200,000 years ago. This diversity underscores their deep evolutionary history.

3. **Shared Characteristics**: Despite variations in language and environment, these groups share several cultural traits, including polyphonic music, body painting, similar creation myths, and an egalitarian social structure. These commonalities are more likely attributed to a shared evolutionary past than coincidence.

4. **Contrast with State-based Societies**: The author contrasts this ancient, largely stateless human history (99% of our timeline) with the more recent advent of states and hierarchies, which began around 5,100 years ago in Egypt. This shift dramatically altered the way societies functioned, moving from cooperation to domination and control.

5. **Human Extinction**: The text also touches on human extinction events. Apart from our own survival (Homo sapiens), at least eight other hominin species once coexisted with us, including Neanderthals. These extinctions remain largely mysterious due to the scarcity of archaeological evidence, but the Neanderthals provide a notable exception, offering some insights into their potential downfall.

6. **Theoretical Speculation on Neanderthal Extinction**: The author suggests that while intelligence played a role in our survival, overestimating human uniqueness and intelligence is common. Neanderthals were likely intelligent too. Factors leading to their extinction might include environmental changes, competition for resources, or interactions with incoming Homo sapiens, possibly including interbreeding.

In summary, this text presents a compelling argument that human history prior to the emergence of states and hierarchies was characterized by egalitarianism, cooperation, and a way of life surprisingly similar to our Paleolithic ancestors. It underscores the deep roots of these societal structures in human evolution and challenges common narratives about human nature and survival strategies.


The text discusses the evolutionary advantages of humans that go beyond individual intelligence. It emphasizes the importance of cooperation and storytelling as key factors contributing to human success.

1. **Storytelling and Imagined Communities**: Humans have a unique ability to create imagined communities, such as nations or companies, through storytelling. This capacity for shared identity encourages cooperation within groups, making them stronger and more likely to survive. Storytelling is evolutionarily advantageous because it helps in passing down knowledge and traditions across generations, fostering group cohesion.

2. **Social Learning**: Humans excel at social learning – imitating and learning from each other – which surpasses that of our closest relatives like chimpanzees. While chimps can use tools and imitate each other, toddlers outperform them in social learning due to the extensive accumulation of knowledge passed down through generations. This 'cumulative cultural evolution' allows humans to adapt rapidly to various environments.

3. **Egalitarianism and Wide Social Networks**: The text highlights how human egalitarian societies facilitate cultural evolution. In contrast to the hierarchical structure of chimpanzee groups, where dominant individuals may stifle learning opportunities for subordinates, humans in egalitarian societies have broader networks of interaction. This enables children to learn from numerous adults across different communities, accelerating the acquisition and transmission of knowledge.

4. **Reevaluation of Cognitive Revolution**: The concept of a rapid 'cognitive revolution' around 70,000 years ago that introduced language, imagination, and cultural learning is now deemed insufficient. Evidence from archaeology and paleontology indicates that earlier hominids (including Neanderthals) demonstrated capabilities like complex tool-making, abstract thought, and symbolic behavior much earlier than previously believed.

5. **Neanderthal Intelligence**: Recent research suggests that Neanderthals were remarkably similar to modern humans in cognitive abilities. They exhibited behaviors such as advanced tool use, burial practices indicating possible belief systems, and even artistic expression (cave paintings). These findings challenge the notion of a significant cognitive gap between Neanderthals and early modern humans.

6. **Neanderthal Extinction**: The text touches on various hypotheses for Neanderthal extinction: competition with early modern humans, environmental changes (like a cold snap), and population vulnerability due to small, fragmented communities. A recent survey of experts indicates that demographic factors – such as low genetic diversity, inbreeding, and susceptibility to population collapse – were likely the principal causes of Neanderthal extinction.

In summary, the text argues that human evolutionary success isn't solely about individual intelligence but also hinges on cooperation, storytelling, social learning, egalitarianism, and cultural evolution. It further challenges the notion of a distinct 'cognitive revolution' by pointing to evidence of complex behaviors in our hominid ancestors, including Neanderthals, and discusses potential reasons for their eventual extinction.


The extinction of Neanderthals, our close evolutionary relatives, was not due to a single cause but a combination of factors. A study of thirteen Neanderthal remains in the Altai Mountains revealed low genetic diversity among these individuals, similar to that found in critically endangered mountain gorillas living in small groups. This lack of diversity and social structure made Neanderthals more susceptible to environmental changes and diseases.

Their small population size and high mortality rates rendered them vulnerable. A 1.5% decrease in the survival rate of Neanderthal children could have led to their extinction within 2,000 years, according to a calculation. Climate change, new diseases, and competition with Homo sapiens likely interacted to cause population declines.

In terms of disaster risk theory, four determinants are considered: threat, vulnerability, exposure, and response. For Neanderthals, climate change and competition with humans were threats, while their fragmented and isolated communities represented vulnerability. The exposure factor (geographical location) and response (adaptive measures) are less understood due to lack of evidence.

Neanderthals' downfall was not a result of inferior intelligence but rather their disconnected social structure compared to early modern humans. Neanderthal populations were fragmented, lacking the genetic diversity and cultural knowledge that could have helped them survive in diverse environments, unlike Homo sapiens who maintained large, interconnected societies.

The extinction was a process of multiple local collapses rather than one mass event. These collapses likely varied in cause – disease, harsh winters, or competition with humans. Evidence of violent human-Neanderthal interactions is scant; two Neanderthal skeletons showed signs of injury but their origins remain uncertain.

The story of early human migration also challenges the 'Great Exit' theory, suggesting that anatomically modern humans left Africa much earlier than previously thought. Fossils found in Greece indicate modern humans were present 210,000 years ago alongside Neanderthals. Many early migrations may have ended in extinction or assimilation due to isolation, lack of genetic diversity, and inability to adapt to new environments.

Climatic events, such as the eruption of Mount Toba 73,000 years ago, likely played a significant role in population fluctuations and potential extinctions during the Paleolithic era. This volcanic winter drastically reduced global temperatures, causing widespread environmental disruption that could have led to severe population declines among early human populations.

In conclusion, Neanderthals' extinction was a complex process involving various interacting factors like climate change, disease, competition with Homo sapiens, and their inherent vulnerability due to small, isolated populations. This narrative underscores the importance of social networks and genetic diversity in survival, lessons that resonate even today in understanding species vulnerability to extinction.


The text discusses the resilience of early human societies during periods of significant climate disruption, focusing on volcanic eruptions that caused global cooling. It highlights that while North America, Europe, and central Asia were severely impacted, regions like northern India and southern Africa remained relatively unaffected. The Toba supervolcano eruption around 74,000 years ago is a prime example, where isolated groups in the Levant likely suffered die-offs, while others in India escaped relatively unscathed.

The text also mentions three other volcanic events in the northern hemisphere—the Aniakchak eruption (~1645 BCE), Mazama eruption (~5630 BCE), and Laacher See eruption (11,000 BCE)—which led to societal dips in various parts of the world. For instance, the Aniakchak eruption separated Aleut and Inuit peoples in Alaska, potentially causing a lasting ethnic and linguistic divide.

The Palaeolithic era is characterized by frequent community dispersions due to environmental shocks, such as volcanic eruptions. These groups were subsistence-level hunter-gatherers with minimal energy surplus, making any significant drops in energy capture catastrophic. The primary response to such stress was a reduction in the density of communities rather than large-scale societal collapse, as there were no complex urban empires yet.

A key aspect of early human social structure was fission-fusion movements—constant breaking and joining of smaller groups into larger ones for various reasons like tool exchange, building relationships, or seeking seasonal resources. This pattern is common in cognitively advanced species but is particularly pronounced in humans. These movements facilitated the sharing of knowledge, tools, and fostering of social bonds that contributed to their survival during harsh ice-age conditions.

The text emphasizes that while abandoning a settlement or town might seem like collapse today, for early humans, dispersing was a normal survival strategy. It allowed them to avoid dangerous situations and seek out better resources. This mobility, along with their ability to rapidly form and dismantle social structures, has been crucial in understanding later societal collapses.

Furthermore, the text discusses seasonal cycles in early human societies. It uses the example of the Lakota and Cheyenne tribes from the Great Plains who altered their social structure with the changing seasons. During summer bison hunts and sun dance rituals, they would temporarily form a more authoritarian society with distinct roles like a quasi-police force for maintaining order and managing resources effectively.

In summary, the text argues that early human societies were highly adaptable, capable of rapid changes in response to environmental pressures. Their resilience was rooted in mobility, fluid social structures, and seasonal adaptations—strategies that not only ensured survival during harsh conditions but also contributed to cultural exchange and innovation. These traits remain integral to human nature today.


The passage discusses the phenomenon of seasonal cycles in human societies, particularly focusing on examples from various prehistoric cultures around the world. These seasonal shifts involved changes in social structures, political organization, and even sexual norms. 

1. **Shoshone (North America):** During certain periods, they adopted 'rabbit bosses' or 'antelope shamans' for coordinating hunting efforts, then returned to more egalitarian family structures when not engaged in these collective activities.

2. **Wik-Mungkan (Australia):** These people lived nomadically during dry seasons and settled into villages during wet seasons when flooding occurred.

3. **Inuit (Arctic):** They organized into smaller family groups for summer hunting but came together in larger, more egalitarian communities during the harsher winter months, practicing partner sharing.

These seasonal cycles often led to significant social and architectural achievements. For instance:

- **Stonehenge (UK):** Built around 3000-2600 BCE by pastoralists for likely ceremonial purposes, possibly involving animal cross-breeding, partner finding, and feasts.
- **Göbekli Tepe (Turkey):** A 9,000-year-old site with twenty megalithic structures, including large T-shaped pillars, potentially serving as a seasonal settlement for hunting or other communal activities near migratory paths of animals.

The passage suggests that these seasonal cycles are relatively recent phenomena in human history, with the earliest evidence found during the Upper Paleolithic period (36,000-30,000 years ago) in Europe. This period is marked by grand burials and architectural feats:

- **Upper Palaeolithic Europe:** Notable examples include the Sungir burial with thousands of perforated fox canine teeth and mammoth-ivory beads, and the Lady of Saint-Germain-la-Rivière, an adult woman buried with ornaments sourced from distant locations. These grand burials were often accompanied by construction of small buildings from mammoth tusks and bones.

These cycles likely coincided with periods of abundant resources, such as floodplains teeming with game during certain seasons. The people would come together for large-scale hunts, feasts, and possibly even temporary increases in social hierarchy (though evidence suggests these 'nobilities' were more likely fleeting).

The passage further discusses the possible reasons behind these seasonal shifts: adaptation to environmental circumstances. For example, the Wik-Mungkan dispersed during dry seasons and gathered during wet ones; similarly, Inuit adapted their communities based on the harshness of winter conditions.

Finally, the text highlights that these seasonal cycles are unlikely to have occurred earlier than the Upper Paleolithic in Europe, as there's no evidence of such behavior in Africa or other regions during earlier periods. This aligns with the unique environmental conditions of ice-age Europe, characterized by distinct seasons and volatile climate, which could trigger these societal shifts.


The text discusses various aspects of foraging societies, focusing on the Calusa and north-west coast Native Americans, and their complex social structures.

1. **Calusa Society**: The Calusa, a Floridian tribe, were not typical hunter-gatherers. They lived in large villages, had hereditary rulers (a supreme chief), elites, and full-time warriors. Despite not being farmers, they controlled around 20,000 people through a tribute system, demanding food, gold, and captives from surrounding groups. Their society was stratified, similar to medieval Europe, with a ruler claiming ownership of lands and conducting secret rituals to ensure fertility. They used war canoes for raids and practiced human sacrifice, often upon the death of their chief.

2. **North-West Coast Native Americans**: These tribes, such as the Kwakwaka'wakw and Tsimshian, also exhibited hierarchical structures. Some were egalitarian, relying on acorns and nuts, while others had chiefs who waged war, took captives, and kept slaves. They built longhouses, hosted large coastal villages during winter, and relied heavily on salmon runs for sustenance. Elites would host grand feasts to display wealth, cement alliances, and intimidate rivals. Feast-giving chiefs claimed exclusive access to ritual knowledge and sometimes divine connections, likened by some ethnographers to 'terrorist organizations' due to their use of violence and 'black magic'.

The text suggests that these foraging societies were not primitive or simple; they had complex political systems, including warfare, captivity, tribute, and displays of wealth. These practices were often tied to environmental conditions, such as the availability of food resources like salmon, and seasonal changes. The author argues that these societies' flexibility—their ability to adapt and change based on circumstances—was a significant factor in human survival and evolution, contrasting with the perspective of 'collapse' often associated with historical empires. Instead, these seasonal shifts were seen as part of their adaptive strategy, enabling them to respond effectively to environmental challenges.


The text discusses the evolution of human societies, focusing on the shift from egalitarian hunter-gatherer groups to more hierarchical and stratified societies. It introduces two key concepts: "Goliaths" and "lootable resources."

1. **Goliaths**: This term refers to hierarchical societies, whether they are hunter-gatherers (like the Natufians) or agrarian states. The name is derived from the biblical story of David and Goliath, symbolizing the emergence of powerful, dominant entities in human history.

2. **Lootable Resources**: These are resources that can be easily monitored, controlled, and stored. They include food items like wild cereals, domesticated animals, and fish stocks, as well as non-edible resources like strategic trade goods. The ease of control and storage of these resources is crucial because it allows for the accumulation of surplus, which can then be used to support a ruling class or administrators, leading to societal hierarchy.

The text provides several examples:

- **Natufians (12,500 - 10,800 BCE)**: These early foragers in modern-day Syria, Palestine, and Israel showed signs of social stratification, with some individuals having more elaborate burials and larger houses. They also engaged in activities associated with the transition to farming, such as collecting wild cereals and domesticating dogs. However, their society became less stratified during a period of global cooling known as the Younger Dryas (10,900-9700 BCE).

- **Calusa (North America)**: These fishers and foragers demanded tribute from part-time farmers, demonstrating early forms of resource control and societal hierarchy.

- **Pacific Coast Native Americans**: Their reliance on salmon runs illustrates how abundant, easily monitored resources can lead to social stratification, as evidenced by the presence of slavery and unequal food access in some of these societies.

The text also discusses that while agriculture is often associated with the rise of hierarchy due to surplus production, hierarchical societies can emerge even without it, as long as there are lootable resources available. The timing and specifics vary across regions, but generally, sedentism (permanent settlement) leads to intensified agriculture or fishing, then to war and finally the creation of states.

In summary, the text argues that the availability of resources that can be easily controlled and stored is a critical factor in the development of hierarchical societies, regardless of whether these societies are based on hunting/gathering or farming. This perspective challenges the common narrative that links hierarchy solely to surplus production enabled by agriculture.


The passage discusses the transition from egalitarian societies during the Ice Age to hierarchical societies during the Holocene epoch, focusing on three key factors: lootable resources, monopolizable wealth, and caged land. 

1. **Lootable Resources**: These are resources that others depend on or desire. The Nabataeans, for instance, controlled a popular route for incense trade, allowing them to accumulate wealth and power. Similarly, groups around scarce yet highly valued resources like obsidian also gained influence. 

2. **Monopolizable Wealth**: This refers to the ability to control an unavoidable trade route or a concentrated resource, leading to economic gain that can be passed down through generations. Before this, it was difficult for parents to bestow status or advantages onto their children. 

3. **Caged Land (Territory with Few Exit Options)**: This concept refers to land where it's hard for people to leave due to lack of alternative resources. For example, communities relying on specific salmon runs or cereal forests were "trapped" and more likely to accept subordination. 

The author argues that these factors, combined over time, led to the rise of inequality and hierarchical societies. Lootable resources provided economic power, which could be converted into other forms of power (information, violent, political). Monopolizable weapons, like bronze swords or horses, gave one group an advantage over competitors, enabling them to conquer and hold resources of others. 

However, the author stresses that these conditions alone do not guarantee oppressive conditions unless exit options are limited. Species with greater dispersal options usually develop weaker hierarchies. Similarly, human societies with easy escape routes were less likely to become hierarchical. 

The unique twist in human history, according to the passage, is that power became hereditary—a trait not seen in other primates. This hereditary transmission of status and wealth further solidified social hierarchies over time. 

In summary, the shift from egalitarian to hierarchical societies was driven by the accumulation of lootable resources leading to monopolizable wealth, which could be passed down through generations. This process was facilitated when communities were "caged" in lands with limited exit options, making them more susceptible to subordination. The author suggests that while cultural and individual choices played a role, long-term environmental conditions significantly influenced the development of these social structures.


The text discusses the emergence and evolution of warfare in human societies, particularly in the context of the transition from egalitarian hunter-gatherer lifestyles to more hierarchical, agrarian societies. This shift, marked by the advent of "Goliath fuel" (likely referring to the Neolithic Revolution and its associated technologies), led to profound changes in social structures and the nature of violence.

1. **Egalitarian Hunter-Gatherers**: For millennia, human societies were largely egalitarian, with no signs of organized warfare. Disputes were typically resolved through personal conflict or community decisions. Violence was individualistic and not systematically directed at other groups.

2. **Rise of Inequality and Agriculture**: The introduction of agriculture and settled life led to increased social stratification and inequality. As societies became larger, more complex, and hierarchical, the conditions for organized warfare emerged. This shift is evident across various regions worldwide, including Mexico, Japan, Europe, and the Near East.

3. **Archaeological Evidence**: Archaeological findings support this pattern. For instance, in the Oaxaca Valley, evidence of warfare appeared around 800-450 BCE, coinciding with the emergence of the first hierarchical society, the Zapotec. Similarly, in Japan during the Jomon Period, fortifications and weapons started appearing alongside agriculture.

4. **Change in Nature of Violence**: The nature of violence also changed. From being primarily individual or group-based, it evolved into large-scale, organized conflicts aimed at acquiring resources, expanding power, and eliminating perceived threats. This shift is reflected in cave art depicting armed groups from 8000 BCE onwards.

5. **Key Ingredients of Warfare**: The text identifies three key ingredients for organized warfare: large-scale organization to coordinate troops, an ideology to inspire fighters, and tight bonds between groups of fighters (often young men). These elements require extensive social organization, training, and indoctrination, which first became possible with the rise of hierarchies and states.

6. **Modern Parallels**: The correlation between inequality and violence is also seen in contemporary hunter-gatherer societies, where group-on-group violence correlates with larger, denser, and more sedentary groups.

In essence, the text argues that warfare did not decrease over human history; instead, the conditions and motivations for large-scale violence have progressively intensified since the Neolithic era. This shift is linked to the rise of hierarchical societies and agriculture, which fostered inequality, organizational complexity, and new forms of violence.


The text discusses the historical narrative of societal development, challenging the traditional concept of "progress" as a linear ladder of increasing complexity from "savagery" to "barbarism" to "civilization." This model, popularized by 19th-century thinkers like Lewis Henry Morgan, is based on technological and societal advancements, such as the shift from hunting/gathering to agriculture, domestication of animals, development of pottery, and establishment of towns and cities.

The text argues that this model is oversimplified and inaccurate. It points out that some hunter-gatherer societies exhibited hallmarks of "civilization," like sedentism, hierarchy, and warfare, without engaging in agriculture. Similarly, not all complex societies followed the agricultural path; some jumped directly from nomadic living to statehood or remained hierarchical without becoming states.

The author suggests that modern archaeology has shifted towards the concept of "complexity," defining it as an increase in hierarchy, inequality, and centralization. However, this definition is criticized for being selective and misleading. For instance, it might classify societies with fewer languages, simpler environmental management practices, and less centralized religious beliefs as "less complex," even if these societies exhibit more diverse and interconnected aspects of life.

The text also introduces the concept of "dominance hierarchies" to describe the shift observed during the Neolithic era - a gradual, reversible growth towards social structures based on ranked status and authority. This includes characteristics like inequality, warfare, and centralized control over labor and resources, often associated with what we traditionally think of as "civilization."

The critique here is that the traditional narrative of societal progress romanticizes this shift, implying it was an overall advancement. Instead, the text suggests viewing it as a complex, messy process involving both gains and losses, where increases in certain forms of 'complexity' (hierarchy, control) come hand-in-hand with potential losses in other areas (diversity, flexibility). It's important to note that this perspective doesn't deny the existence of these hierarchical structures; rather, it challenges the notion that they represent an unambiguous form of societal "progress."


The text discusses the evolution of human societies from egalitarian hunter-gatherer groups to more hierarchical structures, focusing on the concept of "Goliath" societies characterized by dominance and power concentrated in the hands of a few. 

1. **Palaeolithic Societies**: The author describes early human societies during the Paleolithic era as non-hierarchical, democratic cooperatives. They relied on mutual aid, trade, hospitality, and reciprocity to survive. Violence was used sparingly and only as a last resort for collective defense, not as a tool of dominance by a few. This form of cooperation is considered 'civilized conduct' due to its elements of restraint, political wisdom, and voluntary collaboration without coercion.

2. **Transition to Dominance Hierarchies**: The shift from these egalitarian societies to dominance hierarchies is referred to as 'evolutionary backsliding.' This transition occurred gradually over thousands of years, marked by inequality, the rise of temples and fortified villages, and eventually, the formation of states. The author uses Tepe Gawra, an archaeological site in northern Iraq, as a case study to illustrate this process.

   - **Tepe Gawra**: This site shows multiple layers of settlements over 6,500 years. Initially, it was an egalitarian village that gradually became more unequal with the construction of grand temples around 5500 BCE. These temples were later abandoned after the village was raided and burned, possibly due to increased conflict driven by inequality. Despite attempts to rebuild, the site was repeatedly abandoned and burned over centuries, suggesting the instability of dominance hierarchies even in their early stages.

3. **Agricultural Revolution**: The author argues that the transition to agriculture, often viewed as a revolutionary turning point, was actually slow and fraught with reversals. In Mesopotamia, for instance, seasonal settlements emerged around 12,000 BCE but collapsed due to climate change. Full-time sedentary living didn't appear until about 10,000 years later. Pottery became common only 1,500 years after that, and evidence of warfare didn't surface until another 1,500 years passed. The first state, Uruk, wasn't established until around 3200 BCE—a process spanning thousands of years.

4. **Modern Societies as Goliaths**: Today's societies, including 'liberal democracies' like those in Scandinavia, are described as existing on a spectrum between civilization and Goliath. Despite having elements of democracy (like periodic elections), they still exhibit dominance hierarchies in various aspects of life—workplaces, religions, markets—where power is concentrated in the hands of a few. The author suggests that modern societies are essentially 'Global Goliaths,' characterized by pervasive dominance and inequality.

5. **Democratic Hierarchies vs Dominance Hierarchies**: The text introduces the concept of democratic hierarchies, where temporary leaders are chosen consensually for specific tasks and remain accountable to their followers. In contrast, dominance hierarchies involve regular, top-down imposition of power often backed by violence. The author suggests using a 'consent test' to differentiate between these two forms: if resistance is met with force rather than acknowledged, it's likely a dominance hierarchy.

6. **Chaos**: Another type of social organization mentioned is 'chaos,' where everyone tries to dominate relentlessly but no one achieves lasting power, leading to constant conflict and instability. This concept is reminiscent of Thomas Hobbes' 'state of nature.'

In summary, the text presents a critical view of societal evolution, arguing against a linear progression from primitive to advanced civilizations. Instead, it describes a complex interplay between egalitarianism and dominance, with periodic reversals and setbacks. It emphasizes that modern societies, despite having democratic elements, still exhibit many characteristics of Goliath-like structures characterized by inequality and power concentration.


The text discusses the transition from hunter-gatherer societies to agricultural communities, focusing on why this shift occurred despite its detrimental effects on health and well-being. 

1. **Adoption of Agriculture**: The process of moving from part-time to full-time farming was lengthy. In southwest Asia, for example, it took over 5,000 years for grasses like wheat and barley to increase from around 20-30% to approximately 50-60% of crops grown. This transition period is roughly equivalent to the time span between today and the advent of Classical Greece.

2. **Sedentism and Agriculture**: Sedentism (permanent settlement) often coincided with agriculture. Once communities became reliant on tilled, fixed-field farming, it made sense to stay put due to the full-time labor required for sowing, weeding, and protecting crops from pests. This led to population growth and eventually the development of cities. However, sedentism was not exclusively linked to agriculture; some hierarchical forager societies were also sedentary without farming.

3. **Why Choose Agriculture?** The decision to adopt agriculture isn't fully understood, but it likely involved complex factors. Initially, it might have been driven by seasonal camps used for trade, rituals, or better access to resources. As populations grew and reliance on agriculture increased, people became more tied to the land.

4. **Health Impacts**: The shift to agriculture had significant negative effects on health and well-being. Despite producing more food, farmers suffered from poorer nutrition (lower protein and variety), increased disease due to crowded living conditions, and more strenuous labor. This is evident in reduced height among Neolithic populations compared to their hunter-gatherer ancestors. 

5. **Work Ethics**: Hunter-gatherers are known for minimizing energy expenditure, often focusing on big game hunting when resources are abundant and shifting to less laborious tasks like plant gathering as needed. Farmers, however, were compelled to work harder due to the demands of agriculture. This "principle of least effort" is evident in both historical and contemporary farming practices, where people tend to work less when higher wages are offered because their basic needs have been met.

6. **Variations**: The transition to agriculture varied across regions. Some areas with favorable conditions for farming, like Native American California, never adopted it. Even where agriculture spread, it was often a gradual process with pauses and restarts over centuries. This reluctance wasn't due to ignorance of farming but rather a deliberate choice by hunter-gatherers to maintain their lifestyle.

In summary, the shift from nomadic hunting and gathering to settled farming was complex and multifaceted. While it enabled population growth and urbanization, it also led to significant declines in health and quality of life due to increased labor, poorer nutrition, and more disease. The decision to make this transition likely involved a mix of factors including resource management, societal development, and cultural preferences.


The text discusses the transition from hunter-gatherer societies to agricultural ones, a significant shift that occurred approximately 10,000 years ago known as the Neolithic Revolution. This transition is often puzzling because farming is generally less efficient in terms of time and energy compared to hunting and gathering, offering fewer calories per hour of work. 

The author presents several hypotheses for this shift: 

1. **Population Pressure**: As populations grew, overhunting could have led to a scarcity of game in certain areas, forcing groups to rely more on the predictable, calorie-dense food provided by farming and domesticated animals. This "Malthusian trap" theory suggests that population growth outstripped resources, necessitating a switch to agriculture.

2. **Climate Change and Resource Decline**: Changes in climate or the collapse of other food sources might have compelled groups towards farming. For instance, in Mesopotamia, big game became extinct during the early Neolithic, pushing communities to adopt easier farming methods like flood-retreat agriculture along rivers where conditions were favorable.

However, these factors alone do not fully explain the transition. The author introduces another compelling hypothesis: 

3. **Status Competition (Growth Fetish)**: This theory posits that the desire for status and power played a significant role in the shift to farming. Hierarchical hunter-gatherer societies already had mechanisms to pressure individuals into working harder for community feasts, but agriculture allowed for the production of surplus food, which could be used to host larger, more impressive banquets. These grand feasts were not just about nourishment; they were tools for status elevation, alliance building, and even coercion. 

The author argues that this status competition drove the intensification of agriculture. Once resources became available through farming, there was a constant pressure to outdo one's peers in terms of feast size, variety, and extravagance. This led to a focus on 'conspicuous consumption' - wasteful spending to signal superior status. 

This 'growth fetish' extends beyond feasting to other aspects of early farming communities, such as monumental architecture (palaces, grand burials), human sacrifice, and long-distance trade for luxury items. These practices are not just signs of inequality but also evidence of a preoccupation with growing and accumulating more than is necessary for survival - a drive fueled by social status competition. 

In essence, the text suggests that while environmental factors like population pressure and resource scarcity may have initiated the shift to farming, it was the human desire for status and power that drove its intensification and the development of complex societies. This 'growth fetish' continues to influence our modern values and behaviors, with a persistent focus on material accumulation and outdoing others in wealth and display.


Title: Reversals of Agriculture: Chaos, Inequality, and Violence in Early Farming Communities (Europe & North America)

1. Introduction to the unwelcome transition to agriculture:
   - The shift from nomadic hunting-gathering lifestyles to fixed-field farming was not universally accepted.
   - This transition led to social changes, inequality, and violence in early farming communities worldwide, particularly evident in Europe.

2. Agricultural importation into Europe:
   - Farming practices did not originate in Europe but were introduced via migrants from modern-day Turkey and Greece around 6500 BCE.
   - The spread of agriculture was gradual, taking more than a millennium to reach Ireland, Britain, and Scandinavia.

3. Boom-and-bust patterns in European farming:
   - After the arrival of agriculture, population growth and subsequent collapse were common across Europe.
   - Population spikes occurred as communities flourished for several generations before experiencing dramatic declines or dispersals.
   - The duration of these cycles varied by region; in Ireland and Britain, the population drop happened around 300-400 years after farming's arrival, while in Scandinavia it took 700 years to decrease by 70%.

4. Dietary shifts and abandonment of agriculture:
   - Farmers initially favored grains like wheat but eventually switched to hardier crops (e.g., barley, hazelnuts) as populations declined.
   - These shifts in diet coincided with settlement abandonments and changes in agricultural practices.

5. Increased inequality, wealth accumulation, and violence:
   - As farming communities became more stratified, signs of large-scale violence, fortifications, and defensive structures emerged.
   - The introduction of farming brought not only grain but also warfare and societal inequalities, with some archaeological evidence pointing to massacres involving torture and mutilation.

6. Comparative analysis with North American Ancestral Puebloans:
   - A similar pattern of agricultural boom-and-bust cycles occurred among the Ancestral Pueblo people in present-day southwestern United States.
   - The Ancestral Puebloans relied on "three sisters" crops (beans, squash, and maize) imported from Mesoamerica and experienced four crises marked by population drops, inequality increases, violence spikes, and depopulation before settlement abandonment.

In summary, the transition to agriculture was neither simple nor uniform across the globe. In Europe and North America, this shift led to cycles of booming populations followed by collapses associated with increased inequality, violence, and dietary shifts. These patterns highlight the complex social consequences of adopting fixed-field farming practices, emphasizing that the "inexorable march" of agriculture was not always smooth or welcomed by all communities.


The text discusses the reasons behind the collapse of early farming communities, focusing on two main factors: climate change and disease.

1. Climate Change: Early farmers were more vulnerable to climate fluctuations due to their reliance on specific crops and weather conditions for food production. The Puebloans in the southwestern United States, for instance, experienced multiple cycles of boom (population growth) and bust (depopulation) tied to droughts. Despite surviving earlier climatic fluctuations, a severe fourth drought led to widespread depopulation as maize production failed, causing food shortages and societal collapse.
