Similarly, the first farmers in Europe faced challenges during wetter, cooler periods that negatively impacted their crop yields, leading to population declines.

2. Disease: The advent of agriculture brought humans, animals, and crops together in dense settings, creating an environment conducive for zoonotic diseases (those transmitted from animals to humans). Early farming communities were susceptible to these "density-dependent" diseases because they had larger populations with less mobility, which allowed pathogens to spread and mutate. For example, some archaeological evidence suggests that plague outbreaks occurred among Neolithic Scandinavian communities around 5,200 years ago. Although these early waves of the plague were not as devastating as later pandemics like the Black Death, they still could have played a role in destabilizing some farming settlements.

Other factors contributing to the collapse included social tensions and conflict (scalar stress) arising from increased population densities in urban settings. As communities grew larger and more unequal, disputes, theft, and other forms of social friction intensified. This escalation in conflict could have led some people to leave their settlements in search of less contentious living conditions or to form new, more egalitarian communities.

However, the text also notes that these explanations do not fully account for why societies abandoned their farms just as inequality and hierarchy were becoming entrenched. The emergence of social stratification posed a significant challenge, as it represented a departure from the more fluid, egalitarian social structures of hunter-gatherer civilizations. People might have left their settlements not only due to external pressures (climate change, disease) but also because they objected to the growing inequality and the new power dynamics that came with organized agriculture.

In summary, early farming communities faced multiple threats, including climate-related challenges like droughts and diseases facilitated by high population densities. These factors contributed to cycles of boom and bust as populations expanded and then collapsed due to environmental or health crises. Additionally, the emergence of social stratification and inequality in agrarian societies likely played a role in driving some individuals and groups to abandon their settlements in search of more equitable living conditions.


The passage discusses two ancient settlements, Çatalhöyük in Turkey and Jenne-Jeno in Mali, which were once thriving, egalitarian communities that later experienced significant changes leading to their decline.

1. **Çatalhöyük (9000 BCE - 6000 BCE)**: This site was one of the largest Neolithic settlements in the world, with around 7-8,000 people living in a small area of about ten hectares. The houses were multifunctional, serving as homes, temples, and mausoleums. There was no evidence of social hierarchy or warfare. The inhabitants practiced agriculture alongside foraging, with a diet consisting of various grains, legumes, and meat from domesticated animals.

   The collapse of Çatalhöyük occurred around 6250 BCE to 6000 BCE, marked by a gradual decline rather than a sudden catastrophic event. This decline was characterized by increased inequality, with larger houses indicating greater wealth disparity. There were fewer signs of communal activities and more private animal ownership. Evidence also suggests an increase in violence, indicated by healed skull fractures and slingshot projectiles found at the site. The exact cause of the decline is unclear but could have been due to factors like climate change or resource depletion.

2. **Jenne-Jeno (250 BCE - 900 CE)**: Located in modern Mali, Jenne-Jeno was one of Africa's earliest urban centers. At its peak around 800 CE, it housed about 50,000 people within a forty-two-hectare area. The settlement lacked public buildings like palaces or plazas; instead, it was composed of interwoven alleys and compounds where various professions (potters, blacksmiths, fishers, herders) lived in close proximity.

   Jenne-Jeno also started as an egalitarian society with no signs of social stratification or wealth disparity. Its residents relied on a diverse diet including fish, cattle, and African rice. The decline began around 900 CE when the first wall was built around the urban center, coinciding with the rise in trans-Saharan trade opportunities. This increase in wealth and power likely led to social changes. About a century later, nearly two-thirds of the original settlement were abandoned as families moved into smaller, self-sufficient villages, while a chief took control of the central mound.

Both Çatalhöyük and Jenne-Jeno exemplify how early complex societies could evolve from relatively egalitarian structures towards more hierarchical ones over time, often associated with growing inequality and the emergence of new forms of wealth accumulation or power dynamics. These changes were gradual processes rather than sudden collapses, highlighting the complexity of ancient social transformations.


The text presents three historical cases of societies that experienced shifts from more egalitarian structures to centralized, hierarchical ones, followed by rebellions or abandonments due to rising inequality.

1. **Jenne-Jeno (West Africa, c. 1180 CE)**: Originally an egalitarian settlement, the city's ruler moved his palace to Djenné after flooding affected rice harvests. This shift allowed the chief to establish a more centralized authority, with loyal families following him while others dispersed into the countryside.

2. **Çatalhöyük (Anatolia, c. 7500-6000 BCE)**: This Neolithic settlement was characterized by communal living and shared spaces until around 6000 BCE when signs of increased social stratification appeared. The construction of larger houses, specialized workshops, and more elaborate burials suggest the emergence of an elite class. The city's abandonment is suggested by a shift in residential patterns and decreased use of common areas.

3. **Taosi (China, c. 2000 BCE)**: Initially a large, fortified city with segregated living quarters for the wealthy and poor, Taosi saw its elite-dominated structure overthrown around 2000 BCE. The destruction of monuments, palaces, and ritual sites signaled a rebellion against the ruling class. Despite this upheaval, the city continued to grow and flourish for another two centuries in a seemingly chaotic state.

The common thread among these societies is their initial egalitarian structures that were gradually supplanted by centralized authority and growing inequality. The responses to this shift varied: Jenne-Jeno saw some follow the leader while others left, Çatalhöyük experienced abandonment, and Taosi witnessed a violent rebellion leading to destruction of symbols of power.

These patterns echo counter-dominance strategies seen among foragers, where tensions against domineering individuals or classes can lead to various forms of resistance: from individual departure (voting with feet) to collective action (voting with fire). 

The text also briefly mentions two additional cases from Mesoamerica:

- **San Lorenzo (Mexico, c. 1250-1000 BCE)**: The Olmec's first capital was a ceremonial center with a grand Red Palace built for elite feasts. However, tensions between the growing elite and commoner hunter-gatherers led to abandonment as residents moved away or were forced out. The city's grand structures were later defaced and buried in an act of rebellion against elite rule.

- **Monte Albán (Mexico, c. 1500 BCE - 150 CE)**: Initially a small ceremonial center with no signs of centralized power or extreme wealth disparity, Monte Albán grew into a city hosting thousands of people. It maintained relative equality until the rise of inequality around 7th century CE, when rulers began to assert more control, leading to the construction of palaces and monuments celebrating their power.

In all these examples, the societal shifts from egalitarianism to hierarchy, followed by reactions against increasing inequality, demonstrate recurring themes in human social organization across different cultures and time periods.


The text describes two ancient Mesoamerican civilizations, Teotihuacan and Tiwanaku, which experienced significant societal changes leading to their eventual decline.

1. **Teotihuacan (Mexico):**

   - **Establishment & Layout:** Teotihuacan was a pre-Columbian metropolis founded around 100 BCE, covering 20 square kilometers and housing up to 100,000 people. It was a well-planned city with quadrants, notable for its colossal pyramids - the Pyramid of the Sun, the Pyramid of the Moon, and the Feathered Serpent Pyramid. Despite evidence of advanced knowledge (like writing systems), it lacked clear signs of centralized hierarchy in its art or architecture.

   - **Change & Decline:** Around 200 CE, after a massacre involving around 200 victims at the Feathered Serpent Pyramid, the city underwent dramatic changes. The pyramid was desecrated and later covered by a raised platform in a 'termination ritual', symbolizing a severance of cosmic connection with deities. Post this event, the focus shifted from monumental architecture to urban renewal, with the construction of multi-occupancy apartment buildings. This period was marked by relative equality in wealth distribution, as suggested by house sizes and inferred income levels comparable to modern Denmark.

   - **Final Decline:** Around 500 CE, Teotihuacan entered a phase of increased isolation, with walled compounds becoming more autonomous and signs of wealth disparity emerging. In about 500 CE, the city suffered a massive fire, with over 150 buildings destroyed, including all on the Street of the Dead. This was followed by demolition of temples, pyramids, and public buildings, likely due to internal strife or rebellion rather than foreign invasion. The population halved within a century, and the city was eventually abandoned.

2. **Tiwanaku (Bolivia):**

   - **Egalitarian Beginnings & Growth:** Tiwanaku started as an egalitarian settlement on the shores of Lake Titicaca around 500 BCE, growing into a city of tens of thousands. However, with expansion, inequality and hierarchy increased, marked by the erection of monumental stone figures of high-status individuals, cordoned-off public ceremonial centers, and top-down control of agricultural land.

   - **Decline & Revolution:** By 800 CE, Tiwanaku had become highly stratified. This unsustainable system led to over-exploitation of resources and people. Around this time, the monolithic Gateway of the Sun was toppled, storage jars smashed - signs of a revolution. The departing populace rejected the elite culture, evident in post-Tiwanaku pottery devoid of 'Front Face Deity' and 'Puma Head' iconography. New, smaller rural communities maintained some ceremonies but in open collective spaces, signaling a shift from centralized authority to communal practices.

   - **Climate Change Pressure:** Both Teotihuacan and Tiwanaku faced pressure from climate change. San Lorenzo's abandonment coincided with drought, while Tiwanaku's collapse was linked to Lake Titicaca level fluctuations indicative of prolonged dryness. These environmental stressors likely exacerbated existing societal tensions, contributing to each civilization's downfall.

In both cases, the societies experienced a shift from egalitarianism to hierarchy, followed by rebellion or conflict leading to their decline. Climate change may have acted as a stressor, intensifying social unrest and resource scarcity issues. These historical narratives offer insights into the complex interplay between societal structures, environmental factors, and human responses in ancient civilizations.


The text discusses several ancient civilizations in North America that were once urban centers with monumental architecture, similar to those found in Mesoamerica. These societies, which existed before European contact, include Cahokia (in present-day Illinois), Moundville (Alabama), and various Huhugam sites across the southern Appalachians.

Cahokia, established around 1050 CE, is considered the earliest complex society in North America. It featured vast earthen mounds used for ceremonial purposes and a centralized political structure. The city grew rapidly after the introduction of domesticated corn around 900 CE, which became a staple food source. However, Cahokia declined around 1250-1400 CE, leaving behind abandoned mounds and a once bustling metropolis. The reasons for its collapse are not definitively known but likely include environmental changes and social inequalities.

Moundville, another significant site, developed around 1000 CE and reached its peak population of approximately 10,000 people by 1250-1400 CE. Similar to Cahokia, it had flat-topped mounds, plazas, and evidence of a religious elite receiving tribute in the form of food from surrounding villages. Over time, Moundville shifted from an urban center to a ceremonial hub, with most residents moving back to their original villages. By the 16th century, all pyramids were abandoned.

The Huhugam culture, located in present-day Arizona, also built flat-topped mounds for public rituals and had plazas and ballcourts reminiscent of Mesoamerican cities. They developed complex irrigation systems to support their agriculture, primarily cultivating the "three sisters" (corn, beans, and squash). The Huhugam society shared similarities with Mesoamerican civilizations in terms of architecture, ball games, and religious practices.

The rise and fall of these North American Goliaths bear striking resemblance to other ancient civilizations worldwide, such as Çatalhöyük, Jenne-Jeno, Taosi, Wari, Moche, and Pukura. These societies expanded during favorable climatic periods (like the Medieval Warm Period), adopted agriculture, and developed urban centers. However, they eventually collapsed due to increasing inequality and social unrest, mirroring patterns observed across different continents.

The introduction of Mesoamerican elements, such as corn cultivation and religious practices, played a significant role in shaping these North American societies. Yet, despite these influences, each region developed unique cultural identities, illustrating the capacity for local adaptation and innovation. The collapse of these ancient civilizations underscores the challenges of managing complex societies and highlights the recurring theme of social inequality leading to societal unraveling throughout history.


The text describes a pattern of societal development and collapse across various indigenous North American civilizations, including the Huhugam (Sonoran Desert), Puebloans, Calusa, and north-west coast Native Americans. 

1. **Emergence of Complex Societies**: Each of these societies initially developed from small, egalitarian communities into complex hierarchical structures. This transition was often characterized by the emergence of a priestly class or elite group that asserted control over resources and power. They exhibited their dominance through various means such as grand architecture (like pyramids and mounds), extravagant burials, and in some cases, slavery and human sacrifice.

2. **Accumulation of Power**: These elite groups utilized religious and social advantages to amass power. They often claimed exclusive access to special knowledge or skills, using this as a tool for control over the population, resources, and information. This fusion of different forms of power – religious authority, violence, political influence, and wealth – became a blueprint for gaining and maintaining societal dominance.

3. **Collapse**: Despite their initial grandeur, these civilizations frequently experienced rapid decline. Reasons for collapse varied but often included disease, drought, overpopulation, environmental degradation, internal conflict, and warfare. In many cases, the societies became increasingly unstable due to elite competition and power struggles leading to infighting and inter-community conflicts.

4. **Climate's Role**: Climate shifts played a significant role in these collapses. The construction of these complex societies often coincided with warmer periods that allowed for larger surpluses, supporting population growth and urban development. However, cooler phases, particularly the onset of the 'Little Ice Age' around 1250-1850 CE, led to droughts and other environmental stressors which undermined these societies' ability to sustain their populations and agricultural systems.

5. **Collective Memory**: A striking similarity across these different indigenous cultures is their oral traditions' shared narrative of this period as one of oppression and rebellion against overbearing rulers. Modern Puebloans recall the collapse of Chaco Canyon as a reaction to concentrated power in leaders, while Navajo and Cherokee stories depict their ancestors overthrowing arrogant priestly classes or tyrannical rulers.

6. **Cahokia's Absence**: Interestingly, Cahokia, one of the largest pre-Columbian cities in North America, is notably absent from these collective memories. This could suggest a different historical experience or possibly a deliberate suppression due to its association with later colonial powers.

This narrative underscores how societal structures and power dynamics can lead to both the rise and fall of complex civilizations, often influenced by environmental factors, internal conflicts, and collective responses against oppressive rule.


The text discusses the transformation of Native American societies following the collapse of complex urban civilizations like Cahokia, Moundville, and Huhugam (O'odham). After these centralized elite-driven societies fell, their descendants organized themselves to prevent the re-emergence of dominating hierarchies. 

1. **Decentralization of Power**: Instead of monumental architecture controlled by priests and rulers, smaller mounds were used for communal ceremonies. Religion became more decentralized and public, with spiritual practices shared among community members rather than controlled by a select few. For instance, the Huhugam transitioned from burying elites in elaborate adobe coffins to cremating all dead individuals equally.

2. **Political Restructuring**: Leadership structures evolved into more democratic hierarchies with checks and balances. This often involved councils of men and women, clan-based dispersal across towns, and consensus decision-making processes. Examples include the Cherokee's dual council system and confederacies like the Haudenosaunee (Iroquois).

3. **Shift in Lifestyle**: Many societies moved away from large-scale agriculture towards more mobile, hunter-gatherer lifestyles. This was partly due to climatic changes making extensive farming difficult. Despite this shift, there were still conflicts and some degree of inequality, but overall, it provided greater freedom and healthier diets for many people.

4. **Resistance to Domination**: The trend across various cultures shows a strong human inclination to resist domination. This is evident in acts like deliberately destroying elite monuments or leaving communities altogether (voting with feet and fire) when faced with oppressive hierarchies. 

5. **Historical Misconception**: The text challenges the conventional wisdom that centralized governments were necessary for large-scale agriculture and city formation. Instead, it suggests that cities often predate states, and dominating elites frequently hijacked established food production systems leading to conflict and eventual collapse.

6. **Egalitarian Origins of Urban Life**: Many urban communities started egalitarianly, with no signs of leaders or unequal housing. These communities could persist for extended periods (thousands of years in some cases) before gradually developing into more hierarchical societies marked by inequality, warfare, centralized agriculture, and sometimes human sacrifice, ultimately leading to abandonment or transformation.

The overarching theme is the cyclical nature of human societal development – from egalitarian origins to emergence of dominating hierarchies and subsequent resistance or collapse, often influenced by environmental factors and deeply ingrained psychological traits against subjugation.


The text discusses the fragility and potential abandonment of early complex societies, or "Goliaths," and explores reasons why some societies evolved into enduring empires while others did not. 

1. **Fragility of Early Goliaths:** Despite a common pattern where citizens left or abandoned Goliaths when walls weakened and social control was underdeveloped, the timeline and outcomes varied. For instance, Tiwanaku and Cahokia collapsed relatively quickly (a generation or two), while Çatalhöyük and San Lorenzo declined over centuries. The aftermath also differed: some areas remained vacant, like parts of the Mississippi Basin around Cahokia, whereas other regions saw more unequal and autocratic societies emerge, such as those following Monte Albán in Oaxaca Valley.

2. **Archaeological Evidence:** A study by Dan Lawrence et al. (2023) analyzed hundreds of urban sites across Southwest Asia from 12,000 to 2,000 years ago. Contrary to expectations, the longest-lived settlements were often in marginal environments rather than productive agricultural lands. Settlement longevity was negatively correlated with centralized arrangements; as hierarchy increased, the settlements became less persistent. Early, more egalitarian societies proved surprisingly resilient, averaging over 3,000 years during the 12,000-10,000 BCE period before declining with Goliath growth.

3. **Reversibility of Urbanism:** This pattern might extend to more recent discoveries in the Amazon. Advanced irrigation systems and substantial urban centers were found beneath the rainforest canopy, such as a 6,000-platform city in Ecuador from 2,500 years ago. Despite their impressive scale, these cities eventually abandoned for dispersed lifestyles, emphasizing that urbanism and accompanying inequality are reversible.

4. **New Fuels, New Goliaths:** Not all early Goliaths collapsed or were abandoned; some evolved into larger, more permanent structures. The introduction of sweet potatoes to 16th-century New Guinea, for example, boosted agricultural productivity and population growth without leading to the formation of a centralized state, unlike in Egypt. This highlights that creating a surplus is necessary but not sufficient for developing a state.

5. **Agriculture as Necessary But Not Sufficient Condition:** Ten independent centers of agricultural production worldwide only resulted in four developing states: the Near East, Yellow River Basin in China, Andes, and Mesoamerica. States served as political frameworks for Goliaths, enabling more organized, stable, large-scale societies. However, agriculture alone does not ensure state formation, as evidenced by New Guinea's agricultural surplus without a centralized authority or Egypt's early hierarchical structures without extensive farming.

6. **Goliath Fuel:** The concept of "Goliath fuel" refers to caged land, lootable resources, and monopolizable weapons that can explain why states emerged in specific regions (like the four aforementioned centers) but not elsewhere, such as Japan. This theory suggests that while agriculture provides the necessary surplus for full-time rulers, additional factors like control over land and resources are crucial for establishing and maintaining large-scale, enduring societies.

In summary, early complex societies or "Goliaths" were often fragile and prone to abandonment when walls weakened and social control was inadequate. Archaeological evidence suggests that settlement longevity decreased with increased hierarchy, indicating resilience in more egalitarian societies. While agriculture is necessary for state formation, it's not sufficient; additional factors like resource control play essential roles. The uneven emergence of states worldwide can be attributed to the presence or absence of "Goliath fuel," ultimately shaping the trajectory of societal development.


The text discusses the factors that led to the emergence of early macro-states, particularly focusing on the role of domesticated crops and animals as "lootable resources." These lootable resources were crucial for the formation and consolidation of power in these early civilizations.

1. **Restricted Exit Options**: Early states had restricted exit options, meaning it was difficult for people to leave or migrate away. This was often due to geographical barriers like rivers (Mesopotamia) or deserts (Egypt). 

2. **Monopolizable Weapons**: New weapons were necessary to control larger populations. The advent of bronze metallurgy around 3000 BCE provided hand-held weapons like swords, which helped in consolidating power.

3. **Domesticated Crops and Animals as Lootable Resources**: After the end of the ice age, domesticated crops and animals became available. These were not just food sources but also crucial for taxation and wealth accumulation by ruling classes.

   - **Cereals**: Crops like wheat, barley in Mesopotamia and Egypt, rice and millet in China, and maize in Mesoamerica were highly valued. They had regular harvest periods, were calorically dense, and couldn't be easily hidden or harvested unpredictably. This made them ideal for taxation, contributing to state formation.
   
   - **Domesticated Animals**: Animals like cattle, goats, sheep, horses provided storable food (milk, meat), materials (hide, wool, bone, sinew), and labor (for plowing fields). They significantly increased surplus and wealth disparity, aiding in state development.

4. **Uneven Distribution**: The availability of these lootable resources varied geographically. For instance, China received pigs, rice, and millet; Mesopotamia and Egypt got cattle, goats, sheep, wheat, barley, and einkorn; Mesoamerica was given turkey, beans, squash, and maize; while the Andes were handed llamas, alpacas, and potatoes.

5. **Why Some Regions Failed to Develop States**: Not all regions with agriculture formed states. This was often due to lack of highly lootable crops:

   - **New Guinea, Western Africa, the Sahel, Ecuador, and Eastern US** had domesticated crops but lacked easily visible, storable, high-yield resources. For example, bananas in New Guinea rot quickly, squash in Ecuador and the eastern US has a prolonged harvest period, and taro, potatoes, sweet potatoes can be stored for only 1-6 months underground after harvest.

In summary, the text argues that the availability of certain domesticated crops and animals, particularly those that are highly visible, have predictable harvest times, can be stored for long periods, and require significant labor to grow, played a pivotal role in the development of early macro-states. These resources enabled taxation, wealth accumulation by elites, and population control necessary for state formation. Conversely, regions lacking such 'lootable' resources often did not develop centralized states.


The text discusses the concept of "Goliath" societies, referring to complex, hierarchical states that emerge when certain conditions are met. These conditions include having lootable resources (like rice or millet) and a population density high enough to necessitate asset protection and status competition. 

Several historical examples are given: 
1. Western Africa and the Sahel: Despite having abundant resources like African rice and pearl millet, these regions lacked domesticated animals and had low population densities. The geography provided numerous escape routes, contributing to a slower pace of state formation.
2. Japan: Rice cultivation, introduced around 689-1015 CE, laid the foundation for elite groups and eventually an empire. The Japanese imperial lineage dates back to around 500 CE.
3. Eastern North America: Civilizations like Cahokia arose after the introduction of corn from Mesoamerica but struggled during the Little Ice Age, indicating that the conditions for a Goliath were not always met consistently.

The Indus Valley Civilization is presented as an exception to this rule. Despite having resources like rice and millet and being located in a riverine valley, it never developed into a hierarchical state. Possible reasons include low population density (leading to less need for asset protection) or cultural norms that prevented state formation. 

The text also highlights the concept of "escape" from Goliath societies, referencing Zomia, a mountainous region in Southeast Asia inhabited by stateless peoples numbering over 130 million. This area spans ten countries but is not controlled by any of them, suggesting that escape or avoidance of complex states is historically common and continues to the present day.

The author concludes by noting that while Goliath societies tend to re-emerge after collapses in many regions (like China, Egypt, and Mesopotamia), history also includes numerous instances of people successfully escaping such systems.


Zomia is a region encompassing highland areas of Southeast Asia, primarily above 300 meters in elevation. It's a vast, largely ungoverned territory that stretches across parts of Myanmar, Thailand, Laos, Vietnam, China, and India. The concept of Zomia was popularized by anthropologist James C. Scott, who argued it's not the remnant of primitive societies but a conscious creation of people seeking to escape state control.

The formation of Zomia is rooted in resistance against lowland states and empires. Over centuries, various groups—including refugees, exiles, fugitive slaves, and oppressed minorities—fled to these highlands to avoid subjugation. This was often triggered by the violence, economic disruption, and diseases associated with state formation in the lowlands. 

Scott posits that Zomia's societies are largely egalitarian due to this shared history of resistance against domination. However, some hierarchical structures did persist among certain groups after migration, albeit less pronounced than in their lowland counterparts. 

The geography of Zomia is crucial to its stateless nature. Its rugged terrain—mountains, steep slopes, and dense forests—makes it difficult for larger, organized forces to invade and control. These landscapes offer numerous advantages for guerrilla warfare: they facilitate hit-and-run tactics, create natural barriers, and provide multiple escape routes. 

This geographical advantage is not unique to Zomia. Throughout history, marginalized groups worldwide have utilized challenging terrains—like the Great Dismal Swamp in North America or the Amazon rainforest—to evade domination. 

Agriculture plays a significant role in Zomia's resistance strategy too. Certain crops, like cassava, taro, and potatoes, are less attractive to ruling classes because they're hard to store, invisible when left in the ground, and require less labor. This makes them 'escape crops,' allowing farmers to maintain self-sufficiency without being coerced into wage labor or taxation. 

Finally, agricultural practices like shifting cultivation (or swidden farming) enhance mobility. Unlike settled farming, which anchors people to specific locations, swiddening allows communities to move their fields regularly. This increased mobility makes them harder to control and exploit.

In essence, Zomia represents a successful model of resistance against state formation. Its inhabitants have used geographical barriers, 'escape crops,' and mobile farming practices to maintain autonomy and resist assimilation into larger, hierarchical societies. This pattern isn't confined to Zomia; it echoes across various cultures and historical periods as a strategy for preserving freedom from domination.


The text discusses the evolution of Goliaths, or dominant hierarchical societies, over time. It highlights how these societies, despite emerging independently across different regions and cultures (a phenomenon known as convergent evolution), often developed similar structures and strategies to address common challenges.

1. **Common Challenges and Solutions**: Early states faced a universal problem: controlling lands distant from the capital. To overcome this, they established internal administrations or bureaucracies. This is evident in civilizations such as Mesoamerica's Monte Alban, Egypt, Mesopotamia's Uruk, and ancient China. These societies delegated authority to local governors or elites to manage territories at a distance from the central ruler, a practice known as 'government at a distance.'

2. **Cultural Transmission**: Agrarian, state-based communities typically had effective ways of transmitting culture across generations and regimes, often through written systems. Elites could also deliberately adopt and amplify successful practices or ideas from other groups. For example, coin-based currencies emerged independently in China and Lydia but were later embraced by governments for wider use. Similarly, Christianity was already spreading in Rome before Emperor Constantine's official adoption, which accelerated its rise.

3. **Selection for Power**: Successful strategies in violent conflict tended to prolong the life of Goliaths and facilitate their growth. Over time, empires accumulated technologies and practices that enabled them to conquer and control larger groups over broader territories. This included methods like demanding tribute, using human sacrifices to solidify rule, and employing minor bureaucrats or accountants for administrative tasks.

4. **Contrast with Stateless Societies**: The text contrasts these evolving Goliaths with stateless societies like those in Zomia. These groups often avoid centralized authority by living in remote, hard-to-reach areas, minimizing the growth of lootable resources, and cultivating narratives warning against hierarchy. Their strategies are adaptive responses to thwart potential domination.

5. **Historical Perspective**: The author prompts readers to consider how we might view historical "Goliaths" differently if they had collapsed without leaving a trace, similar to Cahokia in Native American history. This encourages reflection on the narratives we construct around powerful civilizations and the potential biases in our historical interpretations.

In summary, the text argues that despite geographical and cultural differences, early human societies independently evolved similar structures and strategies due to convergent evolution – solving common problems in comparable ways. The successful implementation of these solutions facilitated the growth and longevity of hierarchical societies, driving the accumulation of power and influence over time. Meanwhile, stateless societies employ alternative strategies to resist centralized authority, often by maintaining mobility, living in remote areas, and preserving cautionary tales about past hierarchies.


The passage discusses several convergent evolutions among early "Goliaths," or powerful, centralized states. These commonalities highlight how similar challenges and solutions emerged independently across different civilizations.

1. **Human Sacrifice**: This practice was widespread among the earliest Goliaths, including Cahokia, Tiwanaku, Teotihuacan, Monte Alban, Olmecs of San Lorenzo, and many others from Mesopotamia, China, Americas, and West Africa. The Shang Dynasty in China is an example where around 13,000 people were sacrificed over two centuries. This practice served to establish the ruler's power by demonstrating their ability to legitimately kill members of their own group, thereby showcasing their superiority and control. The predictors of human sacrifice across cultures are hereditary rulers, larger populations, and internal conflict over resources.

2. **Use of Violence**: Human sacrifice is a form of random violence that early leaders used to assert their power. This display of arbitrary execution was once the ultimate proof of supreme authority, placing the ruler above ordinary moral standards. Over time, such unrestrained violence became less common as laws and social expectations developed, but its historical significance in establishing legitimacy remains.

3. **Narratives of Domination**: Early Goliaths developed similar stories to justify their dominance over large populations. The Hobbesian narrative, emphasizing order and peace amidst chaos, was adopted globally - from Europe to China and India. Empires often portrayed themselves as bringing unity and balance to the cosmos, while those outside their borders were depicted as chaotic and in need of civilization. This narrative was used to legitimize hierarchical structures by suggesting that order and progress were only possible under the rule of these centralized states.

4. **Civilization vs. Barbarism**: A universal myth among Goliaths is the contrast between "civilized" and "uncivilized." This dichotomy was used to justify inequalities within these societies. Non-state peoples were often labeled as less developed or even savage, while those within the state were portrayed as superior. This narrative continues today, with the term 'civilization' generally associated with progress and refinement, contrasting those outside the state system.

These convergent evolutions demonstrate how similar challenges faced by early centralized states led to strikingly similar solutions, regardless of geographical or cultural differences. This convergence underscores the universal nature of human societal development under comparable conditions.


The text discusses the concept of "Goliath Evolution" as a framework to understand the emergence and development of large, hierarchical societies or Goliaths throughout history. This theory posits that certain characteristics have consistently appeared across different cultures and time periods due to evolutionary pressures, often leading to dominance hierarchies that benefit those at the top.

1. **Mandate of Heaven/Divine Right**: Many early societies, including the Calusa, had a divine justification for their rulers' power. This "Mandate of Heaven" or similar beliefs served to legitimize hierarchies, suggesting that the ruler's authority came from a higher power.

2. **Ideology and Domination**: Ian Morris defines ideology as a set of lies beneficial to those in power. In all societies, stories have been used to justify inequalities. The modern narrative of meritocracy, which asserts that wealth and power are earned through individual skill and performance, is an example. Studies show that belief in meritocracy correlates with higher economic inequality, creating a feedback loop where the myth justifies increasing disparities.

3. **Moralizing Religions**: The development of moralizing religions—belief systems centered around punitive gods who reward or punish based on moral behavior—is also seen as an outcome of Goliath evolution. These religions emerged after the formation of large, unequal societies and served to control populations by enforcing hierarchical norms. Examples include the Abrahamic faiths (Islam, Christianity, Judaism) and the Incan god Viracocha.

4. **Social Power Fusion**: The first states combined several forms of power: authoritative hierarchy (political power), backed by force (violent power), supported by rituals and narratives that legitimized rule (information power), and extracting resources from the populace (economic power). These elements often converged around a central theme of male dominance.

5. **Patriarchy**: A common feature across many Goliath societies is patriarchy—a social structure where men hold most power, and women are marginalized. This evolution began with men's inherent tendencies towards status competition, often expressed through violence, combined with their physical advantages. In hunter-gatherer societies, these differences didn't lead to large-scale oppression due to the muted nature of violence. However, with the advent of warfare over lootable resources, the need for organized male fighters arose, leading to patrilineal structures and a shift in power dynamics between sexes.

6. **Evolution of Patriarchy**: Successful early conquerors tended to have strongly patriarchal cultures, which they then imposed on the societies they dominated. Over time, institutions, religions, and legal codes reinforced male rule and diminished women's status. The first states codified these inequalities, with laws like the Code of Ur-Nammu (2100 BCE) punishing women for adultery while leaving their male partners unscathed. This pattern was mirrored in subsequent legal codes and religious doctrines, further entrenching patriarchal norms.

In essence, the "Goliath Evolution" theory argues that various aspects of modern hierarchical societies—from divine justifications for power to moralizing religions and gender inequalities—are outcomes of a historical process driven by the pressures of large-scale social organization. These characteristics emerged and persisted due to their utility in maintaining control within unequal societies, reinforcing the dominance of those at the top.


The text discusses the evolution of societal structures, focusing on the emergence of patriarchy and societal fragility associated with the accumulation of lootable resources. Here's a detailed summary and explanation:

1. **Emergence of Patriarchy**: The development of lootable resources around 8,000-4,000 years ago led to a power imbalance among men, resulting in patriarchal societies. This isn't attributed to a deliberate social structure created by men for their benefit, but rather as an unintended consequence of evolutionary pressures. Men who were more prone to violence and aggression had a higher likelihood of seizing power due to minor differences in psychology and physiology. This domination was then reinforced over time through rules and religions, leading to deep-seated gender inequalities.

2. **Goliath Evolution**: The term "Goliath" refers to powerful entities (states, organizations) that can amass significant resources and control over others. The evolution of Goliaths involves various 'balancing feedbacks' or mechanisms aimed at stabilizing these power structures. These include the development of administrative systems, bureaucracy, writing, and other information processing tools to manage larger populations effectively.

3. **Collapse of Hierarchical Societies**: Many early hierarchical societies, such as the Puebloans, European farmers, and Mississippi mound-based kingdoms, lacked these stabilizing mechanisms and thus were prone to collapse. They often exhibited fission-fusion cycles (periods of unity followed by fragmentation), high levels of hierarchy, and inequality before collapsing. This pattern suggests that societal inequality isn't automatic but is actively contested and resisted.

4. **Ageing of Societies**: The text introduces the concept that hierarchical societies tend to become more fragile over time—an "ageing" process. Even well-established empires like the Western Roman Empire and Lowland Maya, despite having coped with internal strife and external threats for centuries, eventually succumbed to collapse. A study by complexity scientists using the Seshat Global History Databank and MOROS databases confirmed this trend: states were more likely to terminate in their first 200 years, after which they maintained a relatively stable but high rate of termination. This pattern mirrors human mortality rates, where the risk of death increases up to a certain age and then stabilizes at a high level until death occurs.

In essence, the text argues that the accumulation of lootable resources can lead to societal power imbalances (patriarchy) and increased fragility over time. This "Goliath's Curse" is reinforced by mechanisms that attempt to stabilize these power structures but may also contribute to their eventual collapse if not balanced appropriately. The ageing of societies reflects a growing vulnerability to internal strife and external pressures, paralleling human mortality trends.


The text discusses the phenomenon of increasing societal vulnerability and inequality over time, particularly in relation to the accumulation of "lootable resources" or Goliath fuel. This concept is illustrated through historical case studies and statistical analysis.

1. **Societal Vulnerability and Critical Slowing Down**: As societies grow more complex, they become increasingly vulnerable to collapse. This trend is likened to the phenomenon of 'critical slowing down' observed in biological systems, where organisms respond less effectively to disturbances as they age or reach a state of maximal complexity. Examples include the first farmers of Europe and Pueblo societies of North America, which showed signs of this critical slowing down (slower recovery from shocks) before their respective collapses.

2. **Inequality and Lootable Resources**: The rise of inequality is strongly linked to the availability of lootable resources—resources that can be monopolized, controlled, or extracted. This pattern is evident across various historical societies, from early farmers to ancient civilizations. Key factors contributing to this include lowered mobility (immobilized land), large populations, and surplus resources that are predictable, dense, and transferable across generations.

3. **Power Dynamics**: Wealth derived from lootable resources can be easily translated into other forms of power, creating a self-reinforcing cycle where the rich accumulate more wealth and subsequently more power. This dynamic is exemplified by historical instances such as feast-throwing leaders amassing political influence through their ability to produce surplus food, or modern corporations lobbying for policies that increase their profits.

4. **Economic Disparity**: Over time, the value of capital (like land and stocks) tends to appreciate more rapidly than labor wages, exacerbating wealth disparities. This phenomenon, referred to as a 'wealth pump', systematically transfers wealth from the bottom to the top of society, perpetuating inequality.

5. **Historical Inequality Levels**: Across numerous pre-modern states, inequality levels were often staggeringly high, approaching theoretical maximums where a small elite captures most of societal surplus while the majority barely survives. Modern states generally show lower, though still significant, levels of inequality.

6. **Extractive Institutions**: The text also introduces the concept of extractive institutions, which measure the concentration of power within a society. Societies with more concentrated power—including control over resources, decision-making, violence, and information—tend to exhibit higher levels of elite wealth extraction from the broader population. The degree of democracy or diversity in media is used as an indicator of this power distribution.

In essence, the text argues that societal complexity and resource concentration often lead to increased vulnerability and inequality, perpetuated by self-reinforcing cycles where wealth translates into power, exacerbating existing disparities. This pattern is observed across diverse historical contexts, suggesting it's a fundamental challenge of complex human societies.


The passage discusses the concept of power imbalance and its negative effects on societies, using historical examples from ancient civilizations. 

1. **Inequality and Extractive Power:** Increasing wealth inequality leads to a concentration of power, making societies more extractive. This means that those with more power (Goliaths) take more energy, labor, and resources from the rest of the population (Davids). This is seen in various forms of power structures: government, companies, families, and ancient civilizations.

2. **Unequal Exchange:** Power imbalance often results in unequal exchanges. For instance, if two parties have equal bargaining power, their trade should be fair. However, when one party gains significant advantage (through weapons, status symbols, etc.), they can force a more favorable but unfair exchange. This is illustrated by the rulers of Monte Alban who took most of the corn harvest from their subjects in return for a small portion, using this surplus to build grand monuments and live lavishly.

3. **Justification of Unequal Exchange:** Elites often justify unequal exchanges by offering intangible or exaggerated benefits. For example, priests claiming weather control, kings representing gods, or modern billionaires promising space travel. These narratives provide an explanation for the exchange and its 'fairness', making the population more accepting of exploitative conditions.

4. **Costs of Inequality:** As societies become more unequal and extractive, the costs outweigh the benefits. Early Goliath civilizations like Tiwanaku, Çatalhöyük, and San Lorenzo didn't just collapse under domination but showed signs of internal decay due to inequality. This includes poorer physical and mental health, more violence, and social ills such as higher crime rates and teenage pregnancy.

5. **Wealth Pump & Popular Immiseration:** Inequality establishes a 'wealth pump' where elites benefit at the expense of the poor. This leads to 'popular immiseration', or the impoverishment of the masses, as living costs rise and standards of living decrease.

6. **Elite Competition:** As wealth inequality grows, competition among elites intensifies for high-status positions. Elites have more resources to stir up trouble, such as initiating wars or revolutions, leading to instability within societies.

7. **Goliath's Curse:** Extractive institutions contain the seeds of their own destruction. As inequality rises and elite competition intensifies, internal erosion weakens societies. Early Goliaths lacked mechanisms to control their populace, leading to patterns of fission-fusion or outright abandonment.

8. **Collapse Triggers:** While weak institutions and growing inequality can destabilize societies, additional factors are often needed for collapse. Historical examples include the deposition of a ruler due to neglecting vital natural forces (like the Wind and Storm Cloud) that ensure prosperity, leading to famine, anger, and rebellion.

In summary, power imbalance, or the Goliath phenomenon, can lead to societal fragility and collapse due to unequal exchanges, rising costs of inequality, elite competition, and vulnerability to natural and social disruptions. Historical civilizations that became overly extractive and unequal often faced internal strife and eventual decline.


The passage discusses the factors contributing to the collapse of ancient civilizations, focusing on the interplay between societal vulnerabilities and external shocks. It highlights that while environmental factors like climate change, drought, disease, and invasions played significant roles, their impact varied greatly depending on a society's resilience or vulnerability.

1. **Climate Change and Drought**: Climate change often preceded collapses, but its role wasn't uniform. Severe droughts could devastate societies, as seen in the final termination of the Puebloans. However, similar climatic shifts didn't always lead to collapse; Monte Alban's neighboring settlements, undergoing a comparable drought, did not abandon their cities.

2. **Societal Vulnerabilities**: Societies became increasingly fragile as they grew more hierarchical and unequal. Discontent among the populace and elite conflicts emerged. These societies lacked effective mechanisms to control large, potentially hostile groups of people, making them susceptible to crises.

3. **Shocks and Tipping Points**: Large shocks like war, rebellion, or drought could knock these vulnerable societies out of their "valley" (their usual way of life), leading to collapse. Smaller disturbances might also suffice if the society was already in a precarious state – this is referred to as a 'tipping point.'

4. **Extractive Institutions**: More extractive institutions made societies more vulnerable by shallowing their "valley," reducing their ability to absorb shocks. This decreased their resilience and increased the likelihood of collapse when hit by a significant event.

5. **Disease, Invasion, and Response Capacity**: Packing people together in cities could lead to disease outbreaks, potentially causing exoduses. Invaders could also wipe out communities. The capacity to respond effectively to crises was compromised by extractive arrangements, further increasing vulnerability.

6. **Resilience vs Vulnerability**: The passage uses the metaphor of a ball rocking in a valley to illustrate societal resilience or vulnerability. A shallow valley (extractive institutions) makes a society more prone to collapse, while effective responses can help maintain balance even during shocks.

7. **Historical Examples**: The Late Antique Little Ice Age (536-660 CE) is used as an example of how different societies reacted differently to similar climatic challenges. Despite experiencing comparable temperature drops, some societies like Monte Alban underwent significant changes or collapse, while others remained stable. 

8. **Predictors of Resilience**: The biggest predictor of resilience was the democratic or extractive nature of the society. More inclusive societies (less extractive) had less famine, disease, conflict, and organizational change during this period.

In summary, the collapse of ancient civilizations wasn't solely due to environmental factors but was significantly influenced by societal vulnerabilities exacerbated by extractive institutions. These vulnerable societies were more susceptible to shocks like droughts, diseases, and invasions, leading to collapse when their resilience was overwhelmed. The capacity for effective response and societal inclusiveness were key factors in determining a civilization's resilience during such challenging periods.


The passage discusses the advantages of democratic societies in responding to crises, particularly natural disasters, drawing on historical evidence and contemporary studies. 

1. **Historical Evidence**: The text begins by referencing a study of thirty-three societies across different climate-related disasters, concluding that more democratic groups tended to respond better. This was due to local people's ability to act quickly and effectively on new challenges, unlike distant elites who were often insulated from threats like droughts. The quicker response of democratic societies led to less dramatic changes (like collapse) being necessary. 

2. **Social Capital**: Democracy also fosters stronger social connections, or 'social capital'. During times of stress, this leads to more effective collaboration. This is because democratic processes involve voting, deliberating, and civic engagement, which strengthen community ties. 

3. **Modern Studies**: Numerous modern studies support these historical findings. A study using the Emergency Event Database found that democratic countries with robust state capacity experienced fewer fatalities and affected individuals during disasters, ranging from floods to earthquakes. This is attributed to both the resources available to strong states and the equitable distribution of aid in democracies, unlike in extractive institutions where power is concentrated among a few. 

4. **Wisdom of Crowds**: The passage also delves into the 'wisdom of crowds' theory, which posits that large diverse groups make better decisions than a small elite. This concept was first proposed by statistician Francis Galton and has since been supported by various studies showing crowds outperforming individuals in predictions related to sports, geopolitics, and general knowledge. 

5. **Information Sharing**: Effective decision-making within these groups relies heavily on open information sharing. Each individual brings unique perspectives or pieces of the puzzle; only when they exchange this information freely can a complete picture emerge. 

6. **Deliberative Democracy**: The text further explores 'deliberative democracy', where randomly selected citizens, briefed by experts, deliberate and make policy decisions. This approach has shown promise in reducing political polarization and even aiding reconciliation in post-conflict societies, as seen in Bosnia. It's also noted that similar methods are used in scientific endeavors like the IPCC, where large diverse groups of scientists collaborate to make accurate predictions and decisions.

In conclusion, the passage argues that democratic societies are more resilient in the face of crises due to their ability to respond quickly, foster social capital, leverage the wisdom of crowds, and facilitate effective decision-making through information sharing and deliberation. This contrasts with extractive institutions, which tend to be less responsive and socially cohesive.

The text discusses the historical pattern of state formation, often characterized by warlords or military strongmen who seize power through force and negotiation with other elites. This template is seen across various civilizations, from ancient Egypt to modern-day examples like Liberia's Charles Taylor.

In Liberia, the civil war (1989-97) was fought over control of natural resources such as diamonds and rubber. Taylor, one of the main factions' leaders, controlled 'Taylorland,' a territory that extended into neighboring Sierra Leone, where he held monopolies on resources like diamonds, timber, and rubber. His forces were partly funded by Firestone, an American tire company, which provided food, cash, and checks in exchange for protection of the company's rubber plantation. Despite Firestone's denial of any collaborative relationship, this is an example of a private entity supporting a warlord in exchange for maintaining order around their economic interests.

Taylor's victory in the post-war election in 1997, despite his controversial campaign slogan, was aided by his control over radio stations, which he used to intimidate voters into believing the war would resume if he lost. After assuming office, Taylor continued illicit activities such as aiding the smuggling of blood diamonds and weapons and embezzling government funds.

The text argues that this pattern—warlords becoming state leaders through force—is not unique to Liberia or Africa but is a common historical phenomenon. It cites examples from China, where dynastic transitions often involved warlords seizing power, and Egypt, one of the few exceptions.

The narrative then shifts to contemporary examples of this pattern in Medellín, Colombia (2009-2012) and the Democratic Republic of Congo (DRC). In Medellín, two organized crime groups (razones) fought for control, leading to a truce and the establishment of La Oficina, a criminal council that governed like a mini-state. This included managing borders, settling disputes, and maintaining high prices for drugs through violence and intimidation (El Pacto del Fusil).

In the DRC, armed groups responded to increased value of rare earth minerals by establishing control over mines. They 'taxed' miners in exchange for protection, with more elaborate administrations emerging around gold mining due to its less lootable nature (requiring more sophisticated systems for tax collection).

These examples illustrate the overlap between warlordism, statehood, and organized crime. All involve hierarchical structures that coercively extract resources from a territory and population—a definition virtually indistinguishable from that of a state. 

The text concludes by emphasizing the provision of services as a survival strategy for these self-centered despots. Even warlords, driven primarily by personal gain, often provide basic services like protection and dispute resolution to maintain citizen welfare, ensuring a larger, healthier population that yields more resources. This is reminiscent of the services early states provided under leaders like Narmer, highlighting the continuity between historical state formation and contemporary organized crime dynamics.


The text discusses a sociological perspective on state formation and collapse, as outlined by Katherine Hirschfeld in her book "Gangster States." This theory likens the establishment of states to criminal enterprises or rackets. 

1. **State Formation as a Criminal Enterprise:** According to this view, when there's a stationary economic surplus, it becomes profitable for groups to establish predatory monopolies or "rackets." These can be established through force, often involving lootable resources and monopolizable weapons. For instance, in ancient Mesopotamia, warfare began with the Halafians taking over obsidian trade routes by force, transforming fair-trading relationships into extractive tribute systems.

2. **State Collapse:** The collapse of such rackets involves their destruction and regeneration. Even if a state collapses, the resources that made it attractive often remain, incentivizing neighbors or internal competitors to violently re-establish similar schemes. This pattern is seen across various historical cases, including Yugoslavia, the Soviet Union, Afghanistan, and others. 

3. **Increased Violence During State Formation:** The text references a study of violence over 12,000 years, which shows that violence was low in Neolithic times but spiked dramatically during the formation of early states like Uruk and First Dynasty Egypt. This violence peaked at around 20-30% of skeletons showing signs of conflict. This pattern is also seen in other parts of the world, such as in the Americas with the rise of Monte Albán.

4. **Gendered Nature of Violence:** The perpetrators of this violence have historically been men, often fighting-age males. For example, during the 1994 Rwandan genocide, less than 3% of the population committed violent crimes, but they were predominantly young to middle-aged males. 

5. **Collapse as a "War of the Few Against the Many":** During collapse, violence is not random; it's typically carried out by trained individuals - soldiers, mercenaries, or paramilitary groups from either the previous regime or foreign powers. This results in disproportionate death and disappearance rates among the population, as these trained fighters clash over control of resources and power structures. 

6. **Case Study: Wari Collapse:** The text provides a historical example of this phenomenon with the collapse of the Wari empire in the Andes (600-1000 CE). The collapse was marked by increased violence, including tripling of non-lethal head injuries and a quadrupling of fatal head injuries. Additionally, evidence suggests cannibalism among survivors during a severe drought.

In essence, Hirschfeld's theory presents state formation and collapse as cyclical processes driven by groups vying for control over valuable resources, with violence often being the weapon of choice. This perspective underscores that state collapse can lead to intense, gendered violence, disproportionately affecting certain demographics.


The text discusses the concept of "collapse" in historical societies, focusing on population declines often attributed to such events. It challenges the common portrayal of collapse as an abrupt, catastrophic apocalypse and instead presents it more as a process involving significant migration rather than mass deaths.

1. **Population Decline Estimates**: The text presents high-end estimates of population decline during various historical collapses (Table 3). These include the Late Bronze Age collapse (1200-1000 BCE), Mayan lowlands collapse (800-1000 CE), invasion of the Americas (1500-1600), late Roman imperial system collapse (400-600 CE), and Rapa Nui collapse (1722-1877). The percentage of population loss ranges from 30% to over 95%.

2. **Migration vs. Death**: The author argues that these high death tolls are often exaggerations or misleading. Most of the population decline results from people moving away due to conflict, disease, famine, and malnutrition rather than direct deaths. In ancient societies, those who migrated often became less archaeologically visible, their remains and activities becoming smaller and more dispersed in rural areas.

3. **Modern Parallels**: The author uses the Syrian civil war as a modern parallel. Despite the ongoing violence, far more people have been displaced (14 million) than killed (500,000 - 600,000). This pattern holds true for historical collapses, where most population declines reflect migration rather than deaths.

4. **Inaccuracies in Historical Records**: The author points out that estimating historical population sizes and losses is challenging due to poor record-keeping. Most data comes from occasional state censuses, which often underrepresented the total population. This makes it difficult to accurately count lives lost during periods of collapse.

5. **Causes of Collapse**: The author identifies several common causes of historical collapses: conflict, disease, famine, malnutrition, and disrupted trade. War or civil strife is almost universal in these cases, while diseases are a significant factor in eight out of ten reviewed cases. Malnutrition and starvation are also prevalent, appearing in seven out of ten cases.

In summary, the text suggests that historical collapses were not sudden, violent apocalypses but rather complex processes involving mass migration driven by factors like conflict, disease, famine, and disrupted trade. The high death tolls often associated with these events are likely overestimated due to limitations in historical record-keeping and the archaeological visibility of population declines.

6. Military Spending: Historically, war-making was a major budget item for states. The Roman Empire spent half to three-quarters of its budget on military; similarly high proportions were seen in the Song Dynasty, early modern Britain, and France. 

7. Stranger Kings: Conquest often resulted in foreign rulers governing newly formed states. Early kings often claimed superiority, whether through divine connection or special ethnicity, leading to 'stranger-kings' like those of Mesopotamia's Ur, Uruk, and Eridu, which were likely conquered agricultural communities.

8. Enslavement: War captives often became slaves, with some records suggesting up to 36,000 captures in ancient Mesopotamia (though this is likely exaggerated). Slavery was also a result of debt obligations.

10. Attraction to States: While coercion was common, people might also be drawn to states for opportunities in trade, work, larger social networks, religious rituals, and grand monuments. Many early Goliath-building projects began as ceremonial centers uniting communities.

The text underscores that while violence and conquest played significant roles in state formation, participation wasn't always forced; allurements like trade opportunities, social connections, and shared rituals also attracted individuals to these early societies.

Studies show that threat scenarios can synchronize brainwaves, enhancing group cooperation and task completion speed. This heightened coordination is crucial for survival in threatening conditions but may also increase openness to authoritarianism.

8. **Authoritarianism**: Individuals exposed to threats often exhibit increased obedience to high-status figures and a desire to punish rule-breakers - characteristics of authoritarianism. Studies suggest this tendency may be genetic, with around 10-25% of individuals in modern high-income countries scoring highly on authoritarian scales.

In summary, the passage explores the complexities of state formation, highlighting variations in state behavior from extractive rackets to more democratic entities. It underscores how conflict and resource availability shape societal structures, influence brain functioning, and foster both cooperation within groups and authoritarian tendencies among individuals.
