Sargon of Akkad, often hailed as the world's first emperor, isn't exactly that due to the complexities surrounding the definition of an "empire" during his time. The distinction between a state and an empire was not as clearly defined in ancient civilizations as it is today. Here are key points explaining why Sargon's rule can be seen both as a state and an empire:

1. **Territorial Control**: While Sargon did control a vast area, stretching from the Mediterranean Sea to the Persian Gulf, it's essential to understand that ancient conceptions of territory were different from our modern understanding. The Akkadian Empire was not as centralized or bureaucratically organized as later empires like Rome or China. Sargon's control likely varied greatly across his vast domain, with some areas more effectively governed than others.

2. **Political Structure**: Sargon's rule can be seen as an early form of imperialism due to the extent of his territorial expansion and the aggressive methods used to maintain it. However, the political structure of his empire was not as hierarchical or bureaucratic as later empires. Unlike absolute monarchies, Sargon's rule relied heavily on local elites and puppet leaders, which means that his control might have been more loosely held in some regions.

3. **Ideological Basis**: Ancient empires often lacked the ideological underpinnings that later empires used to justify their power. Sargon's rule was not underpinned by an explicit ideology of imperialism or divine right to rule, as seen in later periods. Instead, his claim to power was more about military prowess and divine approval, as depicted on the Victory Stele.

4. **Administrative Capabilities**: The administrative capabilities of ancient empires were limited compared to their successors. Communication technologies were primitive, and long-distance travel was arduous. Sargon's ability to govern his vast territory effectively would have been much more challenging than that of later emperors who could leverage written records, roads, and faster transportation methods.

5. **Cultural Homogeneity**: Unlike many modern or historical empires, the Akkadian Empire did not aim to impose a single cultural identity across its vast territory. Instead, it allowed local customs and religions to persist, which contrasts with the assimilationist policies seen in later empires.

In summary, while Sargon's rule extended over a significant area and demonstrated imperial ambition through conquest and tribute extraction, the lack of sophisticated bureaucratic structures, centralized control, explicit ideological justifications, advanced communication technologies, and cultural homogenization policies places his rule on the blurry line between state and empire. It was more akin to early forms of imperialism than to later, highly developed imperial systems.


The passage discusses the concept of empires, their characteristics, and the blurred lines between states and empires. It asserts that all states can be considered forms of empire due to their inherent power dynamics of domination and resource extraction, albeit on varying scales. 

Empires, according to the text, are systems where one group exerts control over others, either through direct conquest (formal) or by controlling resources (informal). This dominance often involves violence or its threat, ensuring unequal exchange. The Akkadian Empire, for instance, conquered cities, installed puppet rulers, and demanded tribute. In contrast, modern entities like the United States are suggested to exert informal control through economic strategies such as 'unfair free-trade' agreements.

The passage also highlights how the distinction between hierarchical societies (like states) and empires can be blurry. Examples include the Calusa, who violently extracted resources from neighboring communities; early city-states with a dominant core ruling over a larger hinterland; Rome, which expanded through conquest; and the British Empire, which started with domestic conquests before moving onto colonies abroad. Despite differences in size and complexity, these societies share a fundamental characteristic of unequal resource exchange and control.

Scholars often struggle to define empires precisely because the line between states and empires is fine, being more about degree than kind. The text suggests that calling states 'empires' might be a simplification, but the difference is minor. 

The passage then transitions to discussing the Early Bronze Age collapse (c. 2200-2000 BCE), a period when various civilizations across the Mediterranean and Middle East experienced transformations or collapses. These include the Akkadian Empire, Old Egyptian Kingdom, Indus Valley Civilization, and Malta's Temple Period. 

The most commonly proposed cause of this collapse is severe climate change, specifically a 'megadrought' affecting regions from the Nile to Spain. This drought correlates with urban shrinkage and settlement abandonment. However, establishing direct causation is challenging due to regional climatic variations, with some areas experiencing sudden droughts while others saw gradual changes.

Despite the environmental pressures, these collapses didn't mean the end for these populations. Instead, they led to decentralization of power - emperors lost central authority, and local leaders or assemblies gained influence. This disaggregation could be seen as precursors to future imperial structures. For instance, in Syria's Euphrates Valley, popular assemblies and councils of elders became more prominent post-collapse. Similarly, Egypt saw democratization of both religion and politics following its decentralization. 

The Early Bronze Age collapse serves as a useful historical analogy for understanding potential modern collapse scenarios, given the interconnectedness of our globalized world today.


The text discusses various ancient civilizations and their economic systems, with a focus on the Bronze Age (approximately 3000-1200 BCE). 

In Egypt's First Dynasty, there was a rigid class structure, centralized government, and ritualistic royal funerals involving human sacrifice. After a collapse period marked by increased access to religion and material goods, a new pharaoh regained control. The Middle Kingdom that followed saw a blurring of class boundaries, with rulers presenting themselves as modest leaders rather than divine beings.

In Mesopotamia, empire collapses like Sargon's Akkad or the Babylonian Empire often resulted in a return to autonomous city-states. These local governments handled day-to-day affairs, which was not necessarily viewed negatively as these empires were known for their militaristic and predatory nature.

The Indus Valley Civilization experienced a slow transformation rather than a sudden collapse. Over centuries, people shifted from urban living to village-based economies, growing more resilient crops like millet and reducing reliance on long-distance trade, including with Mesopotamia.

Despite common narratives of ancient apocalypses, these shifts were more nuanced - systems collapsing but not necessarily leading to catastrophic outcomes for all. 

Moving into the Bronze Age, a complex network emerged in the eastern Mediterranean, south-west Asia, and Aegean. This included powerful states like Mycenaean Greece, Minoan Crete, Hittite Anatolia, Canaanites of the Levant, Egypt, Mitanni and Assyria in Mesopotamia, and Kassites of Babylon. 

Notably, elements of capitalism began to appear during this era. In Assyria around 4000-3000 BCE, there's evidence of privately-owned credit systems, 'joint-stock' agreements, interest-bearing debts, and a merchant class with significant autonomy. These were not state-run enterprises but rather privately governed by elite merchant families.

The Assyrian city of Kanesh served as a hub for trade, hosting multinational corporations with characteristics similar to modern ones - common stock ownership, hierarchical structures, profit motives, and international operations. 

States in this network were interconnected through trade. Each relied heavily on exchanging goods with others; for example, Egypt provided gold, Greece silver, Cyprus copper, and Afghanistan tin - crucial for making bronze. This extensive trade network was vital to the power and wealth of these Bronze Age civilizations.

Though the text doesn't explicitly label this period as 'capitalism' using academic definitions, it highlights several key elements such as private property, markets with responsive prices, enforceable contracts, wage labor, and state support for commercial activities - all hallmarks discussed in modern capitalist theory. 

This Bronze Age network laid the groundwork for many aspects of what we now recognize as 'Western' civilization, including elements of Greek culture, monotheistic religion (like Atenism in Egypt), and rudimentary forms of corporate organization and international trade.


The Late Bronze Age (1550-1200 BCE) was characterized by a complex trade network controlled by urban aristocrats who amassed wealth through conquest, forced labor, and taxation of farmers and client states. This system led to significant inequality, as evidenced by the height disparity between the elite and commoners. Pharaohs and high-status individuals were taller due to better nutrition and medical care, while peasants suffered from diseases like tuberculosis and hernias resulting from heavy labor.

This system began to unravel around 1200 BCE, leading to the collapse of many city-states and civilizations in the Eastern Mediterranean and Near East. The collapse was likely triggered by a combination of factors:

1. **Drought**: A severe drought affecting regions from northern Italy to Iran and Iraq lasted 150-300 years, causing crop yield shortages, famine, and increased grain prices. This disproportionately affected areas reliant on agriculture, such as Egypt.

2. **Earthquakes**: The region is seismically active, with major fault lines running through it. A series of powerful earthquakes (potentially 'earthquake storms') struck Mycenaean Greece, Troy, and settlements across Canaan, contributing to the collapse but not solely accounting for it, as evidence suggests rebuilding occurred post-1250 BCE in some areas.

3. **Pestilence**: While there's limited archaeological evidence, historical records indicate possible epidemics. For instance, Pharaoh Ramesses V died of smallpox, and the Hittite royal family was wiped out by an epidemic around 1350 BCE.

4. **Human Threats**: The most notorious are the 'Sea Peoples', purported invaders from the Aegean Sea who ravaged cities across the Mediterranean. This narrative originates from Egyptian inscriptions by Pharaohs Merneptah and Ramesses III, describing battles against these "Sea Peoples" in 1207 and 1177 BCE. However, this account is questionable due to lack of supporting evidence; many cities claimed destroyed weren't, and in some cases, destruction occurred before the Sea Peoples' arrival. More likely, internal conflicts and rebellions were the primary drivers of collapse rather than large-scale foreign invasions.

This multifaceted crisis led to the abandonment or burning of numerous cities, loss of writing systems (like Ugaritic and Linear B scripts), and societal disintegration within a few generations. The exact sequence and relative importance of these factors continue to be subjects of ongoing research and debate among historians and archaeologists.


The Late Bronze Age Collapse (ca. 1200-1150 BCE) was a complex, multifaceted event that resulted from a combination of various stressors, rather than being caused by any single factor. This collapse is often portrayed in a double standard: while elite activities like trade or warfare were seen as signs of cultural vitality, commoner migrations were often depicted mysteriously and apocalyptically.

1. **Drought, Famine, Earthquakes, Disease**: These natural disasters significantly contributed to the crisis. Drought led to famine, weakening populations and making them more susceptible to disease outbreaks.

2. **Inequality and Conquest Empires**: The rise of inequality and the establishment of conquest empires were unstable foundations for societal structures. Subjugated peoples actively resisted their overlords, leading to rebellions and undermining of imperial rule.

3. **Revolts and Interstate Wars**: Rising inequality and imperial conquest sparked numerous rebellions and interstate wars. These conflicts resulted in the destruction of cities, including vital trading hubs, which disrupted the trade networks crucial to the Bronze Age system.

4. **Resource Scarcity**: The destruction of cities and trade routes led to a scarcity of essential resources like bronze (due to disrupted mining and metalworking), gold from Egypt, silver from Greece, and tin and copper from Afghanistan. This scarcity fueled further conflict as societies struggled to maintain their economic stability.

5. **Earthquake Storm**: There was a series of earthquakes during this period, which added to the instability. Piracy might have also increased due to the collapse, making trade even more dangerous and unsettling the Bronze Age network further.

The interconnectedness of the Bronze Age civilizations, while beneficial in times of prosperity, became a weakness during crises. The loss of several key cities or empires could destabilize the entire network, leading to a 'death spiral' where each stressor worsened the situation.

The collapse wasn't uniform across regions; different polities experienced varied outcomes:

- **Hittite Empire**: It fell apart, leaving only small cities and kingdoms in southeastern Anatolia and northern Syria. Its capital, Hattusa, was abandoned and later destroyed.
  
- **Egypt**: Despite breaking into rival factions, its culture and population remained relatively stable. The collapse led to a reduction in taxes and corvée labor for most subjects in rural areas.

- **Assyria**: Described as a 'recession', Assyria showed resilience, although inequality grew over time due to increased reliance on migrant labor and demands for taxation.

- **Mycenaean Greece**: Cities were completely leveled, and trading hubs like Ugarit were burned down. The collapse was severe but unevenly distributed across the region.

The evidence suggests that, counterintuitively, the collapse might have benefited the majority of people living in rural areas (95% of the population). Inequality estimates based on housing sizes indicate that rural regions became more equitable post-collapse. However, urban settlements continued to experience growing disparities into the Iron Age due to the empires' inability to extract wealth from the countryside effectively.

In contrast, areas under empires that experienced little change during the collapse, like Assyria, saw increased inequality. The rich might have become richer, while the poor became poorer due to heightened demands for labor and taxation.

Overall, the Late Bronze Age Collapse was a complex event with diverse impacts across different regions and societal strata. It's essential to view it not as a singular catastrophe but as a series of interconnected crises leading to varied outcomes for different communities.


The text discusses the Late Bronze Age collapse (circa 1200-1150 BCE) and its aftermath, challenging the traditional narrative of a catastrophic, uniform decline leading to a "Dark Age." Instead, it proposes that this period, particularly in regions like Greece and the Near East, was more complex.

1. **Collapse as Elitist Phenomenon**: The collapse was largely an elite phenomenon driven by inequality. Palaces and their rulers were central to the conflicts and downfalls. In contrast, peasant farmers might not have been deeply affected by the loss of overseas trade or distant rulers, focusing instead on their daily lives and agricultural activities.

2. **Reinterpretation of Collapse**: Rather than viewing it as a civilizational collapse, some experts suggest seeing it as the failure of specific large imperial polities due to internal weaknesses and corruption. This perspective allows for the possibility that repressed groups and communities could reorganize and flourish post-collapse.

3. **Benefits of Collapse**: The Late Bronze Age collapse led to various positive outcomes:

   - **Cultural Flourishing**: It provided an environment for the development of epic literature, such as Homer's "Odyssey" and "Iliad," which might not have emerged without such a transformative event.
   
   - **Reduced Inequality**: The collapse leveled wealth inequality, setting the stage for more democratic arrangements. In ancient Greece, this led to initial law codes limiting individual power and eventually to collective decision-making via mass assemblies – foundations for Athenian democracy.
   
   - **Technological Advancements**: Key technological breakthroughs like the Phoenician alphabet occurred during or after this period. This new alphabet, simpler than previous scripts, spread widely, influencing many languages, including English, French, and Spanish.

4. **Bronze Age Collapse vs. Dark Age Narrative**: The term "Dark Age" is outdated among historians and archaeologists as it misrepresents the period following collapse. Instead of gloom, this era brought healthier, wealthier people in many regions, new technology, and democratic models.

5. **Comparative Analysis with Yucatan Collapse**: The text also briefly touches upon a similar phenomenon in the Mayan civilization of the Yucatan Peninsula. Here, drought, warfare, and societal unrest led to city-states' decline. This parallel suggests that systems collapses are not unique to specific historical contexts but can occur across different civilizations worldwide.

In conclusion, the Late Bronze Age collapse and subsequent "Dark Age" were more nuanced than previously thought. They brought about significant societal changes, including cultural advancements, reduced inequality, and democratic innovations, rather than uniform decline. This perspective offers a more balanced view of historical collapses, highlighting the potential for growth amidst adversity.


The Classic Lowland Maya civilization flourished from 150-900 CE across a vast territory spanning present-day Guatemala, Belize, Mexico, Honduras, and El Salvador. This period saw the development of complex urban societies characterized by grand plazas, stone ball courts, and towering pyramid temples. The Maya were renowned for their use of obsidian, a volcanic glass analogous to bronze, which was used in weapons, jewelry, and tools, primarily by the elite.

Their culture included an intricate cosmic calendar, rituals honoring royal ancestors, and a glyphic writing system for memorializing deceased kings' deeds. Unlike a singular empire, this civilization was composed of around sixty to seventy city-states, each with its unique size and power but sharing a common culture. Some cities were colossal, accommodating up to 120,000 inhabitants, making them larger than most European cities at the time.

The Lowlands were essentially a densely populated urban network, as evidenced by a laser scan of Guatemala revealing over 60,000 ancient structures per square kilometer and extensive canal systems. Despite their sophistication, these city-states existed in a volatile political environment, frequently raiding and conquering each other. This led to cycles of expansion and contraction, with power shifting between different kingdoms, such as Kaanul, Calakmul, Tikal, Copán, Quiriguá, and others.

The Terminal Classic Period (800-1100 CE) marked a dramatic collapse of the southern Lowland Maya civilization. This decline saw multiple cities depopulated, with some losing up to 90% of their residents. Monument production ceased, luxury goods creation plummeted, and the long-count calendar was abandoned.

The collapse was likely triggered by a combination of factors, primarily a severe drought that affected corn, bean, and squash cultivation – staple crops vulnerable to reduced rainfall. The unpredictability and volatility of rainfall during 700-800 CE exacerbated the situation, making it challenging for cities to sustain their populations.

However, the drought alone may not fully explain the catastrophic collapse. Simultaneously, evidence indicates widespread warfare and violence during this period. Many cities showed signs of massacres, with elite rulers and nobles often targeted. Some cities, like Cancuén, were attacked abruptly, resulting in the slaughter of the ruling class and destruction of their monuments and regalia.

The drought's impact varied across the region; some areas experienced it more severely than others. For instance, Peten collapsed before any detectable drought occurred, while Tikal and Cancuén were abandoned amidst violent upheaval. Despite these challenges, not all settlements were abandoned, and some western regions showed little environmental stress but high levels of warfare.

In summary, the collapse of the southern Lowland Maya civilization was a multifaceted phenomenon involving severe drought, erratic rainfall, and increased warfare. These factors likely interacted in complex ways to destabilize the social fabric, leading to depopulation and the decline of once-thriving city-states. The precise interplay between these elements remains an active area of archaeological research and debate.


The collapse of the Lowland Maya civilization, which occurred around 800-950 AD, was a complex phenomenon influenced by multiple factors rather than being solely due to environmental degradation. Here's a detailed explanation:

1. **Environmental Degradation**: Increased soil erosion in lakes and fewer animal bones indicate significant deforestation and soil depletion, likely exacerbated by droughts. This led to a decline in the energy return on investment (EROI), meaning more energy was needed to extract less yield from the land.

2. **Drought**: While drought played a role in triggering societal breakdown in some cities, its impact varied. Cities that were already fragile due to environmental degradation suffered more, while robust ones could withstand it better.

3. **Economic Factors and Trade**: A shift in trade dynamics was a crucial factor in the collapse. As competitors on the coast thrived, people from the interior Lowlands moved to these regions for better economic prospects. This migration reduced the population in the southern Lowlands, despite post-drought environmental recovery evident in abundant wildlife remains.

4. **Inequality**: Wealth disparities increased throughout the Classical Period and worsened during the Terminal Period. The collapse led to a decrease in this inequality as elite fortunes were destroyed. Kingdoms with higher levels of inequality, reflecting extractive institutions, were more susceptible to collapse.

5. **War and Conflict**: With reduced trade opportunities due to drought, Mayan kingdoms turned to warfare instead. This resulted in further loss of life and displacement of people.

6. **Resilience and Continuity**: Despite the collapse, not all Maya disappeared. Northern settlements grew, and new cities emerged, indicating resilience. The story of the Maya after this period seems to involve a shift towards more commercial, seafaring societies in the east, west, and north, with Chichén Itzá becoming a dominant center.

In summary, the collapse of the Lowland Maya was multifaceted, involving environmental degradation, drought, economic shifts, social inequality, warfare, and migration. The societies' increasing reliance on trade and their extractive institutions made them vulnerable to these challenges. Yet, the Maya civilization didn't vanish; it evolved and persisted in different forms across various regions.


The Mayan civilization, which flourished from around 2000 BCE to the arrival of the Spanish in the 16th century CE, experienced significant shifts and collapses throughout its history. After the decline of Tikal around 900 CE, Chichén Itzá emerged as a major power from approximately 800-1200 CE during the Classic period. This city saw a population peak of around 50,000 inhabitants and was known for its impressive architecture and advanced knowledge in fields such as astronomy and mathematics.

However, Chichén Itzá's dominance waned, and power shifted to the city-state of Mayapan in the Lowlands around 1200 CE. During the Postclassic period (900-1519 CE), Mayapan grew to a population of 15,000-20,000 people but eventually collapsed decades before European contact.

The collapse of Mayapan is believed to be a result of drought, inequality, and elite infighting. Archaeological evidence indicates periods of "terror and war," noble family persecutions, and population decline leading up to the city's abandonment after 1450 CE. Drought, which significantly impacted rain-fed agriculture, was likely exploited by competing elites to sow discontent among the populace.

The Mayan civilization also showcases resilience and resistance despite collapses. Upon Spanish arrival in the 16th century, they discovered a prosperous people engaging in trade across a vast network of smaller towns and settlements using various currencies like cacao beans, shells, stone beads, copper, and cotton. The Maya continued writing in their glyphic script, living in cities, and warring with each other, although on a smaller scale than before the collapse.

The loss of writing systems is a recurring theme in civilizational collapses. For instance, both the Mayan and Mycenaean Greek civilizations lost their scripts following collapses. Similar instances include the Indus Valley Civilization script and Linear A used by the Minoans on Crete. Writing has historically been linked to power and state control, making it vulnerable during periods of political instability.

In the case of the Maya, their glyphic script survived initial collapses but was eventually exterminated by Spanish colonizers who banned its use, burned books, enforced Spanish language adoption, and persecuted, tortured, and killed Mayan writers. Despite this, some Maya continued to write in Yucatec using the Spanish script and preserved traditional texts like Chilam Balam books.

Writing was historically practiced by small elite classes, making it a fragile institution during times of power shifts. The exclusivity of writing systems limited their resilience; thus, they were more easily eradicated when targeted by incoming conquerors or as state support waned, as seen in Egypt with the transition from hieroglyphs to Greek scripts under Roman rule.


The text discusses two ancient African empires - the Aksumite Empire (c.150-900 CE) and the Ghana Empire (around 300-1203 CE) - and their respective scripts, Ge'ez and Old Mande, which were widely used but eventually fell into obscurity following the collapse of these empires.

The Aksumite Empire was a trading power based in present-day Ethiopia and Eritrea, with its two key cities being Adulis and Axum. It thrived due to strategic geographical positioning on the Red Sea, enabling control over trade routes for luxury goods like gold, ivory, and incense. The empire's prosperity was initially supported by a wetter climate that improved farming conditions but later suffered from soil erosion caused by intensive farming and loss of trade networks due to conflicts with the Byzantine and Persian Empires, as well as the Umayyad Caliphate's destruction of Aksumite ports. This led to a decrease in the empire's 'energy ratio' (a metaphorical term for productivity), ultimately causing its decline and the obsolescence of the Ge'ez script.

The Ghana Empire, located in present-day Mauritania and Mali, emerged as a dominant force following the Sahara's desiccation, pushing human populations to its edges. Camels introduced in the 3rd century allowed for more effective Saharan trade networks, facilitating the export of gold, salt, and slaves. The widespread use of iron weapons enabled a large military force. Despite its initial power, the Ghana Empire fragmented due to unclear reasons, leading to the rise of the Mali Empire (c.1230-1650 CE).

The Mali Empire, under the reign of Mansa Musa, reached its peak in wealth and size, covering over 1 million square kilometers. Its decline began after Musa's rule due to a series of succession crises and short-lived leaders. 

In both cases, linguistic and cultural shifts were collateral damage of these empires' collapses. Ge'ez became a liturgical language used primarily in Ethiopian churches, while Old Mande evolved into the Manding languages spoken today. These examples illustrate how collapse can rearrange power structures and lead to the obsolescence of scripts, writing systems, and languages.


The text discusses the rise and fall of several ancient empires across different regions - the West African kingdoms (Ghana, Mali, Songhai), the Mayan civilization, the Hittite Empire, the Phoenicians, and the Aksumite Empire. It highlights common patterns in their development and collapse, suggesting parallels between seemingly disparate civilizations separated by vast distances.

1. **Interdependence and Status Competition**: Both the Mayan cities and West African kingdoms learned that interdependence and status competition could be detrimental to their survival. In both cases, these societies were connected in complex networks, which made them vulnerable to systemic collapse when disrupted.

2. **Imperial Cycle**: The Mayan civilization and West African kingdoms followed a similar pattern of one city or kingdom rising to power, only to be eclipsed by another, leading to a cycle of capture and fracture. This is seen in the succession of Ghana, Mali, and Songhai empires in West Africa, each grappling with internal strife and eventual decline due to factors like succession disputes, civil wars, and loss of trade routes.

3. **Decentralization**: Some civilizations, like the Lowland Maya, were highly decentralized, preventing any single city or kingdom from establishing a lasting large-scale empire. On the other hand, others like the Bronze Age systems had larger empires connected in an extractive quilt, while West Africa saw a sequence of three major empires, each struggling to maintain control over its constituent parts.

4. **Trade Dependence**: Many of these empires were heavily reliant on trade for their prosperity and survival. The decline in trade routes or the emergence of new ones (as seen with the Portuguese diverting gold trade from West Africa to Europe) could lead to economic instability and, eventually, collapse.

5. **Succession Disputes**: Internal strife, particularly succession disputes, weakened many of these empires. In West Africa, the practice of polygyny by rulers resulted in numerous children, complicating succession rules and leading to frequent power struggles.

6. **Variation in Collapse**: The text emphasizes that system collapses were not uniform across time and space. Some cities or empires might collapse quickly (in months), while others could take centuries. The people living through these periods often perceived discrete disasters rather than a slow, generational collapse.

7. **Reversion to Earlier Forms of Organization**: After the collapse of large-scale empires, many societies reverted to more egalitarian city-states or rural living. This was observed in Classical Greece, Phoenicia after the Late Bronze Age collapse, and among some Mayan polities shifting towards democratic seafaring communities in response to drought and oppression.

8. **Failed Experiments**: The text suggests that large-scale empires were a relatively recent development in human history, particularly in regions like the Mediterranean and West Africa. When these systems collapsed, societies often returned to earlier forms of organization, which sometimes proved more resilient and innovative in the face of environmental or societal changes.

This overview illustrates that despite geographical and cultural differences, ancient civilizations shared common challenges related to governance, trade, and societal structure, leading to patterns of rise and fall that echo across continents and millennia.


The text discusses the early history of Chinese imperial dynasties, focusing on the Xia, Shang, Zhou, Warring States, and Qin periods.

1. **Xia Dynasty (circa 2200-1850 BCE)**: The Xia is believed to be the first Chinese dynasty, established near the Yellow River. Little is known about its end, but it's said that a tyrannical ruling family was overthrown by new leaders: the Shang Dynasty.

2. **Shang Dynasty (1600-1046 BCE)**: The Shang are known for their bronze weaponry and the invention of Asia's first writing system, oracle bone script, used for divination. Despite their prophetic tools, they couldn't predict their own downfall, which came at the hands of the Zhou Dynasty.

3. **Zhou Dynasty (1046-256 BCE)**: The Zhou introduced the "Mandate of Heaven," a concept suggesting that rulers were divinely sanctioned and could be deposed if they failed to govern effectively. Over time, feudal lords gained autonomy, leading to the Warring States period when China fell into perpetual warfare.

4. **Warring States Period (481-221 BCE)**: This was a violent era marked by constant conflict among various states as they vied for power and territory. The eventual winners, the Qin, employed brutal tactics such as mass killings to consolidate their rule.

5. **Qin Dynasty (221-206 BCE)**: The Qin unified northern China, establishing the first large-scale empire. They centralized authority by imposing legalism as the dominant philosophy, standardizing measurements and currency, constructing an extensive road network for military purposes, and initiating work on what would become the Great Wall.

Throughout this period, we see how elite networks facilitated trade, diplomacy, and power consolidation but also amplified shocks and instability. The rise of writing systems enabled record-keeping and ideological justification for political changes. The Warring States period was a hotbed of philosophical innovation, which the Qin suppressed to enforce their rule. These cycles of rise, collapse, and reformation are common themes in ancient civilizations, highlighting the complex interplay between technological advancements, political structures, and societal resilience or vulnerability.


The text discusses the political history of imperial China, focusing on the themes of unity, collapse, and the role of elite competition. It begins with the Qin Dynasty (221-206 BCE), China's first unified empire, which was established rapidly but fell just as quickly due to widespread rebellion and conflict among elites and commoners.

Despite its short rule, the Qin's legacy of a centralized, divine empire persisted. The subsequent Han Dynasty (206 BCE - 220 CE), often considered a golden age for China, oversaw significant inventions like paper and the sundial, and is reflected in modern Chinese self-identification as "Han Chinese" or use of "Han characters". However, this prosperity was underpinned by extreme social inequality. Wealthy families of officials, landlords, and merchants amassed vast riches, often evading taxes through private armies, leading to peasant rebellions such as the Yellow Turban Rebellion (184-205 CE).

The text then introduces a key concept: the cyclical nature of Chinese imperial rule. Following the Han and Jin dynasties, China experienced five periods of unified empire—Tang (618-907 CE), Song (960-1276 CE), Yuan (1276-1368 CE), Ming (1368-1644 CE), and Qing (1644-1912 CE). Interruptions, like the Five Dynasties and Ten Kingdoms Period (907-960 CE) and the Jin Dynasty (1115-1234 CE), were relatively brief. 

A significant observation is that while dynasties could collapse due to warlords, corruption, or popular uprisings, China's imperial system itself never truly collapsed; bureaucracy, written language, and government structures persisted through these transitions. This is in contrast to societal collapses observed elsewhere, where such systems might be entirely lost.

The text also delves into the paradox of Chinese emperors' longevity in weak regimes. Despite the Tang Dynasty's renowned prosperity and cultural influence, its later emperors had a 50% chance of being assassinated due to elite competition. Conversely, long-ruling emperors like Kangxi of the Qing Dynasty presided over weak states. This pattern is attributed to what Yuhua Wang calls "the sovereign's dilemma": a powerful, cohesive elite can bolster the state but also threaten it. 

China faced persistent threats from steppe warriors, necessitating a unified elite for defense. However, this unity could lead to internal conflicts and government erosion via corruption, tax evasion, or coups. For instance, Emperor Shenzong of the Northern Song (1067-1085 CE) maintained elite divisions to avoid a coup, weakening his military against steppe raids that eventually led to the Jin Dynasty's establishment.

Popular uprisings were common throughout Chinese history, from the Yellow Turban Rebellion to the Taiping Rebellion (1850-64 CE), which is recognized as the deadliest civil war in human history. Climate change often exacerbated these situations, triggering rebellions and contributing to dynastic downfalls, such as during the Qing Dynasty (1644-1912 CE).

In essence, the text illustrates how China's imperial cycles were shaped by a delicate balance of elite competition, external threats, popular unrest, and climate factors. Despite periods of collapse or weakening, the resilience of its imperial system allowed for continuous regeneration, making Chinese history unique in its longevity and continuity despite frequent political upheaval.


The text discusses the complex interplay of climate, elite competition, and corruption in the rise and fall of Chinese dynasties, specifically focusing on the Qing Dynasty. 

1. **Climate and Natural Disasters**: The Qing Dynasty experienced numerous droughts and floods in the Yellow River region, which, according to historical records and modern studies, often preceded significant upheavals like the Boxer Rebellion. These natural disasters not only caused immediate hardships but also undermined the legitimacy of the ruling Mandate of Heaven, leading to rebellions and potential dynastic collapse. This pattern is supported by studies correlating Chinese warfare with periods of cooling caused by volcanic eruptions over a millennium. 

2. **Elite Competition**: The text highlights that while climate played a role, it was not the sole determinant in dynastic cycles. Elite competition and corruption were equally, if not more, significant factors. As the number of elites (wealthy merchants, bureaucrats, generals, religious leaders) increased, so did status-seeking behavior and power struggles. This infighting could escalate as high-status positions became scarce relative to those seeking them. The text cites examples from Chinese history (like the Han Dynasty where merchants sought political power) and compares it with historical collapses elsewhere, such as the Western Roman Empire and Lenin's rise in Russia.

3. **Cooperation Among Elites**: The text also notes that while elite competition was prevalent, cooperation was not absent. Chinese princes often united to depose an unwanted emperor before resuming their rivalries. This pattern is mirrored in modern times with businesses competing commercially yet collaborating politically to thwart regulatory threats.

4. **Structural-Demographic Theory**: The authors reference the Structural-Demographic Theory, which posits that increasing inequality, popular immiseration, and elite competition contribute significantly to societal collapses across different historical contexts. 

5. **Status as a Motivator**: Finally, the text underscores the role of status as a driving force behind elite behavior. Public loss of status can intensify competition among elites, as seen in modern examples like Donald Trump and Tucker Carlson.

The passage discusses the persistent nature of large-scale empires (referred to as "Goliaths") across various civilizations, particularly in China. It argues that while there are instances where Goliaths fail or never emerge, history shows a consistent trend of their resurgence due to several factors:

1. **Lootable Resources**: These are resources that can be exploited by a centralized power, such as fertile land for farming or valuable minerals. The passage suggests that once these resources are harnessed and utilized, they persist even after the collapse of an empire. For instance, the Kwakwaka'wakw people in North America maintained a hierarchical society due to their access to abundant salmon.

2. **Weapons**: The longer weapons are in use within a population and the more widespread knowledge of their creation becomes, the harder it is to eliminate them. This is because the collective memory retains information about weapon-making, making it difficult for new, alternative weapons to replace established ones.

3. **Ideas and Culture**: Legitimized hierarchies become deeply ingrained in cultures over time. Even after a collapse, these ideas can be resurrected by future leaders. For example, the Mandate of Heaven, a concept that justified imperial rule in China, remained relevant for millennia despite various dynastic changes. Similarly, genetic evidence suggests that India's caste system, established around 1500 years ago, has persisted with minimal intermarriage.

4. **Geography and Population Dynamics**: Geographical features and population density play significant roles in the persistence of Goliaths. Mountainous regions like Zomia or areas with low population density and ample exit options (like North America) are less likely to develop centralized empires because they offer easier alternatives to hierarchy, such as foraging or migration. In contrast, landscapes that become dependent on intensive agriculture over generations tend to favor hierarchical structures due to the difficulty of transitioning back to alternative lifestyles.

The passage also highlights how China's unique historical circumstances facilitated the persistence of its empires. The constant threat of invasion from steppe nomads spurred the development of a strong bureaucracy and written language, which served as foundations for regenerating imperial structures after collapses. This was aided by the geographical proximity to the steppe, which necessitated centralized authority for effective defense.

In summary, the text posits that the resilience of large-scale empires is driven by factors such as the availability of exploitable resources, widespread knowledge of powerful weapons, deeply ingrained cultural norms justifying hierarchy, and geographical conditions favoring centralized rule. China's history exemplifies these principles, with its bureaucracy, standardized language, and historical records enabling the easy regeneration of empires following collapses.


The text discusses two empires - Chinese and Roman - and their rise, decline, and impact on their respective regions, using the term "Goliath" to refer to these powerful entities. 

**Chinese Goliath:**

1. **Formation:** China, being relatively flat with few natural barriers, was vulnerable to Steppe Raiders from the north. In response, China developed a hierarchical system and eventually formed its own empires, including during the Yuan Dynasty when it was ruled by Mongol conquerors.

2. **Expansion:** The geography of China - crisscrossed by rivers supporting large-scale farming and facilitating trade - enabled easy expansion. However, this also made it easier for Goliaths to dominate without significant natural obstacles providing refuge for resistance.

3. **Legacy:** The Chinese Goliath left a deep imprint on its landscape and people, comparable only to the Roman Goliath in historical impact.

**Roman Goliath:**

1. **Origin & Rise:** Rome started as one of many city-states in the Mediterranean, founded by legendary twin brothers Romulus and Remus, sons of Mars, the god of war. The early Romans were warriors who gradually transitioned from raiding to settling down in city-states.

2. **Expansion Strategy:** Rome grew by conquering neighboring city-states or forming alliances that prohibited communication among the allies. Conquered peoples became Roman citizens but had to pay taxes, funding further military campaigns. 

3. **Military and Economy:** Rome maintained a large standing army (10-25% of men above 17 in the Republic), which was funded by low taxes for elites and minimal interference in local governance. Conquered territories provided resources like gold, silver, slaves, and food, enriching Roman citizens and placating allies.

4. **Decline (The Three Falls of Rome):**
   - **Imperial Overstretch:** The empire expanded too vastly, becoming unsustainable to govern effectively. 
   - **Diminishing Returns on Extraction:** As the empire grew, it extracted fewer benefits from each new territory due to increasing administrative costs and resistance from conquered peoples.
   - **Learning from the Oldest Empire:** Rome's aggressive expansion and reliance on conquest set a pattern that proved unsustainable in the long term.

5. **Legacy:** Despite its eventual collapse, Roman influence has been enduring, shaping Western civilization in numerous ways, including law, architecture, language, and governance structures. 

The text highlights how both empires - through their vast reach, military might, and exploitation of resources - left profound marks on the lands and cultures they dominated. Despite their different geographical contexts, China's and Rome's histories share striking parallels in their rise to power, internal dynamics, and eventual decline.


The Roman Republic, established in 509 BCE, was ostensibly a democratic system with assemblies and voting for citizens. However, it was essentially an oligarchy controlled by a few elite families who dominated the Senate and magistracy positions. As wealth disparity grew, so did military power, ultimately leading to Julius Caesar's coup and the transition from Republic to Empire in 27 BCE.

Despite its victories, Rome faced constant threats: Germanic tribes to the north, the Parthian and Sassanid empires to the east, and recurring challenges from city-states like Carthage. The Roman Empire, despite its military successes, was marked by stark economic inequality. 

Rome's ruling class consisted primarily of a land-owning aristocracy, with the Senate (300-600 members from patrician families) and provincial governors wielding significant power. This elite class often acted autonomously due to minimal oversight and tax collection, leading to rampant corruption, rule-breaking, and tax evasion. 

The Roman aristocracy's exploitation of commoners was systematic. They imposed heavy taxes, sold peasants into slavery, and shielded them from conscription. This economic lopsidedness resulted in social unrest; Jewish revolts, uprisings in Gaul and Spain, and slave rebellions were common. 

By the peak of the Empire, around a third of its economic output was controlled by just 1.5% of households. Despite grand public works like villas, bathhouses, and aqueducts, most Romans lived near subsistence level. The Roman economy relied heavily on conquest revenues for sustenance, which became insufficient over time due to increasing military expenditures, dwindling silver mines, and depleted currency value through debasement. 

This fiscal crisis sparked the 'Third Century Crisis' (235-284 CE), a period of almost fifty years of civil war as disgruntled armies turned against the empire for better pay and plunder. Diocletian's reforms, including bureaucratic expansion and tax increases, temporarily stabilized the situation but failed to address underlying inequality issues. 

As inequality persisted, farmers abandoned land due to unfavorable taxation, leading to a dependence on Germanic mercenaries for military service. The final blow came in 476 CE when these mercenaries, unable to be paid, deposed the last Roman emperor, symbolizing the fall of the Western Roman Empire. This downfall was due to a combination of economic lopsidedness, over-taxation, and an unsustainable reliance on external forces for military strength.


The text discusses the fall of the Western Roman Empire and the concept of "imperial overstretch," using Rome's history as a primary example.

By the fifth century, urban centers were being abandoned, leading to significant depopulation across the empire.

The Eastern Roman (Byzantine) Empire continued for centuries but eventually fell in 1453 to the Ottomans. In the West, local Germanic leaders often merged with Roman ruling classes, integrating their cultures.

Despite Rome's initial self-perception as a defender of liberty and justice, its expansion led to significant costs. Rome grew from a small city-state (around 60,000 people in 500 BCE) to an empire covering approximately 5 million square kilometers – a scale of growth comparable to a medium-sized American city conquering the entire USA. This expansion provided benefits like neutralizing threats, generating revenue, and offering plunder but also entailed high maintenance costs and the creation of new enemies along the borders.

6. **Diminishing Returns on Extraction**: The text then introduces the concept of "diminishing returns on extraction," a pattern seen across different civilizations. As societies grow, they become more vulnerable to external threats like invasions, diseases, and droughts. The larger and more complex these societies become, the harder they are hit by such crises. 

In summary, the text provides a historical analysis of the fall of the Western Roman Empire, highlighting factors like hoarding leading to famine, climate change and disease, political instability, cultural shifts, and the consequences of imperial overstretch – the point where the costs of maintaining an empire exceed its benefits. It also introduces broader themes such as "diminishing returns on extraction," suggesting that larger, more complex societies can be more vulnerable to crises due to their extensive infrastructure and interdependence.


The passage discusses a concept called "Goliath's Curse," which refers to the challenges faced by large-scale, centralized societies (referred to as 'Goliaths') over time. The first iteration of this curse, known as the Primal Curse, was characterized by incompetence and resistance to hierarchy, leading to difficulties in controlling populations, particularly when states had limited capacity. As societies grew larger and more organized, they developed administrative systems, innovations, and new sources of power (referred to as 'Goliath fuel') to manage these challenges.

Despite these advancements, the core issue remained: rising inequality leading to concentrated power and extractive institutions. This inequality exacerbates social problems such as violent crime and poor mental health among the populace. Elites, driven by competition for status, power, and high-status positions, engage in increasingly extreme measures like coups, civil wars, or rebellions to gain advantage, a phenomenon known as elite factionalism.

To placate the disenfranchised populace and mitigate elite strife, states often embark on expensive conquests or intensify production, leading to environmental degradation and increased vulnerability to natural disasters like drought. As societies become more top-heavy with powerful elites, decision-making becomes less effective, especially when it involves changing the status quo that benefits these elites.

The consequences of this extractive dynamic include growing instability, internal conflict, resource drain from the government, state capture by private elites, and poor decision-making. This leads to a more fragile society, with private elites taking an increasingly larger share of extractive benefits. Eventually, a shock (such as climate change, disease, or invasion) can cause the state to collapse.

The author refers to this pattern as "diminishing returns on extraction." As states seek to extract more resources through increased bureaucracy, intensified production, and conquests, these methods become less effective over time due to mounting costs and negative consequences. Similarly, elites' attempts to extract wealth through tax evasion, corruption, and private armies yield diminishing returns as they impoverish the populace and deplete their support systems. This cycle of extraction and its decreasing effectiveness contributes to societal fragility and the recurring pattern of fission (splitting) or fusion (merging) among early Goliaths or larger empires.

The text discusses the concept of "Diminishing Returns on Extraction" (DROE) as a primary factor contributing to the fall of ancient civilizations, using Rome as a prime example. DROE refers to a situation where growing inequality, oligarchy, corruption, and elite factionalism over lootable resources eventually lead to societal fragility.

Roman history exemplifies this process: Initially a republic with more balanced power, Rome evolved into an unequal autocracy. This transition saw the rise of disenfranchised citizens and ambitious elites, leading to corruption, as seen in Roman governors exploiting their subjects. Additionally, there were declining yields from mining (silver from Spanish mines) and agriculture (due to deforestation and eroding soils).

These issues compounded, creating a predicament where the populace was impoverished and hostile, the military was privatized, the government accumulated massive debt, internal governance was corrupted by elite capture of high offices, and both military and bureaucracy were bloated. This made Rome vulnerable to external threats like invasions, internal strife (civil wars), droughts, and diseases.

Historians generally agree on the factors leading to Rome's fall - corruption, institutional decay, invasions, climate change, plague, and power struggles over resources and status positions. However, debate persists regarding which were most influential. Walter Scheidel, a renowned historian of empires, has labeled the collapse of both the Han and Western Roman Empires as cases of DROE.

The text also highlights similarities across other collapses, such as those in the Mediterranean, Yucatán, China, and among early civilizations like Teotihuacan. These share common patterns: growing inequality, oligarchy, corruption, elite factionalism over surplus resources, diminishing returns on extraction, followed by shocks (like droughts, diseases, or invasions) leading to societal disintegration.

The "Primal Curse" - a term used to describe the common vulnerabilities of early civilizations – shares elements with DROE. Both involve increased inequality and extractive institutions making societies more fragile. However, the Primal Curse also includes weak control mechanisms against counter-dominance intuitions, which were less critical in large empires like Rome due to their stronger control mechanisms.

The text concludes by noting that despite these common factors, some civilizations managed to resist collapse for extended periods. The Eastern Roman Empire (Byzantine) is cited as an exception, surviving over a thousand years against significant challenges like plague and the Late Antique Little Ice Age, Avar and Slav invasions, and wars with the Sasanian Empire. This resilience suggests that understanding these dynamics can help modern societies avoid similar pitfalls.


The resilience and longevity of the Byzantine Empire can be attributed to several key factors:

1. Shared Religion: The Orthodox Christian faith played a significant role in unifying the elites and broader populace during times of crisis, fostering a sense of shared identity and purpose against the religious threat of Islam. This communal bond strengthened the empire's internal cohesion and resistance to external pressures.

2. Provincial Elites' Dependence: By integrating civil administration with military, the Byzantine Empire made local elites dependent on the imperial system. These elites were granted military titles and responsibilities, creating a symbiotic relationship that discouraged open defiance or competition against the central government.

3. Decentralization: The empire demonstrated flexibility in response to its declining resources by decentralizing power. This shift involved granting land to soldiers instead of paying them, which not only reduced financial strain but also fostered a sense of ownership and commitment among the troops. The simplified bureaucracy and local defense of lands further motivated fighters to protect their homes and families, strengthening loyalty towards the empire.

4. Favorable Geography and Climate: Although geographical advantages are often considered a matter of luck, they significantly contributed to Byzantine survival. The Taurus mountain range acted as a natural barrier, slowing down Arab expansion and providing a defensive advantage for the empire. Additionally, cooler, wetter conditions favorable to agriculture improved food production, supporting a healthier population and reducing vulnerability to famine.

The decline of states like Rome often had unforeseen positive consequences for public health and wealth distribution:

1. Shrinking Houses and Fractured Trade: As empires fell, so did state-protected trade and luxury goods, leading to more localized economies. In Roman Britain, house sizes decreased after the withdrawal of imperial support. Cities dependent on subsidized grain or other forms of imperial aid could shrink dramatically when those supports were cut off, as seen in Rome's population drop following the end of the cura annonae (grain supply).

2. Wealth Equality and Improved Health: Paradoxically, collapse sometimes results in greater wealth equality and improved health outcomes for populations. The fall of Rome, for example, led to a decrease in dental caries and bone lesions, suggesting better overall nutrition and hygiene. In the broader context of human history, periods of imperial decline have often been associated with increased stature and improved health among populations shifting away from urban grain-based diets towards more diverse food sources rich in animal protein.

These insights suggest that crises can sometimes catalyze positive change for the wellbeing of a population, despite the economic hardships they bring about.

The text discusses two main themes: Petrarch's misconceptions about the post-Roman era and the oversimplification of historical progress driven by technological advancement.

1. **Petrarch's Mistakes**: Petrarch, a 14th-century Italian scholar, coined the term "dark age" to describe the period from 500 to 1300 AD in Europe. He was deeply admiring of Classical Greece and Rome and lamented what he perceived as the cultural decline following Rome's fall. However, the text argues that Petrarch made two key mistakes:

   - **Forgetting Technology**: The term "dark age" implies a period of intellectual and technological stagnation. Yet, this era was not devoid of advancements; it saw innovations like mechanical clocks, improved mirrors, tidal mills, and the heavy plough, which significantly boosted agricultural productivity in Medieval England.

   - **Misinterpretation of Collapse**: Petrarch viewed Rome's fall as an unmitigated catastrophe leading to barbarism. The text contends that this 'rise and fall' narrative is incorrect. Instead, societies often experience periods of decline followed by rebirth or transformation rather than a straightforward progress-collapse cycle.

2. **Forgetting Technology in Historical Narratives**:

   - **Progress Narrative**: Traditional histories portray civilizational development as a continuous upward arc, with periodic collapses into 'dark ages'. This narrative assumes that societies naturally evolve towards greater technological sophistication and cultural achievement.

   - **Technological Determinism**: Many believe progress is primarily driven by technological innovation. This perspective posits that technological advancements are the engine of improvement, pushing against societal decay. However, the text challenges this view:

     - **Roman Technological Limitations**: Contrary to popular belief, the Roman Empire did not lead significant technological breakthroughs. Many key productive technologies like glassblowing and watermills were actually developed in the Hellenistic east before Rome's rise. The empire mainly disseminated and modified these existing innovations.

     - **Medieval Innovation**: Following Rome's fall, Medieval Europe was more innovative than often assumed. This period witnessed significant advancements like improved windmills, watermills, and the heavy plough, which greatly enhanced agricultural productivity.

   The text concludes by suggesting that our understanding of historical progress needs to move beyond simplistic narratives of rise and fall or linear technological advancement. It encourages recognizing the complexities of societal evolution, including periods of decline that can spark new innovations and transformations.

The Byzantines' Greek Fire recipe was known only by a few imperial agents; its production ceased when oil supplies were cut off, leading to the technology's disappearance.

- **Loss of Utility**: Technologies become obsolete when they no longer serve a purpose. For instance, Roman plumbing became less relevant in rural areas where simpler cesspit systems sufficed.

This historical perspective challenges the common assumption that technological advancement automatically leads to improved quality of life for the majority. Instead, it suggests that societal structures, access to resources, and cultural practices play equally important roles in shaping human well-being.

This suggests that factors beyond technological progress, like economic and social conditions, significantly influence human health and growth.

This implies that progress, as traditionally measured by economic growth and technological advancement, doesn't necessarily correlate with increased well-being or happiness.

3. **Elites' Control Over Surplus**: The author argues that most of the benefits from technological advancements and economic growth in pre-modern societies were captured by elites (rulers, clergy, merchants). This was often achieved through coercion or unequal systems of land ownership. As a result, despite centuries of technological progress, the majority of people saw little improvement in their lives or health (as reflected in their heights).

4. **The Black Death's Impact**: The Black Death, which struck Europe in the mid-14th century, provides a stark example of this phenomenon. Despite its horrific toll—killing up to half of Europe's population—it ultimately led to better conditions for survivors. A reduced labor force gave peasants more bargaining power, leading to higher wages, improved nutrition, and increased heights.

   - **Inclusive Institutions**: Areas with stronger social networks (like guilds, village communities) and institutions that protected the common good benefited most from this labor shortage. These societies were able to redistribute wealth more effectively, preventing elites from consolidating their power further.

   - **Unequal Impact**: However, not all regions experienced these positive outcomes equally. Places lacking such inclusive structures (like parts of Eastern Europe and Russia) saw less redistribution and remained more unequal post-plague.

5. **Lessons for Colonial Expansion**: These historical examples—the fall of Rome and the Black Death—offer insights into how societal collapses or shifts can sometimes lead to improvements in living standards for ordinary people when they result in redistributions of power. This understanding, the author suggests, might help explain how less-developed European kingdoms were able to establish colonies and dominate other societies later on.

In summary, this text argues against a simplistic view of progress, where technological advancement inevitably leads to improved living standards for all. Instead, it posits that societal collapses or dramatic shifts—by disrupting existing power structures and redistributing resources—can sometimes lead to significant improvements in the lives of ordinary people. However, these benefits are not guaranteed and often depend on the presence of inclusive institutions capable of effecting fairer distributions of wealth and power.
