It challenges the idea that states would swiftly reactivate crucial systems after a disaster, pointing to the slow rollout of COVID-19 vaccines despite their clear economic benefits as evidence to the contrary. 

The author also presents statistics on the likelihood of certain global catastrophes: 

   - **Volcanic Eruptions**: The chance of a high-impact volcanic eruption in a century is estimated at one in five.
   - **Space Weather Events**: Extreme space weather events, such as solar flares causing geomagnetic storms, have a probability of up to 20.3% per decade for an event as bad as or worse than the 1859 Carrington Event. 
   - **Nuclear War**: While exact probabilities are unknown, estimates range from 0.001% to 7% annually for inadvertent nuclear war between major powers like the US and Russia.

In conclusion, while modern states offer many benefits in terms of public goods and social welfare, their complexity makes them vulnerable to catastrophic failure. Similarly, our reliance on global systems for survival introduces new risks that should not be underestimated.


The text discusses several interconnected global risks, focusing on energy depletion and its implications for industrial civilization, as well as other existential threats like nuclear war, pandemics, and technological catastrophes.

1. **Nuclear War & Existential Risks**: The likelihood of a major nuclear event (either between nations or initiated by non-state actors) is estimated at around 0.1 to 1% annually. Following Russia's invasion of Ukraine, superforecasters revised this probability upwards to 2%. Pandemics are also seen as a significant risk, with one occurring roughly every 129 years on average, giving a 38% chance within an average lifetime. This risk might double due to climate change and environmental destruction pushing animals out of their habitats, increasing the chances of zoonotic infections.

2. **Speculative Threats**: The text also mentions less-quantifiable risks such as the creation of a powerful Artificial General Intelligence (AGI) or an engineered virus, which could have catastrophic consequences. 

3. **Energy Depletion & Fossil Fuels**: The fossil fuel industry is increasingly relying on complex and costly methods like fracking and tar sands extraction due to the depletion of easier-to-access reserves. This results in a lower energy return on investment (EROI), or energy ratio, meaning it takes more energy to extract the same amount of fossil fuels. For example, the U.S. energy ratio for oil and gas dropped from 1200:1 to 5:1 between 1901 and 2007, a 240-fold decrease. Globally, depletion is outpacing technological efforts to increase this ratio.

4. **Energy Crisis & Industrialization**: The falling energy ratio of fossil fuels poses a significant challenge for maintaining current levels of industrial activity and affluence. If the ratio falls below a critical threshold (dubbed 'energy cliff' or 'energy cannibalism'), it might become impossible to sustain our modern civilization as we know it. This is because, historically, industrial progress has been heavily dependent on low-cost fossil fuels.

5. **The Rungless Ladder Analogy**: The text uses an analogy of a ladder where each step represents a rung in human civilization's advancement. As we climb, the rungs below us disappear, making any fall more severe. Today's high level of technological and environmental complexity means that potential collapses could be rapid and widespread, unlike historical collapses which occurred gradually over many generations.

6. **Systemic Vulnerabilities & Interconnected Risks**: Modern global systems are interconnected and complex, leading to new vulnerabilities where big shocks can easily spread across borders and sectors. Efforts to mitigate one risk might inadvertently introduce others, increasing systemic risk. 

In summary, the text highlights multiple existential risks—from nuclear war and pandemics to energy depletion and technological catastrophes—and emphasizes how these risks are interconnected and amplify each other due to our complex, globalized society. The declining energy return on investment from fossil fuels presents a significant challenge to maintaining current levels of industrial activity and affluence, potentially making it difficult for civilization to re-industrialize in the face of collapse.

The production and maintenance are carried out primarily by a small group of twenty-four companies, which receive funding from around 287 financial institutions, including banks, pension funds, and insurance companies.

4. Artificial Intelligence (AI): The development of large text models, representing the cutting edge of AI, is dominated by three key companies: Microsoft's OpenAI, Google DeepMind, and Anthropic. All these companies are based in the US, falling under its jurisdiction, making it easier to regulate or oversee their activities. In 2020, around seventy projects were explicitly aiming at developing Artificial General Intelligence (AGI), with half of them based in the US and eight having military connections.

The common thread among these examples is that a small number of powerful actors create and control world-damaging technologies, often keeping their activities shrouded in secrecy or misinformation. This lack of transparency makes it challenging for democratic systems to effectively regulate these threats. The text suggests that these entities prioritize their interests over public welfare, perpetuating harm despite knowing the potential consequences.


The text discusses two significant issues related to secrecy, corruption, and their impact on public policy, particularly in the contexts of nuclear weapons, climate change, and biotechnology. 

1. **Secrecy in Nuclear Weapons Development and Proliferation:** The text highlights how secrecy has been integral to nuclear weapons development, with examples like the Manhattan Project and Castle Bravo. Despite this, secrecy hasn't prevented proliferation; eight countries developed nuclear weapons after the U.S., and more could have but chose not to. Secrecy has instead hindered public discourse on nuclear weapons, creating a global situation where multiple nations possess these weapons, yet discussions remain opaque. 

2. **Revolving Doors in Politics and Industry:** This issue is illustrated through numerous examples across different sectors and countries (US, Israel, EU, Australia). It involves individuals moving between high-level political positions and lucrative roles within industries they once regulated. This practice, often referred to as 'legalized bribery', can lead to conflicts of interest and undermine democratic processes.

He advocated for increased spending on U.S. nuclear modernization while in office, then joined a law firm working for defense contractor Northrop Grumman post-retirement. Northrop Grumman, in turn, spent heavily on political donations and lobbying.
   
   - **Fossil Fuel Industry:** Similar patterns are observed in the EU and Australia. For instance, six major fossil fuel companies and their lobby groups were involved in 71 'revolving door' cases between 2015-21, holding numerous meetings with regulatory bodies and spending significant sums on lobbying.

3. **Impact of Secrecy and Corruption:** These practices distort public policy, leading to outcomes that may not align with public interest or scientific consensus. For example, Australia, despite its renewable energy potential, lacks a carbon emissions price due partly to industry lobbying. The overall message of the text is one of skepticism towards the current trajectory and governance of AI development, particularly questioning the motives and practices of key industry players while advocating for more open dialogue and robust regulatory frameworks to manage the risks associated with advanced AI.


The text discusses the use of game theory as an explanation for various societal issues, including arms races, environmental destruction, and status-driven consumption, often referred to as "races to the bottom." 

1. **Arms Races**: Game theory posits that in a world without a unifying authority (international anarchy), states acting rationally will arm themselves because they can't trust others not to do so. This results in a security dilemma where both parties stockpiling weapons leads to collective harm, despite being individually beneficial.

2. **Environmental Destruction (Tragedy of the Commons)**: Similarly, game theory explains environmental degradation through a thought experiment where self-interested individuals overuse shared resources because individual restraint leads to competitive disadvantage. This results in collective harm, as each actor pursues short-term gains without considering long-term consequences.

3. **Status Races**: A third type of race, status races, is also explored. These are competitions for prestige or superiority (like billionaires competing through high-emission lifestyles), which often drive conspicuous consumption and expansion, contributing to environmental issues.

Criticisms of this game theory perspective include:

1. **Human Irrationality**: Real-world evidence contradicts the assumption that humans are rational and self-interested as game theory suggests. Studies show people often exhibit reciprocity, concern for self-image, aversion to inequity, and don't always act in ways predicted by game theory models.

2. **Tragedy of the Commons Misapplication**: The tragedy of the commons doesn't universally occur in reality. For example, communities (including non-state groups) often solve this problem without outside intervention, as demonstrated by economists Elinor and Vincent Ostrom's research on successful community management of shared resources.

3. **Historical Evidence**: The assumption that all humans and human groups are prone to security dilemmas and arms races is challenged by our Palaeolithic past, where peaceful fluid civilizations existed in a state of anarchy without organized warfare. Additionally, various "peace systems" throughout history have managed conflict and resource management through norms and culture, not just governmental structures.

In conclusion, while game theory provides a mathematically elegant explanation for these societal phenomena, it doesn't fully align with empirical evidence about human behavior and historical precedents. It's crucial to consider alternative explanations and the role of cultural norms, governance structures, and collective action in shaping our responses to issues like arms races and environmental degradation.


The text discusses how certain entities like states, corporations, and potentially algorithms behave differently from individual humans due to their nature as "dominance hierarchies" or artificial agents. 

1. **Artificial Agents**: States, corporations, and increasingly algorithms are not human beings but artificial constructs imbued with decision-making power, longevity beyond creators, and the capacity to act in self-interest. They are analogized as 'artificial men' (states) or 'bodies' (corporations), lacking human attributes like feelings or conscience.

2. **Dark Triad Traits**: These entities exhibit traits associated with the Dark Triad: psychopathy, narcissism, and Machiavellianism. They are self-obsessed, self-interested, suspicious, posturing, competitive, and amoral in their pursuit of power or profit. 

3. **Competition vs Cooperation**: While often portrayed as engaged in zero-sum games of competition (like arms races), these entities also collaborate when it serves their interests. For example, defense companies trade and cooperate despite being competitors, and fossil fuel companies lobby together against regulatory threats. 

4. **Historical Precedent**: This dynamic is not new; historical empires often competed but also cooperated when it benefited them. The term 'racket' is used to describe this phenomenon, where entities compete for gain (like controlling territory or market share) yet collaborate to maintain their power and extract resources from others (like taxing peasants or suppressing science).

5. **Emergence of Goliaths**: Over time, as human societies evolved under Holocene conditions (characterized by exploitable resources), groups that excelled at conquest and status-seeking grew stronger. This is a form of multi-level selection where both individual 'strongmen' and entire societies prone to aggressive competition thrive.

6. **Modern Relevance**: The text suggests that this historical pattern continues in the modern world, with states and corporations (and potentially algorithms) driving many global issues, including arms races, environmental crises, and technological risks, often while cooperating to maximize their benefits. 

The reason some groups—like nation-states and large corporations—are more prone to this dynamic than others (such as individuals or small communities) is attributed to their unique structure and incentives. As artificial entities designed for competition, power accumulation, and self-interest, they are predisposed to engage in behaviors that resemble the Dark Triad traits, leading to both intense rivalries and unexpected collaborations.


The text discusses the concept of "Goliath" systems, referring to dominant entities or structures that drive growth, conquest, and exploitation. These Goliaths have historically been associated with ideologies that justify universal expansion, such as European colonizers' civilizing mission and Islamic caliphates' jihad. Over time, these systems have evolved into competitive 'Goliath traps,' including arms races, races to the bottom, and status races driven by conspicuous consumption.

The text discusses the concept of "Evolutionary Suicide," a theory suggesting that the global system of extraction and exploitation, which has historically driven the growth and power of empires (referred to as 'Goliaths'), now poses an existential risk to the entire planet. The text uses examples like cancer and laboratory experiments with bacteria changing their environment into an inhospitable state, ultimately causing their own demise. In this context, the author suggests that the same dynamic is at play globally: Goliaths have evolved to become more effective at extraction, but these very adaptations now threaten planetary survival.

It points out that this approach relies heavily on continuous innovation and new ideas to address emerging issues before they become irreversible. However, research suggests that innovation is slowing down globally, with decreasing numbers of patents per inventor and declining research productivity across most sectors.

5. **Recombinant Growth Limitations**: The text argues against the economic faith in 'recombinant growth,' where new inventions in one area spark more innovation elsewhere. It suggests that this model may not hold true due to increasing costs of research, necessity for larger teams and resources, and potential depletion of 'low-hanging fruit' ideas.

In conclusion, the author argues that the global system's historical drivers of growth - population increase, military strength, economic exploitation - are now causing existential risks to the planet. The text questions the assumption that technological progress will continue indefinitely to solve these problems and highlights evidence suggesting a slowdown in innovation. This leads to concerns about our ability to escape the 'Goliath traps' (status races, arms races, and races to the bottom) without a global collapse.

"Killing Goliath" is a concept introduced by historian and sociologist Yasha Mounk, which refers to the struggle of individual humans against overwhelming institutional or technological forces that threaten our freedom, democracy, equality, and human connection. The term "Goliath" symbolizes these dominant entities, which have evolved throughout history in different forms:

1. **Main form of caging (Palisades - Walls):** In ancient times, the primary method of control was physical barriers like palisades or city walls to physically restrict movement and access. As societies advanced, this evolved into more abstract forms of confinement such as laws, regulations, and social norms that limit individual freedom.

2. **Main monopolizable weapons (Stone - Nuclear Weapons):** Throughout history, the balance of power has shifted with technological advancements in weaponry. From stone tools to obsidian, bronze, iron, gunpowder, and finally nuclear weapons, these advancements have often led to empires rising and falling due to their military might.

3. **Curse (Primal - Global):** Each era's "curse" refers to the negative consequences or unintended outcomes of these powerful entities. For instance, in primitive societies, it could be famines or epidemics; in colonial times, it was the exploitation and genocide caused by imperial expansion; today, it's global challenges like climate change, technological surveillance, and nuclear proliferation.

Mounk argues that while technology can amplify our capabilities and solve some problems, it also introduces new risks and threats to our values and way of life. The "Goliath" metaphor encapsulates these institutional or technological challenges that we face in different historical contexts:

   - **Early Goliaths** (Stone, Obsidian, Copper) represented the power of early states, religions, and armies.
   - **Aquatic Resources & Grain (Agricultural Revolution)** marked the beginning of settled societies, agriculture, and complex institutions.
   - **Colonial Empires (Grain, Commodities, Walls, Gunpowder)** symbolize the era of imperialism, marked by global trade, exploitation, and the rise of nation-states.
   - **Global Goliath (Fossil Fuels, Grain, Commodities, Passports, Identity Cards, Surveillance, Nuclear Weapons)** refers to our contemporary world, characterized by global corporations, international institutions, mass surveillance, and nuclear threats.
   - **Silicon Goliath (Data, Fossil Fuels, Grain, Commodities, Advanced Mass Surveillance, Nuclear Weapons)** represents the current technological era, with AI, big data, and digital surveillance reshaping society and posing new risks.

Mounk's main point is that technology alone cannot guarantee a desirable future; it's crucial to foster democratic institutions, human connection, and ethical values alongside technological progress. He warns against becoming too reliant on powerful entities (the "Goliaths") that could ultimately undermine our freedom, equality, and humanity. The solution lies in collective action and vigilance to ensure technology serves the greater good rather than becomes a tool for oppression or catastrophe.

The text discusses the Fermi Paradox, often mistakenly attributed to Enrico Fermi, but actually first posed by Frank Drake in 1975. The paradox questions why, given the vast number of stars and planets in the universe, we have not yet encountered signs of extraterrestrial civilizations. 

The author argues that this isn't a true paradox but rather Hart's Mystery - our apparent solitude in an immense cosmos. They emphasize the high stakes of preserving life on Earth, as we might be the universe's sole sentient beings for a long time.

The piece then shifts focus to the likelihood of human extinction. Despite humans' 200,000-year survival, extinction rates among mammals suggest our odds of being wiped out annually are about one in 14,000, or even higher if considering all hominids. However, these statistics may not apply to a species spread across the globe with technological capabilities.

The author proposes using the concept of "minimum viable population" (MVP) - the smallest number needed for a group to survive and reproduce without facing detrimental genetic effects. Historical examples, like the peopling of the Americas or Polynesia with groups as small as 100, illustrate this point.

Applying MVP to an intergalactic context, estimates suggest that between 50 and 44,000 individuals might be necessary for a self-sustaining colony, depending on factors like genetic diversity and the proximity of group members. 

Despite these seemingly low numbers compared to Earth's current population (8.2 billion), the text argues that complete human extinction appears unlikely even in drastic scenarios. For instance, a collapse leading to 99.99% population loss would still leave around 810,000 survivors with access to supplies and time to learn survival skills.

However, the author notes that future collapses might be more challenging due to a warmer climate with fewer habitable regions, potentially making post-collapse recovery more difficult than past examples like Polynesia. 

In essence, while the universe's vastness makes it seem probable there should be other intelligent life, we haven't found any concrete evidence yet. As for human extinction, despite potential risks, current data suggests it remains a low probability event due to our large population and adaptability. Yet, future environmental changes could complicate recovery efforts post-collapse.

The passage discusses the phenomenon of doomsday bunkers being built by the super-rich as a means to survive a catastrophic event, referred to as "the event." These bunkers are often luxurious, equipped with amenities like pools, wine vaults, and hydroponic farms. Some owners even hire private security forces, including ex-Navy SEALs, for protection against potential looters or mobs.

The author, Douglas Rushkoff, critiques this "bunker mentality" as a flawed strategy for several reasons. Firstly, these bunkers may not withstand a real world-ending disaster; even the Svalbard Doomsday Seed Vault, designed to withstand extreme conditions, was nearly compromised by a flood. Underground farms could be vulnerable to contamination or failure over extended periods of crisis.

Moreover, this approach undermines the historical evidence suggesting that strong social ties and community cooperation are key to surviving societal breakdowns. Communities with robust social networks tend to fare better in disasters because they can quickly reorganize, share resources, and support each other when central authorities fail. The bunker-dwellers' strategy of investing in places (bunkers) rather than people (relationships) is likely to backfire, contributing to societal collapse instead of mitigating it.

The passage also explores the potential resilience of poorer populations in a post-collapse scenario. Areas heavily reliant on global industrial infrastructure might suffer more when it collapses (e.g., loss of critical machinery or farming supplies). In contrast, places like sub-Saharan Africa, already accustomed to unreliable utilities, could potentially weather such a crisis better.

The Mycenaean kingdoms' deep collapse resulted in a more egalitarian society with city-states like Athens emerging. This shift was facilitated by several factors: the extreme poverty following the collapse, which leveled wealth disparities; Greece's geography of islands and mountains, providing escape routes from oppressive rulers; and the introduction of iron weapons, making it easier for citizens to form effective militias. These factors combined to favor inclusive, democratic societies over autocratic rule.

Instead, it advocates for investing in social relationships and community resilience as more effective strategies for navigating potential future crises. The historical precedent of post-collapse Greece demonstrates how drastic societal changes can occur, often leading to increased democracy and equality when the conditions are right.

The passage discusses a historical and evolutionary perspective on human societal development, focusing on the emergence of dominance hierarchies and the concept of "Goliath" societies. 

1. **Emergence of Dominance Hierarchies**: As human populations grew and resources became scarcer, people started to rely more heavily on lootable resources like grain and fish. With the introduction of weapons and confined landscapes, smaller groups began to assert control over these resources, leading to the formation of dominance hierarchies - a step back from the more unique human social structures.

2. **Birth of Authoritarian Leaders and the State**: The rise of inequality and hierarchy led to an increase in warfare. In response, people sought strong leaders for protection, giving birth to authoritarian impulses and the formation of states. These early rulers were often the most aggressive and overconfident individuals, leading to corruption and misrule.

3. **Goliath's Curse**: As these dominance hierarchies grew, they became increasingly extractive, concentrating wealth and power in the hands of elites. This led to a series of problems including poor decision-making, overexpansion, environmental degradation, and the immiseration of the masses. The "Primal Curse" saw people rebelling or fleeing from weak Goliaths, while larger empires experienced internal strife due to corruption and inequality.

4. **Colonial Empires**: Larger colonial empires seemed to escape some of these curses due to rapid technological and economic growth. However, they introduced a new form of extraction on a global scale, leading to the exploitation and often collapse of other societies. This created what's referred to as the "Global Goliath."

5. **The Silicon Goliath**: The passage extends this historical narrative into the modern context, suggesting that we are now entering an era of a "Silicon Goliath" characterized by powerful tech and finance sectors. The text discusses several strategies to "kill Goliath" - the entrenched, concentrated powers that drive status races, arms races, and exploitative practices, leading to existential risks. Here are detailed explanations of the proposed solutions:

1. **Democratizing Political Power:**

   - **Open Democracy:** This involves a shift from traditional representative democracy towards more direct forms of governance. Instead of electing representatives who may be influenced by wealth and power, citizens' assemblies or juries would be randomly selected to make decisions. These bodies would create national policies for governments and provide oversight of corporations.
   
   - **Technological Enhancements:** New technologies enable online platforms to aggregate public opinions on critical decisions, allowing for mass participation in the democratic process without the need for physical assemblies. This approach harnesses the wisdom of crowds, potentially leading to new, profound ways of decision-making.
   
   - **Reforming Corporations:** To prevent concentrations of unaccountable power, corporations could be transformed into large-scale worker cooperatives. These would be governed by workers alongside deliberative juries and have an overriding legal goal to provide social and environmental benefits rather than short-term returns for investors.

2. **Addressing Economic Inequality:**

   - **Progressive Taxation:** Highly progressive tax systems can significantly reduce wealth inequality. For example, the US had an income tax of over 90% on highest earners from 1944 to 1963 without causing economic collapse; instead, it contributed to a golden age for the U.S. economy.
   
   - **Wealth Tax:** A tax on wealth, such as land and stocks, can further level the playing field. Capping wealth at a certain level (e.g., $10 million) ensures that no individual accumulates excessive wealth beyond their needs. Similarly, limiting the income of top earners within companies to five times that of the lowest-paid worker helps prevent extreme disparities.
   
   - **Global Measures:** A global minimum wage (set at half the median wage in a country) and comprehensive debt jubilees can reduce international economic disparities, strategies historically proven effective.

3. **Democratic Control of Information and Military:**

   - **Breaking Up Monopolies:** Dismantling big tech and media monopolies through legislation and vigorous enforcement of existing anti-trust laws can distribute control over information more evenly. This prevents a few entities from dominating public discourse.
   
   - **Transparency:** Making power structures more transparent, including governments' operations, is crucial for maintaining accountability and preventing the abuse of power.

The author argues that while these changes might seem radical or impractical, they are necessary to escape "Goliath traps" - harmful patterns of competition driven by unchecked power - and address existential risks effectively. By implementing these strategies, society can work towards a more equitable, democratic, and sustainable future.

The text provided appears to be excerpts from a broader work that discusses various concepts related to societal structures, human behavior, evolutionary processes, and risks. Here's a detailed explanation of some key points:

1. **Goliath**: This term refers to a collection of interconnected hierarchies where certain individuals dominate others to control energy and labor. It can be observed in various forms across different societies, such as the state, patriarchy, wealth disparities, etc. The evolution of these dominance hierarchies is known as "Goliath Evolution."

2. **Goliath Fuel**: This refers to ecological and technological conditions that facilitate the establishment of a Goliath. It includes lootable resources (easily accessible, stolen, and stored), monopolizable weapons (weapons or tactics that can be concentrated for an advantage), and caged land (controlled territories).

3. **Goliath Traps**: These are destructive competitive dynamics dominance hierarchies can fall into due to short-termism by status seekers. The three main Goliath traps are:
   - Races for Status: Competitive pursuit of higher social rank, often leading to mutual exploitation and degradation.
   - Arms Races: Intensifying competition in weaponry or power, resulting in wasteful and dangerous escalations.
   - Races to the Bottom: Engaging in increasingly harmful practices to gain a competitive edge, ultimately hurting all participants.

4. **Diminishing Returns on Extraction**: As states and elites try to extract more resources, labor, and energy from their citizens or neighbors, several negative consequences ensue:
   - Increased inequality
   - Corruption
   - Oligarchy (poor decision-making due to concentration of power)
   - Overstretch (spreading resources too thinly)
   - Popular immiseration (decline in living standards for the majority)
   - Elites factionalism (increased conflict among elites)
   - Declining energy ratio (decreased overall productivity and efficiency).

5. **Goliath's Curse**: This is a theoretical framework describing the lifecycle of dominance hierarchies (Goliaths). It has two iterations:
   - The Primal Curse: Early Goliaths fall apart due to weak institutions, strong counter-dominance intuitions, and rising inequality.
   - The Second Iteration: As states grow larger and more complex, they become susceptible to a decline triggered by their own extractive nature (Diminishing Returns on Extraction).

6. **Dark Triad**: This refers to three antisocial personality traits often observed in individuals who seek power within hierarchies:
   - Psychopathy: Callousness, lack of empathy and remorse.
   - Narcissism: Inflated sense of entitlement and self-importance.
   - Machiavellianism: Manipulating others for personal gain.

7. **Darker Angels of Our Nature**: This phrase encapsulates a combination of psychological traits that can lead to the formation of Goliaths, including status competition through domination, embracing strong authority structures when threatened (authoritarian impulse), and becoming corrupted by power.

8. **Death-Star Syndrome**: Large, robust systems that appear resilient but are surprisingly fragile if struck with sufficient force in the right spot. This is a metaphor for how complex societies can collapse unexpectedly due to various internal or external shocks.

9. **Democracy**: A spectrum of political practices ranging from hierarchical (like ancient Athens for adult males) to non-hierarchical (egalitarian foragers like the Hadza). It's characterized by more or less equal and inclusive participation.

10. **Evolutionary Backsliding**: The movement from egalitarian, fluid civilizations into dominance hierarchies resembling those of gorillas and chimpanzees. This is the process through which societies can revert to more authoritarian structures.

11. **Evolutionary Suicide**: A situation where short-term evolutionary pressures lock a population onto a destructive path leading to long-term extinction.

12. **Existential Risk & Global Catastrophic Risk**: These terms refer to the likelihood of a deep, lasting global collapse or human extinction due to various catastrophic events or processes (e.g., nuclear war, pandemics, environmental disasters). The distinctions lie in the scale and duration of potential damages:
   - Existential Risk: Human extinction or a deep, lasting global collapse.
   - Global Catastrophic Risk: Unprecedented loss of more than 10% of global population and disruption of critical systems on which the world depends.
   - Global Decimation Risk: A scenario leading to a 10% loss of global population within a year.

13. **Extractive Institutions**: Great concentrations of power leading to institutions where elites extract more resources, labor, and energy through unequal exchange. This can manifest in various forms like authoritarian governments, oppressive theocracies, or bureaucracies controlled by the rich.

14. **Rungless Ladder**: A metaphor for how powerful Goliaths tend to have deeper and more widespread collapses. In modern times, this heightened severity of collapse is attributed to more potent weapons and more degraded social fabric.

They also note that some contemporary definitions (like those focusing on the loss of industrial technologies or modern amenities) are not applicable to pre-modern societies and may be overly dramatic or value-laden.

4. **Proposed Definition**: The author's proposed definition of collapse emphasizes common consequences across case studies, such as losses in hierarchy, energy capture, and population density, without relying on terms like 'complexity'. This definition recognizes the multifaceted nature of collapse, viewing it as a spectrum ranging from shallow to deep, with varying degrees of severity, rapidity, and endurance.

5. **References and Accessibility**: The author notes their intention to provide scholarly references where necessary but also acknowledges the limitations many readers face in accessing academic resources due to lack of affiliations or financial constraints. They aim for balance by citing both original research and accessible popular sources, providing page numbers when possible.

6. **Case Study**: An example is given from Timothy R. Pauketat's "Cahokia: Ancient America's Great City on the Mississippi," suggesting a potential chapter or section detailing the collapse of the Cahokia civilization as an illustrative case study in the broader narrative of societal collapse. 

In essence, this text outlines a comprehensive and critical approach to understanding societal collapse, emphasizing the importance of recognizing various forms of power dynamics within societies and providing a nuanced definition that avoids overly simplistic or value-laden terms.

The text discusses several historical and sociological themes, primarily focusing on the nature of civilizational collapse and the evolution of power structures within societies. Here are key points from each section:

1. **Remittances, Governance, or Other Factors Contributing to Post-Conflict Recovery**: The article refers to a study (Third World Quarterly 33, no. 7 (2012)) that debates the factors contributing to recovery following a civil war, such as remittances from abroad, improved governance, and other elements. It is noted that this doesn't imply an overall net benefit, as the conflict can also lead to the emergence of armed groups (like Hezbollah) that persist afterward.

2. **Roman Empire's Population Relocations**: According to Walter Scheidel in "Escape from Rome," massive population relocations occurred within the Roman Empire for political control purposes. Rebellious groups were moved to fortified areas, while loyal ones were shifted to hostile territories. The rulers of these movements are referred to as 'Inka' (or 'Inca'), meaning 'ruler' in Quechua.

3. **Historiography and Periodization**: The text critiques the prevalence of periodizations that celebrate empire and view times of decentralization as chaotic intervals. This text presents a synthesis of various scholarly works discussing the evolution of human society, particularly focusing on the transition from hunter-gatherer communities to more complex social structures, including states. 

1. **Thomas Hobbes' Leviathan**: This political philosophy treatise by Thomas Hobbes (1651) introduces the concept of a 'state of nature', where life is "solitary, poor, nasty, brutish, and short". According to Hobbes, humans enter into a social contract and form a state to protect themselves from each other.

2. **Frans De Waal's Primates and Philosophers**: In this work, primatologist Frans De Waal argues against the 'veneer theory', which suggests human morality is a thin layer over an inherently selfish nature. Instead, he posits that moral behaviors are rooted in primate social instincts and have evolved over time.

3. **Historical Evidence of Early States**: The text cites works like Upinder Singh's "Political Violence in Ancient India" (2017) and Monica L. For instance, the Mahabharata and Digha Nikaya suggest complex societies with laws and hierarchies in ancient India.

4. **Hobbes' State of Nature Reinterpreted**: Charles Edward Merriam's 'Hobbes's Doctrine of the State of Nature' (1906) offers a reinterpretation of Hobbes' concept, suggesting that the state of nature is not solely about individual self-interest but also includes social bonds and cooperation.

5. **Crowd Behavior and Panic**: Lee Clarke's 'Panic: Myth or Reality?' (2002) and John Drury et al.'s 'Representing Crowd Behaviour' (2013) challenge the notion of mass panic, arguing instead for a more nuanced understanding of collective behavior in emergencies. 

6. **Paleolithic Era and Human Evolution**: The text discusses the Paleolithic era as a time marked by harsh climates (referred to casually as the 'ice age'), which may have driven human evolution and social development. Richerson et al.'s 'Was Agriculture Impossible During the Pleistocene?' (2001) and Robert Bettinger et al.'s 'Constraints on the Development of Agriculture' (2009).

Migliano et al., 'Characterization of Hunter-Gatherer Networks' (2017)), co-residence patterns (Kim R. Hill et al., 'Co-Residence Patterns in Hunter-Gatherer Societies' (2011)), and the concept of 'fictive kinship'.

10. **Deep History of Humanity**: Cecilia Padilla-Iglesias' research indicates a deep history of interconnectivity among Central African hunter-gatherers (2022, 2024), suggesting that human societal evolution may have been more complex and interconnected than previously assumed.

The text discusses several points related to hunter-gatherer societies, their lifestyles, health, and misconceptions about violence. Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Lifestyle and Health**: Hunter-gatherers, despite living in diverse environments, share common characteristics such as ample exercise, social connections, and varied diets devoid of processed foods. This lifestyle has several health benefits. A study by J. Colette Berbesque et al. (2014) found that hunter-gatherers experience less famine compared to agriculturalists.

Their gut microbiomes are also more diverse than those of modern populations from different regions (Wastyk et al., 2021; Schnorr et al., 2014; Carter et al., 2023). This diversity is linked to better health outcomes, including mental health.

(2019) found that Agta hunter-gatherers had more leisure time when not engaged in agricultural work. The estimate of 77 hours per week for an average American parent, including work, housework, childcare, and commuting, contrasts sharply with the hunter-gatherer lifestyle.

4. **Misconceptions about Violence**: There are several misconceptions about pre-agricultural societies being inherently violent. Douglas P. Fry and Patrik Söderberg (2014) argue against this "myth" of hunter-gatherer warfare, citing ethnographic evidence showing that such societies are often peaceful. Philip Thomson and John Halstead's (2023) research also suggests a lower level of violence in pre-agricultural societies compared to modern ones.

5. **Scholarly Perspectives**: Widerquist and McCall (2018) explore how prehistoric myths influence modern political philosophy, while Haas and Piscitelli (2013) critique the use of ethnographic data to infer the prehistory of warfare. Fry and Söderberg (2014) specifically debunk myths about hunter-gatherer violence.

In essence, the text challenges common assumptions about hunter-gatherer lifestyles by presenting evidence that these societies often enjoy better health outcomes and less violence than is commonly believed. It also underscores how modern lifestyle factors, like sedentary work and processed diets, contribute to contemporary health issues.


The text discusses various perspectives on prehistoric violence and warfare, drawing from several academic sources. Here's a detailed summary and explanation:

1. **Prehistoric Violence and Warfare Estimates**: The paper begins by citing studies suggesting low levels of violence in the Paleolithic era. Thomson and Halstead's 'How Violent Was the Pre-Agricultural World?' estimates a 1-3% mortality rate from violent causes, with adjustments ranging from 3.2% for the Palaeolithic to 9.7% for the End-Palaeolithic. These figures are considered tentative due to potential undercounting (skeletal remains represent a small percentage of overall population) and overcounting/undercounting issues related to preservation biases.

2. **Critiques of Prehistoric Violence Estimates**: Several scholars have critiqued these estimates, including Steven Pinker in 'The Better Angels of Our Nature', Azar Gat in 'War in Human Civilization', and Ian Morris in 'War! What Is It Good For?'. R. Brian Ferguson, in his work 'Pinker's List: Exaggerating Prehistoric War Mortality', argues that Pinker's analysis overestimates prehistoric warfare mortality.

3. **Comparisons with Modern Violence**: Despite the sparse data and methodological issues, unadjusted Palaeolithic violence estimates often appear lower than modern rates when comparing causes of death (e.g., suicide in the Paleolithic might not leave skeletal traces). However, such comparisons are complex due to differences in causes of death and reporting methods.

4. **Chimpanzee Behavior as a Proxy for Human Violence**: Some researchers use chimpanzee behavior (e.g., lethal aggression) as an analogy for understanding human violence. Kevin D. Hunt's 'Chimpanzee: Lessons from Our Sister Species' and R. Brian Ferguson's 'Chimpanzees, War, and History' explore this idea, but it's important to note the limitations of such comparisons.

5. **Reluctance to Engage in Violence Among Humans**: Studies like Dave Grossman's 'On Killing' suggest that humans are often reluctant to engage in violence, even trained soldiers. This challenges simplistic views of our species as inherently war-like.

6. **Paleolithic Violence and Warfare**: Tibor Rutar's 'The Prehistory of Violence and War' provides a comprehensive review concluding that violence was low during the Paleolithic, suggesting warfare as we know it likely didn't exist. Violence seems to follow an inverted U-pattern, rising from hunter-gatherer societies to the medieval period and then decreasing in modern times.

7. **Mobile Forager Bands and Lethal Aggression**: Fry and Söderberg's study 'Lethal Aggression in Mobile Forager Bands' found low rates of lethal aggression among mobile foragers, suggesting that the structure of these societies might inhibit large-scale violence.

8. **Mass Emergencies and Collective Behavior**: Lastly, the paper references literature on how people behave during mass emergencies, often exhibiting cooperation rather than panic or aggression. This challenges simplistic notions of human behavior in crisis situations.

In essence, the text presents a nuanced view of prehistoric violence and warfare, highlighting methodological issues in existing estimates, critiques of prominent theories, and alternative perspectives drawn from anthropology, primatology, and sociology. It concludes that our species' propensity for violence might be less pronounced than commonly assumed, particularly during the Paleolithic era.


The text discusses several historical, anthropological, and sociological themes related to human behavior during crises, cooperation, and societal structures. Here's a summary:

1. **Myths of Mass Panic**: The idea that people panic en masse during disasters is debunked by historical evidence. For instance, the Black Death did not lead to violence against Jewish communities despite some evidence suggesting their involvement in disease transmission. Instead, historical pandemics generally brought people together rather than causing division and hatred.

2. **Cooperation and Sharing**: Early human societies, particularly hunter-gatherers, were characterized by cooperation and food sharing. This behavior was not limited to couples but extended to the entire community, promoting a sense of shared resources and collective well-being. Even in modern hunter-gatherer societies, food sharing is common, and this pattern has roots dating back 400,000 to 100,000 years.

3. **Democratic Tendencies**: Humans have a natural inclination towards democracy and egalitarianism. This is seen in ancient societies that practiced forms of direct democracy, as well as in modern hunter-gatherer groups where decisions are often made collectively. 

4. **Gender Egalitarianism**: The evolutionary origins of human cooperation and shared resources can be traced back to our ancestors' gender egalitarian tendencies. Women's active participation in hunting and resource gathering contributed to this shared approach. This is supported by archaeological findings, ethnographic studies, and genetic diversity patterns.

5. **Eye Contact and Social Structure**: The tolerance for eye contact among nonhuman primates predicts their social structure, suggesting that human societies may follow similar patterns. 

6. **Division of Labor**: While there's evidence suggesting women participated in hunting activities, it doesn't necessarily mean there was no division of labor based on gender. The extent and nature of such divisions likely varied across different cultures and historical periods.

The text emphasizes that human societies have a long history of cooperation, sharing, and democratic tendencies, contradicting the myth of mass panic during crises. It also highlights how these traits might be rooted in our evolutionary past, contributing to our unique social structures and resilience as a species.

The passage discusses various aspects of human evolution, social structures, and the concept of "fission-fusion" societies. Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Fission-Fusion Societies**: This term refers to social structures where groups split (fission) and merge (fusion) based on seasonal activities or resource availability. This pattern is observed in some contemporary hunter-gatherer societies, as well as in certain prehistoric communities. It's suggested that this mode of organization may have played a significant role in human evolution.

2. **Social Transitions and Evolution**: The authors cite several studies (Jennings, Grove et al., Foley & Gamble) that propose fission-fusion as one of the key factors driving social transitions and evolutionary changes in hominins. These transitions might include shifts from nomadic to settled lifestyles, or variations in group size and structure.

3. **Gossip and Social Bonding**: A study by Dunbar (2004) suggests that about two-thirds of human conversation is dedicated to social topics, often referred to as 'gossip'. This behavior is thought to have evolved to strengthen social bonds within groups, which is crucial in fission-fusion societies where members frequently split and reform.

4. **Evidence from Prehistoric Sites**: The text references several archaeological findings that provide evidence of early human adaptations related to fission-fusion dynamics:

   - **Ishango Human Remains (Crevecoeur et al., 2016)**: These late Stone Age African remains suggest high levels of social diversity and complexity, consistent with a fission-fusion lifestyle.
   
   - **Police-like Functions in Prehistoric Societies (Wengrow & Graeber, 2015)**: The authors describe how some prehistoric societies (Shoshone and Wik-Mungkan) organized rotating police forces to manage group dynamics during seasonal changes. This cycling of responsibilities may have served as an accountability mechanism.
   
   - **Neolithic Farming vs. Bronze Age Agricultural Revolution (Stevens & Fuller, 2012; Bower, 2012; Lang et al., 2013)**: These studies challenge the traditional narrative of a Neolithic "farming revolution". Instead, they propose that a Bronze Age agricultural advance might have been more significant. Furthermore, archaeological evidence from sites like Göbekli Tepe suggests early human societies were complex and possibly herders rather than farmers.
   
   - **Mammoth-bone Structures (Various Sources)**: These structures, found in sites across Europe and Asia, are thought to have been symbolic celebrations of hunting success or trading hubs, rather than permanent dwellings. They indicate complex social behaviors beyond simple subsistence.

5. **Moon Cycles and Gender Equality**: Some theories propose that certain cultures aligned societal shifts with lunar cycles to synchronize with menstruation, maintaining gender equality through coalitions of women (Knight's 'sex-strike' theory; Power's 'Female Cosmetic Coalitions').

6. **Critique and Controversies**: The passage also points out discrepancies and debates within the field. For instance, there's a contradiction between Mauss's work on Eskimo seasonality and Graeber & Wengrow's interpretation in "The Dawn of Everything". This highlights the ongoing nature of archaeological and anthropological research.

In essence, the passage explores how understanding fission-fusion societies can provide insights into human evolution, social complexity, and the diverse ways ancient humans organized their communities based on environmental and social factors. It also underscores the importance of ongoing research and critical interpretation in archaeology and anthropology.

This text discusses the evolution of social structures from ancient hunter-gatherer societies to early states, focusing on the role of resource abundance and lootability in shaping these societal hierarchies. 

1. **Pleistocene Era**: The Pleistocene era, or 'New Stone Age', was characterized by nomadic, egalitarian forager societies. These communities were marked by a lack of social hierarchy due to several factors: abundant exit options (the ability to move to new food sources), underdeveloped military technology (no monopolizable weapons), and no evidence of prolonged dominance hierarchies.

2. **Holocene Era Transition**: The transition into the Holocene era, around 10,000 years ago, saw a significant shift in human social structures. This change is attributed to the increased availability and reliability of certain resources, particularly those that could be stored or 'looted' – a concept referred to as Goliath fuel.

   - **Behavioral Ecology Perspective**: According to this perspective, dense, reliable resources tend to lead to more hierarchical social arrangements. This is because resources that can be accumulated and controlled provide the potential for power concentration. The availability of such resources led to the emergence of complex hunter-gatherer societies with social hierarchies, like those found among Northwest Coast Native Americans who relied heavily on salmon.

   - **Critiques**: While this behavioral ecology view is dominant, there are dissenters who argue that cultural factors play a more significant role in shaping societal hierarchies. However, these alternative perspectives often struggle to explain broader patterns of variation in hierarchical hunter-gatherer societies compared to the behavioral ecology view.

3. **Agricultural Revolution**: The adoption of farming further intensified resource concentration and, consequently, social stratification. Crops like grain became lootable resources, leading to the development of complex societies with substantial inequalities – a phenomenon James C. Scott explores in his works "The Art of Not Being Governed" and "Against the Grain."

4. **Horseback Empires**: The introduction of horses in North America by Native American societies around 5000 years ago led to a new form of social organization – 'kinetic' or 'horseback' empires, such as the Comanche. These societies used mobility and speed conferred by horses for raiding and resource control, further increasing their power.

5. **Power Framework**: The text also introduces a modified version of Mann's sources of social power framework to understand these shifts in societal structures better. 

In essence, this passage argues that changes in resource availability and lootability have been key drivers in the evolution from egalitarian hunter-gatherer societies to more complex, stratified states. It also highlights ongoing debates within anthropology about the relative importance of ecological factors versus cultural ones in shaping human social structures.


The text presents an overview of various perspectives and theories regarding the origins and nature of human violence, particularly warfare, across different cultures and historical periods. 

1. **Critiques of Carneiro's Circumscription Theory**: The article begins by acknowledging criticisms against Michael E. Mann's adaptation of the Circumscription Theory (initially proposed by Karl W. Deutsch and further developed by political scientist Robert Carneiro). Critics like Terry Stocker, Jianyi Xiao, Julia Zinkina, Andrey Korotayev, and Alexey Andreev argue that the theory fails to explain societies that were not conquest-oriented. 

2. **Mann's Revised Theory**: In response, Mann introduces a revised version of Carneiro's theory. This new model views circumscription (i.e., geographic boundaries) as just one factor contributing to state formation alongside 'lootable resources' and 'military technology.' It also incorporates the 'authoritarian impulse,' suggesting that even a single warring polity could incite neighboring communities to become more authoritarian.

3. **Evolutionary Ecology of Inequality**: Mann's theory is partly influenced by Eric Alden Smith, Jennifer E. Smith, and Brian F. Codding's work on the evolutionary ecology of inequality. They propose that human societies are drawn towards hierarchical structures due to material circumstances shaping our decisions and actions.

4. **Attractor State Concept**: Mann employs a concept from physics—'an attractor state'—to describe how initial conditions and past events can direct societies toward specific organizational forms, including hierarchical ones. 

5. **Historical and Archaeological Evidence of Violence**: The text then turns to historical and archaeological evidence for warfare. It references studies on the Oaxaca Valley (Flannery & Marcus), prehistoric Japan (Nakao et al., Nakagawa et al.), Native North-Eastern America (Dye), and Europe/Near East (Fry, Keith, Söderberg). 

6. **Theories on the Causes of Warfare**: Various theories about what drives warfare are discussed: 

   - **Raiding for Resources**: This idea posits that groups fight over valuable resources like water or food.
   
   - **Raiding for Sex**: Another prominent theory, advocated by authors like Gat and Pinker, suggests that men go to war primarily to gain access to women from rival groups.
   
   - **Social Substitutability**: This concept refers to the ability of one group member to be replaced by another from the same group in acts of violence or retaliation, potentially fueling cycles of conflict.

7. **Caveats and Limitations**: The author acknowledges that interpreting past violence from archaeological evidence is complex and subject to uncertainty due to limitations in the fossil record and potential biases in historical accounts. For example, while some societies seem prone to intergroup conflict based on first-contact observations or cave art, direct archaeological evidence can be scarce.

In essence, Mann's article provides a comprehensive review of existing theories about human warfare and their supporting evidence, demonstrating how these ideas evolve over time and across disciplines. It also illustrates the ongoing debate within scholarly communities about the nature and causes of violence in human societies.


The provided text discusses the concept of social evolutionism, a theory that applies principles of biological evolution to human societies, suggesting they progress through stages of development. Two key figures in this field were Lewis Henry Morgan and Sir John Lubbock. 

Morgan's schema classified human societies into 'lower', 'middle', and 'upper' barbarism/savagery, and 'ancient', 'medieval', and 'modern' civilization, but he did not explicitly reference evolution in his major works. Lubbock, a contemporary of Morgan, also created a similar schema but directly linked it to evolutionary theory.

The text suggests that both men's theories were flawed interpretations of evolutionary principles. In biological evolution, species best-adapted to their environment survive and thrive; however, social evolutionism ascribed purpose and direction to societal progress, which isn't a feature of biological evolution.

The text also explores the energy capture capabilities of different types of hunter-gatherer societies: egalitarian ones subsist at a basic level, while hierarchical ones generate surplus energy for elaborate feasts, luxury items, and monumental structures. 

Critiques on the "stepladder" theory—a progression from 'band' to 'tribe', 'chiefdom', and 'state'—are mentioned, highlighting its limitations. This model is criticized for oversimplifying societal development and ignoring variations among different cultures.

The concept of complexity in archaeology is primarily associated with centralized, hierarchical societies rather than egalitarian ones, despite exceptions. The definition of complexity often ties into inequality, centralization, and hierarchy. 

Lastly, the text introduces the concepts of heterarchy (non-hierarchical systems) and corporate vs despotic power in archaeology, which can be complex and confusing for a general audience. It also hints at critiques of modern democracies being more oligarchic than their ancient counterparts, as per Paul Cartledge's argument.

In summary, the text critically examines the concept of social evolutionism, questioning its validity as an application of biological evolution principles to human societies. It delves into the energy dynamics and complexities of different types of hunter-gatherer societies, while also discussing various critiques of commonly used models of societal progression.


The text discusses the shift from a hunter-gatherer lifestyle to farming (the Neolithic Revolution) and its implications on various aspects of human society. Here's a detailed summary and explanation:

1. **Domestication of Plants**: The process began around 25,000 years ago when hunter-gatherers developed new tools and inadvertently started shaping their environment, favoring fire-tolerant plants (pyrophytes and pyrophiles) while eliminating others. This led to the unintentional domestication of crops.

2. **Transition to Agriculture**: The transition to farming occurred between 10,000 and 3,000 BCE in various parts of the world, including Europe and the Near East. This shift was likely driven by a combination of factors such as population growth, technological advancements, and climate change.

3. **Genetic Impact**: The most recent evidence suggests that around 3.6% of the migrating farmers interbred with local hunter-gatherers during this period. This genetic interchange occurred along both inland and Mediterranean routes of Neolithic spread in Europe.

4. **Physical Changes**: Post-ice age farmers, on average, were shorter than their Palaeolithic hunter-gatherer ancestors. This reduction in height is attributed to the challenges of agricultural life, including more intensive labor and potentially poorer nutrition. One study found early European farmers were 3.82 cm shorter than expected based on their genetics.

5. **Health Implications**: The shift to farming brought about significant changes in health. Early Neolithic villages show evidence of substantial oral infections and enamel defects, suggesting higher rates of disease compared to foraging societies. This phenomenon has been linked to the increased proximity between humans and animals, as well as the consumption of less diverse diets.

6. **Egalitarianism vs. Material Wealth**: The lifestyle of egalitarian hunter-gatherers is characterized by a focus on friendship and leisure rather than material accumulation. They often achieve 'affluence' in the sense that their needs are met without an excessive desire for material possessions. This stark contrasts with farming societies, where surplus production becomes a driving force behind social stratification and wealth disparities.

7. **Surplus Production**: Peasant families typically do not produce surpluses once subsistence needs are met. Their labor is primarily focused on meeting immediate food requirements for themselves and their community rather than generating extra resources for trade or storage. This contrasts with the drive for surplus production in agricultural societies, which often leads to economic hierarchies and social stratification.

In conclusion, the Neolithic Revolution marked a profound shift in human history, transforming not just subsistence strategies but also physical health, population dynamics, and social structures. While farming brought about significant changes, it also introduced new challenges that shaped the course of human civilization.


This passage discusses various aspects related to the transition from foraging (hunter-gatherer) societies to farming communities, focusing on Europe during the Neolithic period. It draws upon several academic sources, including works by Brian Hayden, Stephen Shennan, and James C. Scott.

1. **Principle of Least Effort (PLOE)**: The PLOE suggests that humans tend to minimize effort in their daily activities. While this principle applies generally, it is particularly relevant when considering food acquisition for large groups. Foragers will still seek out flavorful or prestigious foods, but the PLOE dictates that, in day-to-day life, people prioritize efficient methods of feeding themselves and their communities (Trigger, 1990).

2. **Importance of Wild Resources**: Despite the rise of farming, wild resources remained crucial to early agricultural societies in East Asia and Europe (Stevens et al., 2022). This highlights that even with the advent of cultivation, foraging did not entirely disappear.

3. **Floodplain Hypothesis**: The floodplain hypothesis posits that early farming communities formed near rivers due to the predictable and abundant resources these environments provided (Scott, 2010). This strategy allowed for increased food production and population growth.

4. **Reliance on Farming and Fertility**: Modern research indicates a correlation between higher reliance on farming and greater fertility at the individual level (Page et al., 2024). However, cultural context plays a significant role in this relationship. 

5. **Feasting and Social Dynamics**: Feasts played an essential role in early agricultural societies. They facilitated social bonding, alliance-building, and the distribution of resources (Hayden, 2014). Moreover, feasts could be used as a means to display wealth and power.

6. **Neolithic Resettlement Camps**: James C. Scott's concept of "Neolithic resettlement camps" refers to the unprecedented concentrations of grain, animals, and humans that emerged with farming (Scott, 2017). These settled communities were more susceptible to disease outbreaks due to their dense populations.

7. **Disease and Population Fluctuations**: Archaeological evidence suggests that early Neolithic farmers in Europe faced repeated plague infections over several generations (Seersholm et al., 2024). Such diseases could lead to significant population declines, contributing to the instability of these early agricultural societies.

8. **Super-linear Scaling of Crime**: In urban settings, crime rates tend to increase at a faster rate than population growth (Oliveira, 2021). This phenomenon can be attributed to the complex social structures and inequalities that emerge in cities and settled communities.

9. **Population Booms and Busts**: Researchers have proposed various explanations for the fluctuating population sizes observed in mid-Holocene Europe, including disease, environmental changes, and social instability (Kondor et al., 2022). These population booms and busts reflect the challenges early farming societies faced as they adapted to their new way of life.

In summary, this passage explores how various factors influenced the development of early agricultural societies in Europe during the Neolithic period. It discusses principles like the PLOE, the significance of wild resources and feasting, and the challenges these communities faced, including disease outbreaks and population fluctuations. The text also highlights how social dynamics and urbanization played crucial roles in shaping these early societies' trajectories.

The text discusses the concept of societal collapse in ancient civilizations, focusing on three case studies: Teotihuacan (Mexico), Tiwanaku (Bolivia), and Cahokia (North America). 

1. **Teotihuacan**: This Mesoamerican city-state thrived from 100 BCE to 650 CE before its mysterious collapse around the 7th century. Scholarship suggests that climate change, particularly a prolonged drought, played a significant role in the decline. Linda R. Manzanilla's research highlights how internal tensions and elite revolt may have further exacerbated this collapse, as evidenced by signs of inequality and social unrest found at the site.

2. **Tiwanaku**: Located in present-day Bolivia, Tiwanaku was a large pre-Columbian settlement that peaked around 600 CE. It is believed to have experienced a sudden collapse around 1000 CE. John Wayne Janusek's research reveals that the initial signs of inequality emerged when some individuals tried to restrict access to a public monument and control production of valuable goods, leading to rapid abandonment as people returned to autonomous villages.

3. **Cahokia**: This Mississippian culture city was located near modern-day St. Louis, Missouri, and flourished from 1000 to 1400 CE. Its collapse around 1400 CE is attributed to a combination of factors including climate change, droughts, increased conflict over resources, and societal strain. Unlike Teotihuacan and Tiwanaku, Cahokia showed signs of urbanization and state formation. It provided relative peace (though disputed) within its borders while experiencing more conflict along the periphery—a situation reminiscent of the Roman Empire's Pax Romana.

Common themes across these collapses include climate change, internal tensions, elite dominance, resource scarcity, and societal strain. Despite differences in scale, duration, and specific causes, all three examples illustrate how complex societies can succumb to pressures that overwhelm their capacity for adaptation or governance.

The text also mentions related topics like the nature of 'statehood' in these ancient civilizations—noting that terms like "Goliath" (often used to denote states) don't fully capture the complexity and varying degrees of centralized power present in societies such as Cahokia, Huhugam, Puebloans, or Mississippi mini-kingdoms. These were indeed hierarchically unequal dominance structures maintained through force rather than full-fledged states by modern definitions.

3. **Geographical Factors**: The development of states or complex societies seems influenced by geography and population density, which can restrict movement but not entirely prevent it. For instance, river systems in Amazonia were navigable, allowing for some mobility despite the dense forest.

Title: Past Changes in Cultural Complexity, Proceedings of the National Academy of Sciences 113, 16 (2016): E2241-7

The paper discusses various aspects of cultural complexity throughout history, focusing on how societal structures and ideologies have evolved. Here's a detailed summary:

1. **Population Size and Cultural Evolution**: The authors note that some researchers suggest larger populations can hinder cultural evolution (Fay et al., 2019). However, this is based on limited evidence from a single experiment involving paper-plane construction tasks.

2. **General Trends in Cultural Complexity**: The authors emphasize that there are strong general trends in the development of cultural complexity rather than absolute rules. For instance, while many complex societies had writing systems (like the Sumerians), others, like the Inca, developed large-scale empires without it.

3. **Human Sacrifice and Social Hierarchy**: Studies indicate that ritual human sacrifice often coexisted with social stratification in ancient societies (Watts et al., 2016; Winkelman, 2014). This practice may have played a role in promoting and sustaining complex societies. Factors contributing to this include political organization through alliances and the role of ideological integration in creating lasting states and empires (Goldstone & Haldon, 2009).

4. **Ideology and State Formation**: Ideological integration has been crucial in establishing enduring states and empires across different cultures (Goldstone & Haldon, 2009; Scott, 1990). Despite not everyone believing the dominant narratives—slaves often dismissed their masters' superiority, peasants questioned lords’ authority, and citizens doubted emperors’ divine status—these ideologies still played a significant role in legitimizing inequalities.

5. **Inequality Justification**: Throughout history, various ideologies have been used to justify rising inequalities (Piketty, 2020). The transition from animistic beliefs to monotheistic religions marked a significant shift in this regard (Peoples et al., 2016; Whitehouse et al., 2023).

6. **Physical Factors and Social Inequality**: Physical differences, especially strength disparities between genders, can compound into substantial social differences over time, particularly in societies where violence plays a significant role (Miller et al., 1993; Lindisfarne & Neale, 2023).

7. **Meritocracy and Inequality**: Recent research suggests that income inequality correlates with belief in meritocracy, implying a feedback loop where high levels of inequality lead to greater acceptance of its justification (Mijs, 2021).

In essence, the paper highlights how various factors—population size, human sacrifice, ideology, physical differences, and beliefs about merit—interacted over time to shape cultural complexity and social inequality across different civilizations.

The text discusses the dynamics of wealth and power inequality throughout history, focusing on how various factors influence these disparities. 

1. **Historical Inequality**: The author cites Thomas Piketty's "Capital in the Twenty-First Century" to illustrate that wealth inequality has been a persistent feature of modern societies. This is also corroborated by Branko Milanovic's research, which indicates that pre-industrial societies were highly unequal as well. The 'Matthew Principle' – those who have will receive more, and those without will lose even what they have – is used to describe this phenomenon.

2. **Impact of Shocks on Inequality**: While catastrophic events (like pandemics) might seem like potential equalizers, research by Bas van Bavel and Marten Scheffer suggests that their impact depends heavily on existing institutions. If economic and political power is already skewed, such shocks can exacerbate inequality rather than reduce it. This is because elites can leverage crises to consolidate their advantages.

3. **Inclusive vs Exclusive Institutions**: The author refers to the distinction between 'inclusive' and 'exclusive' institutions as outlined by Acemoglu and Robinson in "Why Nations Fail." These terms are typically applied to formal structures like the rule of law, but here, they're expanded to include broader power relations. Inequality in various forms of power (wealth, decision-making, control of information, violence) is posited as a key driver of extractive arrangements historically.

4. **Impact on Society and Well-being**: Richard Wilkinson and Kate Pickett's work, "The Spirit Level" and "The Inner Level," highlight how greater equality leads to better societal outcomes, including reduced stress and improved well-being. 

5. **Climate Change and Polycrisis**: Recent studies by Daniel Hoyer et al., Dániel Kondor et al., and others suggest that long-term socio-cultural factors shape societal responses to crises like climate change. These can lead to population booms or busts, as seen in mid-Holocene Europe.

6. **Variability in Crisis Patterns**: An analysis using the Crisis Database reveals no singular pattern for how crises unfold, implying diverse responses across different contexts and time periods. 

7. **Climate Change and Historical Collapse**: Timothy M. Lenton's research indicates that climate change played a significant role in historical collapses, though the exact mechanisms are complex and varied.

The text emphasizes that while certain factors (like crises or immiseration) might seem like potential equalizers of wealth and power, their impact is heavily influenced by pre-existing societal structures and relations. It underscores the importance of understanding these dynamics to predict and potentially mitigate future disparities, especially in the face of global challenges such as climate change.


The text discusses several key points related to societal resilience, particularly in the context of crises, collapses, and climate change. Here's a detailed summary and explanation of each point:

1. **Crisis and Collapse Dynamics**: The text introduces a framework by Tilman Hartley that suggests a crisis is more likely to lead to societal breakdown or collapse when it involves increased scarcity (growing vulnerabilities due to diminishing returns), more shocks, and closed institutions.

2. **Collapse Explanations**: Joseph A. Tainter's research indicates that while external factors like climate change or invasions can trigger collapses (44% of cases reviewed), growing vulnerability is just as crucial. In other words, societies become more susceptible to collapse due to internal weaknesses before a final shock occurs.

3. **Late Antique Little Ice Age**: The text references studies by U. Büntgen et al. and P. N. Peregrine that explore the impact of climate change on societal changes during the Late Antique Little Ice Age (536 to around 660 AD). These studies suggest that cooling periods led to significant social transformations, though the exact nature of these shifts is still a topic of ongoing research.

4. **Variations in Political Structures**: The text highlights that societies can have different political structures. Some may be leaderless (like early Monte Albán or Teotihuacan), while others, like the Maya, may have hierarchical leadership with significant social disparities.

5. **Social Resilience to Climate Change**: Peregrine's research suggests that societies' resilience to climate-related challenges during the Late Antique Little Ice Age varied based on their institutional inclusivity. More inclusive societies seemed better equipped to handle the adverse effects of cooling periods.

6. **Social Capital**: This concept, introduced by Robert D. Putnam, refers to the networks, norms, and trust that exist among individuals in a community. "Bridging capital," as Putnam calls it, is inclusive institutions that connect diverse groups, fostering cooperation and resilience.

7. **Disaster Resilience**: The text discusses factors contributing to disaster resilience, including social capital and inclusive institutions. Research by Yoon et al., Hore et al., Kasdan, and Kelman et al. suggests that communities with strong social networks, diverse problem solvers, and participatory decision-making processes tend to be more resilient in the face of disasters.

8. **Political Inclusion and Economic Growth**: Acemoglu and Robinson's work indicates that political inclusivity can drive economic growth. A recent meta-analysis supports this idea, though it notes that the democracy-growth link varies across different contexts.

9. **Collective Intelligence and Crowds**: The text references research showing that diverse groups of problem solvers can outperform high-ability individuals (Hong & Page) and how collective intelligence can be used to mitigate global risks (Yang & Sandberg). It also mentions the concept of prediction markets, which leverage collective wisdom for better forecasting.

10. **Superforecasters and Deliberative Democracy**: Superforecasters, as described by Tetlock and Gardner, are individuals who consistently make accurate predictions across a wide range of questions. The text also references the potential benefits of deliberative democracy in decision-making processes, as discussed by Dryzek et al., Landemore, and others.

11. **Post-Conflict Reconciliation**: The final point mentions research on post-conflict reconciliation (Ugarriza & Caluwaerts), highlighting the importance of inclusive political processes in healing societal rifts following conflicts.

Overall, the text underscores the significance of social resilience, inclusivity, and diverse problem-solving networks in helping societies navigate crises, collapses, and climate change. It also emphasizes the value of participatory institutions and collective intelligence in fostering adaptability and robustness against adverse events.

4. **Prehistoric Warfare**: Azar Gat's "The Prehistory of War and Peace in Europe and the Near East" suggests that warfare has been a consistent feature of human history, with its roots extending back to prehistoric times (Ferguson).
